The RED PAGE
A Tuneful Quire
Gosse
Edmund Gosse, with six volumes of verses
to his name, proclaims himself a tired man
in his seventh. The Autumn Garden
(Hemeinann) contains slight verses
chiefly about flowers, with here
and there a passing phase of philosophy.
He writes with all the minor’s accuracy,
saying with precision little things that need
not be said. He writes verse simply because
it is the custom of English critics to write
occasional verse. It is a genteel occupation,
not to be taken too seriously. Yet here is
a pleasing impression of “ A London Fog ” :
In blue-gray fog, as in a sea, we drown ;
The unseen rain soaks down;
Like broken phantom pillars, from each roof
The chimneys soar aloof.
The sky, lost, like some ocean from below.
Melts in one general flow ;
Vague, dull, immense, splashed with the light of
tears,
The long dim pavement sheers.
And now, and now, across its sullied glass,
The blotted figures pass ;
Hope, poverty, ambition, lust, and pain
Glide, muffled, through the rain.
And she whom most we love, or that fell head
Our thoughts hate most, and dread,
Might cleave the blueness at our cheek, nor make
One sentry-nerve awake.
And his “Epilogue” is interesting less for
its poetry than for its fine personal outlook.
Grosse is a critic who does not think all the
poets are dead. He admits that probably
he, in his old age, will fail to understand
the newer poets, but still he will preserve
his faith in beauty.
Before my tale of days is told,
O may I watch, on reverent knees,
The unknown Beauty once unfold
The magic of her mysteries !
Before I die, O may I see.
Clasped in her violet girdle, Spring ;
May April breezes blow to me
Songs that the youngest poets sing ! ..,
New arts, new raptures, new desires
Will stir the new-born souls 6f men;
New fingers smite new-fashioned lyres—
And O ! may Ibe listening then. .. .
Change is the pulse of life on earth,
The artist dies, but Art lives on ;
New rhapsodies are ripe for birth
When every rhapsodist seems gone. ...
Nay, humbly, shrinkingly, in dread
Of fires too splendid to be borne—
In expectation lest my head
Be from its Orphic shoulders torn—
I wait till, down the eastern sky,
Muses, like Msenads in a throng,
Sweep my decayed traditions by,
In startling tones of unknown song.
So, to my days’ extremity,
May I, in patience infinite,
Attend the beauty that must be,
And, though it slay me, welcome it.
Benson
The Poems of A. G. Benson { John Lane)
consists of a selection from six previous
volumes, and betrays the same stodginess as
his quick-coming prose. Many of
his lyrics also are addressed to
flowers ; while others favor the toad, mole,
newt and similar fauna in a reverent Words-
worthian manner reminiscent of the “Lines
written with a Slate Pencil on a Stone, the
largest of the heap, after finding a Young
Tadpole while Walking with a friend on a
Wet Day.” In his “ Prelude,” Benson says
that at the temple gate :
Victim or minister
I dare not claim to be,
But in the concourse and the stir,
There shall be room for me.
Apparently there is room in England for one
who writes deftly on familiar themes, who
utters platitudes with portentous solemnity,
and whose polysyllabic murmur is full of
echoes : “the immemorial sea,” “the multi-
tudinous roar,” “reverberant wings,” and
“ shut of evening,” “ nipping, eager
weather,” etc. In the sonnet’s scanty plot
Benson’s culture produces fair results ; but
the only lyric with any force in it is “ Stand
Aside,” which is a variation of Browning's
“Lost Leader,” and is as mild as cold tea
compared with Swinburne’s “ Clear the
Way.”, He modestly admits that he cannot
sihg as sings the nightingale, thrush or owl :
But I can sing as sings the prudent bee,
As hour by patient hour he goes and conies,
Bearing the golden dust from tree to tree,
Labors in hope, and as be labors, hums.
Humming may be filled with sweet airs in
bee-land, but in mortal verse it is apt to be
monotonous.
Griffyth
Fairfax
J. Griffyth Fairfax proved in The Gates of
Sleep that he possessed a respectable lyrical
faculty. His volume of Poems (Smith, Elder
and Co.) contains work much in
advance of the earlier booklet.
He writes with ease and grace,
and has little to learn on the technical side.
Unfortunately he has not anything new to
say, and does not say the old things freshly.
Even if the quotations which adorn many of
the poems did not suggest it, the poems
themselves show that they are begotten of
books. Familiar fauns and satyrs, naiads
and dryads, Maenads and Bassarids flit
through his pages, and there is hardly an
original image, phrase, or mode of expres-
sion. Milton may not have any monopoly
of “the opening eyelids of the morn,”
but Fairfax, who also uses “the opening
eyelids,” gives us in addition “the eyelids of
the sky ” and “eyelids of the day.” “ The
Wind with amorous fingers ” turns over a
few pages to “ the slow dawn, whose pallid
fingers ” in their turn point to “ the gray
pale fingers of the wistful day.” “ Ariadne
in Naxos ” is a pleasant piece of blank verse,
and there are other things well done : but
somehow the silhouette frontispiece, by
Baron Cedercreutz, of windmills and their
reflection in the stream is symbolical of the
whole of Fairfax’s verse.
Arnold
Wall
Arnold Wall, a professor in Maoriland,
has a distinctive note although it is a low
one. His New Poems (Walter Scott Co.)
contain some characteristic blank
verse, but is more varied in style
than his first volume. The pro-
fessor’s place in the quire is that of “an
unobtrusive floating undertone ” (to quote
one of his own lines). It is intellectual rather
than emotional verse, quiet and precise in
style, but occasionally it bubbles with a little
playful humor. An experiment in “ Head-
Rimes ” is interesting :
Here in our shot-silk harbor rimmed with hills
Dear to the souls of men who marry yachts.
Bask little bays where truant breezes come,
Asking no leave of him who rules the winds ;
They quit th’ advancing column, brisk deserters.
Lay themselves down, sleep lightly, rise and
stretch,
Curl round the sides of the bay, look out to sea,
Whirl one another round in curious dances.
So with your beautiful face, my daily study—
No words can paint it, handled honestly.
Some of the poems are reprinted from The
Bulletin and The Lone Hand.
Psychic Fiction
PSYCHIC phenomena are fashion-
able in novels now-a-days. It does
not require a great deal of study
to get up a certain amount of “local
color,” but not many of the novelists have
visited the region itself, and they fail to
produce any really creepy weirdness.
Some of Stevenson’s short stories are the
best of the kind, and Henry James
achieves gruesome thrills in The Two
Magics. Charles Marriott’s The Happy
Medium and Other Stories {per Sydney
Book Club) includes one or two ventures
into the eerie, but most of the stories are
clever studies of domestic relationships,
with one amusing yarn in which a golli-
wog takes an active part in the punish-
ment of a foolish husband.
Algernon Blackwood’s John Silence
(Dymock’s Library), the adventures of a
psychic Sherlock Holmes, breaks new
ground and succeeds in realising an
other-worldly atmosphere. The • first
“ case ”is a particularly good one. A
writer of humorous stories resorts to a
powerful drug which lays him open to an
invasion by evil forces inhabiting his
house, stored there by a long-gone tenant
of atrocious character. The effect is that
his sense of humor is obliterated, his
rest is destroyed, and he becomes clair-
voyant. He hears sights and sees
sounds. Perhaps the invaded humorist
had read Arthur Rimbaud’s sonnet on
the colors of the vowels : anyhow, he
heard the red bindings of the books in
deep sounds, the yellow covers made
a shrill piercing note, the brown book-
case muttered, and the green curtains
kept up a rippling sound like the
lower notes of a wood-horn. But that
was the least of the troubles from which
Dr. Silence relieved him by removing him
to another house and meeting the ghostly
forces himself. The book is worth read-
ing.
Blackwood’s latest production, Jinibo:
a Fantasy (McMillan), is of a different
and more difficult order, for he attempts
in detail and on a larger scale what Kip-
ling merely suggested in “ They.” An
imaginative boy, who is full brother to
the happy muffin-seller in The Golden Age,
and first cousin to Canton’s W.V., peoples
a neighboring empty house with his
own cheerful creations. A practical
governess, acting under instructions from
a matter-of-fact father to knock the boy’s
silly notions out of his head, terrifies him
by putting an imaginary Monster into
the Empty House. Shortly afterwards
the boy is injured, and in the period of
unconsciousness his subliminal-self ex-
perienced many strange adventures in
the Empty House with its master, Bright,
and subsequently learns how to escape
back to the body by flight. It is a re-
markable fantasy, and could have been
written only by one who has keen
intuitions and intense sympathy with
children.
A Sociological Novel
A Man of Property and The Country House
gave the name of John Galsworthy a special
significance to fiction readers as that of an
artist in fiction to whom social problems had
not become the incubus of “a purpose,”
but were something more than a back-
ground. Fraternity {per Roycroft Library)
is the story of a couple of middle-
class families in London, cultured, refined
and fairly well-to-do, beginning at the time
when a group belonging to the lower-class
impinged upon them and caused grave dis-
turbance in their usually serene and scented
lives. Two brothers Dallison had married
two sisters, daughters of a famous scientist,
who was mentally deranged by an illness,
became detached from current life and
devoted himself with fanatical zeal to the
writing of a great book on Universal
Brotherhood. Throughout the story the
eccentric old idealist moves in and out,
like a Greek chorus, uttering oracu-
lar passages from his Book of Universal
Brotherhood. One of his remarks, that
“ each of us has a shadow in those streets ”
(of the slums) is the keynote of Fraternity.
Hilary Dallison, a novelist, is a sensi-
tive negative person, estranged from his wife,
who occupies herself with painting. Her
model is the principal agent in creating the
disturbances which make the story—primarily
by awakening “the social conscience” in the
artist’s husband, and finally by arousing the
latent animalism in his attenuated blood.
The other Dallison, a sardonic lawyer,
refuses to interfere with “the shadows,” in
whom liis wife is sentimentally interested —
“ It’s all very well up to a certain point, and
nobody sympathises with them more than I
do ; but after that it becomes destructive of
all comfort, and that does no good to anyone,”
Apparently Galsworthy wishes to show the
effect of the social conscience working
in the class represented by the Dallisons.
The sense of smell, according to the
author, is responsible for the im-
penetrable barrier between the classes.
The lawyer’s daughter, in whom the social
conscience had developed rapidly under the
influence of a male cousin, an uncompromis-
ing socialist and sanitationist, finds the
effect of the slums on her senses too much
for her philanthropy, and retreats in dismay.
The novel writer, just at the point where he
was becoming human in his relations with
the model, is driven back by his offended
nose.
The narrative is without a hero or heroine,
and without any definite plot. There is
much gentle irony in it, sometimes wrapped
in quaint symbols, such as “the perfect
dog,” made of sterilised wool, which was
discovered by Hilary’s lady-poodle. It is
written with great charm, but as a novel it is
vague and inconclusive. While the. theorist
on Universal Brotherhood is in the clouds,
the practical sanitationist becomes so
inhuman that he cannot kiss a pretty girl
who loves him —because she funked at
slumming. The author’s sympathy with
“ the shadows ” is evident, but the pictures
of the two classes have no coherency except
in so far as they show the essential fraternity
of both—which is a truism hardly requiring
elaborate statement.
A German-Silvery Discourse
The dialogue form looks easy, but is
extremely difficult; and Homer, Plato
and the rest of the ancients, being dead,
cannot protest if their shades are used by
a modern for the purpose of “ throwing
off” at his enemies or mankind in general.
Landor employed them and many living
persons in his incomparable Imaginary
Conversations, where they interpret them-
selves afresh and incidentally ventilate
Landor’s own views on various questions.
The dry radiance of the Conversations
comes from a more truly Greek spirit and a
greater mastery of English prose than is
to be found anywhere else in his time,
and he lived to be over eighty. Landor
has been unduly neglected, but it is
worthy of note that Nietzsche, in The
Gay Science, regards him as the only
Englishman of last century who could be
ranked as a master of prose.
There have been many Dialogues of
the Dead since Landor’s, and perhaps
the best of them were those in J. K.
Bangs’ light and airy House Boat on the
Styx. More serious efforts, such as the
late Marion Crawford’s With the Im-
mortals, have not been quite successful.
Dr. Emil Reich, a writer on Plato,
modern history and a multitude of other
things, indulges in a little relaxation in
some dialogues called Nights with
the Gods (T. Werner Laurie), which
is something like an attempt to
cross Landor with J. K. Bangs. The
immortals discuss affairs of to-day with
most elephantine efforts to be funny.
Vociferous and inextinguishable laughter
is stated to have followed their ponderous
sallies, which gives one a poor opinion of
the sense of humor possessed by Dr.
Reich’s gods. There is, however, some
shrewd criticism to reward the reader
who survives the bad puns. Diogeries on
Bernard Shaw is worth listening to, and
Caesar’s comment on a House of Com-
mons debate ends with this pronounce-
ment :
“The truth is that no nation makes itself.
It is made by itself only in so far as it reacts
against the powerful influence of others. If
these are feeble and second-rate, the reacting
nation itself will remain feeble and second-
rate. . . The English ought to welcome,
although co combat the rise of Germany.
They imagine that their principal force
comes from their colonies. It will come not
from their colonies, which is geographically
impossible, but from their perennial rivalry
with great Continental Powers. These
rivalries made England, made her colonies.
To give up these rivalries, to cease combating
great Continental Powers, will be the end
both of England and her Empire.”
Willis—and Crick
W. N. Willisjias written something which
he calls The Life of W. P. Crick. It
might have been more appropriately called
“ Memoirs of Willis, with Some Remarks
About Crick and Others.” As a literary
performance it is about as bad as it could
be. The style is tawdry, there is no order or
arrangement, and there is much tedious
repetition. It is stuffed with old anecdotes,
which are mostly spoilt in the re-telling ;
but shining here and there are poetical
extracts like diamonds stuck in mud. If it
is permissible for the Devil to quote Scrip-
ture, certainly Mr. Willis can quote Shakes-
peare, and he does so frequently and with
unction. But he does not do Crick’s
memory much good service*
The story of Crick’s life might conceivably
be written with advantage ,but if done at all,
it would be better if it were done some time
hence, when the heat and dust raised by the
recent land troubles had subsided, and the
man, W. P. Crick, could be seen in truer
perspective. And Crick, with all his faults,
was very much a man, courageous and gener-
ous, a good hater and a better friend. He
was remarkable for an abnormal develop-
ment of intellect in a nature which otherwise
remained primitive. He was an able lawyer
with a touch of the pugilist ; an astute
politician with a dash of the larrikin.
Civilisation has wrapped all the important
acts of life in elaborate ceremonial, and
fighting has its recognised rules ; but if a
young barbarian like Napoleon enters the
field and makes straight for his object when
he ought to be in winter quarters, the
highly-cultivated enemy is disconcerted and
weakened before the fight begins. In some-
what the same way Crick entered into party
warfare. He had unlimited pluck, no respect
for authority, and a hatred of ceremonial.
He acted on the ancient maxim—“Bold,
always bold ! ” —and the directness of his
methods soon made him feared as “ a first-
class fighting man.” As he grew older he
learned to respect the conventions ; but the
primitive instincts were often too strong for
him—otherwise his intellect might have
carried him to a very high place as a public
man.

A WONDERFUL CURE
FOR INDIGESTION
REDFERN TESTIMONY.
“Each morning I woke with the consciousness
that something was not right,” remarked Mr.
William Johnston, of No. 127 Eveleigh Street,
Redfern, to a newspaper representative, who was
making some enquiries. Continuing he said :“I
felt as if I was in for a spell of sickness, and, sure
enough, those misgivings foretold what afterwards
happened. You see, the heat was pretty consider-
able where I was, up at Mole, seventy miles from
Warren, 115 deg. in the shade for a week at a
stretch'being regarded as only a fleabite to what
could happen when the sun was in good form, and
too much of that sort of business gets on a man’s
nerves, and runs him down a bit, I tell you, and
I think I ought to know.”
“Of course, because you are speaking from ex-
perience,” interrupted the scribe.
‘ ‘ Exactly. There were a good few of us working
at an artesian bore, which ejected soda water—
without. I need scarcely remark, any whisky in it
—and too much soda straight is not good. In-
deed, it proved very bad for us, and as we never
got within reach of anything in the way of vege-
tables, it is only to be expected that the conse-
quences were very bad for the blood. Mine, like
the rest, was in a terrible state, sores breaking out
in all directions, and the flies were so troublesome
that we had to go away and sit ig the dark to get
a little peace. The remedies I tried to put myself
in order with were of no avail, and my sufferings
became unbearable. When I sat down to a meal
I would get half way through when an attack of
retching always occurred, so no wonder my strength
fell off like it did. I became as weak as a kitten,
and, the work being laborious, 1 could not keep
going. Matters were about at their worst, when
the overseer went over ,to Bourke, and returned
with some Clements Tonic for the men, and I give
you my word as an honorable man, that after I had
taken but one bottle I felt a different being en-
tirely. The headaohes that I had beforehand
were enough to destroy one’s happiness, without
anything else ; but, besides, I had most of those
wretched symptoms which usually accompany in-
digestion. To begin with, my chest felt quite
clogged up, as though I had swallowed a lump of
was c^o &B'i n g the breathing piassages
and about my loins. I felt as if I had been beaten
with a stick sore and aching. Again, under the
blades of my shoulders there were shap, pricking
sensations as if a needle were at work, and m
audition to all else, there was a perpetual feeling
of wearmess as if I had run myself to death.
When getting out of bed of a morning, I would
have to hold on to it to steady myself, the dizzi-
ness being so great that I would have overbalanced,
and all the time I was going down-hill fast. My
weight was disappearing in a most alarming man-
ner, and I dread to think of what would have
happened had not Clements Tonic made its timely
appearance, through the kindness of our overseer. ”
Me had probably used it himself on some pre-
vious occasion ? ” hazarded the writer.
. .“Hesaid so, and swore by it. Thereisnomed-
icine in the world to equal Clements Tonic, he
used to say, and, my word, he was right, for it
fixed us up splendidly. Its purifving effects on
my blood cannot be too highly spoken of. Enough
to say that the sores soon disappeared, after which
I rested better. I began to eat more freely and
regularly under Clements Tonic’s influence, and
the retching after meals became a thing of the
past. And if Clements Tonic cannot cure indi-
gestion quicker than anything else, I’m a Dutch-
man. It put me right out of touch with each
symptom belonging to that complaint in no time:
then it added to its wonderful work by steadying
my nerves, and giving me as much strength as a
mail could wish for. Whenever I meet a person,
either man or woman, suffering in any particular
bke i did myself, I just tell them what I know
Clements Tonic can do, as in my case, and if they
c l° n Ro find get a bottle or two of that remedy,
then all I can say is, they deserve to suffer. Pub-
lish this any way you please.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, WILLIAM JOHNSTON, of 127 Eveleigh Street
Redfern, in the State of New South Wales, Commonwealth
of Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that
I have carefully read the annexed document, and that it
contains and is a true and faithful account of my illness
and cure by Clements Tonic, and also contains my'full per-
mission to publish in any way my statements—which I
give voluntarily, without receiving any payment; and I
make this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the
same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the
Oaths Act, 1000

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Pickle-Beauties’ Dance.
Staider hearts than mine would frown
On the sins of Pickle Town ;
Buckland-street and all its crew
Gulp a lot of mischief down.
Ye that lodge with Bacchus know
How the stacks of empties grow.
Belies of a hundred feasts
Once I gave a Bottle-Oh.
“ Yous er bonser bloke,” said he,
And straightway invited me
To “ Ther Pickle-Beauties’ Darnce—
Chewsday, ’All ov ’Armenee.
“ Don’tcher wear yer Percy clo’es,
Bet yer life ther Beauties knows
When er toff’s a-quizz’n’ ’em ! ”
Strange the ways of Bottle-Ohs !
“ Arst fer Plugger Bill—thet’s me ! ”...
’Mid the sounds of revelry
I was frankly introduced
Into Push Society.
In an atmosphere that swore
Fifty cobbers held the floor,
Eife-and-concertina band
Sweated beer from every pore.
Gowned in velveteen and fur,
Never maid looked comelier
Than the Pride of Pickle Town ;
I was introduced to her.
She was proper style and class ;
You could see the bits of glass
Glinting in her finger-rings,
And her bangles were of brass.
As we waltzed, a tragic youth,
In a manner moat uncouth,
Chewing cigarettes and beer.
Snarled across his one front tooth.
’Tween the dances on the stair,
Sitting out to get the air,
Once I kissed her silent lips
When it seemed no one was there.
Then she slapped my face and cried,
“Chuck it, mister, you’ve er nide ! ”
’Twas a wild anaemic youth,
Choked with frenzy at my side !
Oh, ye Lord of Stoush ! —a Thug
Swore he’d “ wipe me cross ther lug !”
“ Shut yer ’ead,” growled Bottle-Oil;
“ Me ’n’ ’im ’r’ pals, yer mug ! ”
Beauty smiled and said, “ This gent
Is me friend,” so argument
Ceased, and with the dance renewed
I was very well content.
And, lest there should be regrets,
Shouted beer and cigarettes.
The attenuated youth
Mouthed the quaintest epithets.
Harmony came after storm ;
It was blissful on the form
While the push swopped drinks and danced ;
Things were getting pretty warm,
When said she, “ I’m goin’ ’ome ! ’’ —
Smoothed her hair and fixed her comb. . . .
Never were two lips so sweet
Underneath the starry dome !
Youth is rash and Folly blind,
As immortal gods designed :
With an arm around my neck
How should I have looked behind ?
I was wakened by the crash
Of blue metal through the sash. . . .
’Twas a scared, anaemic youth
Took the palings at a dash.
N.s.w.
W. M. WHITNEY.
Unarmed: A Song of the Last Stand.
The North Sea holds our ships of war, and most
of the Empire’s men,
And the Empire’s batteries groan and roar, for
the Lion has left his den.
The rousing smell of the gun-gas drifts to a nation
about to die;
And, as the smoke of the battle lifts, we see our
foes draw nigh.
Our foes? Yes, we are about to snuff in the breeze
of the Mongol’s curse !
We’re doomed to death, and that’s bad enough—
but our women are doomed to worse !
So pass me my brick and my butcher’s knife, and
let me away to the fight,
And dear, indeed, will I sell my life* with my
bare steel gleaming bright!
The withering grass and the fading flower and the
clover the fat cow chews,
Like this we are—of a passing hour ; we’ll melt
like the morning dews !
But, ere the Mongol grabs my beer, and my good
blood reds the ground.
By the grace of my Brunswick brick I’ll smear a
splash of his gore around !
Ere yet the Mongol mounts my stair and seizes
the furniture,
Some of him shall lie stiffened where my blood
goes down the sewer !
So pass me my blade from the meatman’s block
and my brick, Australian made,
And when I’ve scattered my last, lorn rock, I’ll
face death, unafraid.
Oh, Ethel May shall wail and weep and worry her
golden hair,
And those who then our borders keep shall stare
at her, sitting there !
And though she smile on the foe, or shrink, her
future will be the same—
In heathen hovels she’ll shortly sink to the slave-
girl’s crawling shame.
So pass me out my final feed—the Mongol is
almost due.
Give me, in haste, the things I need—my sticks
and stones, my new.
Sharp axe wherewith, when death is near, to make
my final stand ;
And, for our poor, brave women—here, the
cyanide-draught’s at hand !
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.

/low to Address Letters.
Correspondents to Tim Bulletin would oblige the paver
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor. Bulletin.” is the proper
superscription for “copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, APRIL 29, 1909.
ANOTHER BREAKDOWN IN VIEW.
It becomes increasingly evident that the Fisher
Government stands on a precarious foundation,
and —bar accidents —its days seem to be numbered.
Alfred Deakin, who used to rather despise
office, seems to grow hungrier for it with in-
creasing years, and the question of presenting
Britain with a Dreadnought will probably furnish
the excuse for another shuffle of the political
cards. Deakin, despite liis disastrous coalitions
of the past, could not join with that soured Tory
and traitor Joseph Cook on matters of ordinary
policy, even to compass Fisher’s downfall. But
the Dreadnought craze, or flicker, or whatever it
may be called, is a sort of neutral subject on
which a momentary Cook-Deakin alliance is
possible. True, there is no special reason to
imagine that Britain specially wants to receive a
Dreadnought—it isn’t polite to look a gift ship in
the mouth ; but, all the same, it is practically
certain that Britain would much rather that
Australia took on the steady job of defending its
end'of the Empire in a regular way than that it
should hysterically give its aged parent one un-
manned ship, without any fund to cover repairs
and ultimate replacement, and then give three
cheers for itself and go to sleep. And it isn’t at
all certain that Australia wants to give that ship,
though there is certainly a dribble of subscrip-
tions, partly from title-hunters, partly from
people who regard the gift of a solitary vessel as
an excuse for shirking the much larger matter of
general naval defence, and partly from other
people. Further, neither Cook nor Deakin ex-
plains where the money is to come from to liqui-
date this sudden spasm, for the money will evi-
dently be wanted at short notice. Cook is
against Fisher’s proposed progressive land-tax ;
Deakin alleges that the revenue to be obtained
from the land-tax won’t arrive in time to save
Fisher from a deficit, even if he doesn’t go Dread-
noughting. And neither Cook nor Fisher dares
to openly suggest that we should borrow money
from Old Mother at “ Home ” in order to pay for
Old Mother’s own ship. If they temporarily com-
bine to throw out Fisher on the Dreadnought
question, then they will presumably separate
again, for, except on this one matter, union seems
impossible. That will mean one of two things:
Either (1) Cook, .with a minority Government,
will be struggling to find cash to pay the cost of a
sudden and unprovided-for ironclad while he will
be faced by a hostile majority, part of which,
under Deakin, will declare that he is getting the
money the wrong way, while the other part,
under Fisher, will declare that he shouldn’t get
it at all; or (2) Deakin will be in a similar pre-
dicament. In fact, if Deakin gets into office
over this wretched affair his position will almost
certainly be worse than it has ever been before.
He will be back where he was with three differ-
ences ; he will be minus much of the support which
the Fisher party used to give him ; he will
be minus much of the support and confidence of
his own followers ; and he will be plus
a huge and immediate liability, which—unless he
throws local defence, or old-age pensions, or the
Northern Territory, or the transcontinental rail-
ways overboard—will make his disordered and
shambling finances look like a fleeing army after
a great defeat.
Certainly there is still the chapter of accidents
to reckon with. The Fisher Government, if
defeated, may obtain a dissolution, though that
seems eminently improbable —in fact, it seems
that it would be quite unconstitutional, seeing
that, until Cook gets a show at forming a
Ministry, the possibilities of the House haven’t
been exhausted. And even should the impro-
bable event come to pass, there is no proof
that a general election would make the situation
much clearer. Then, by way of another possi-
bility, Deakin is a worn and weary-looking man
these times, and his physician may any day order
him out of the political arena for a rest, and by
the time he comes back refreshed the Dread-
nought question may have settled itself. Finally,
Deakin may go the way Abdul Hamid went, and
find his own followers clamoring at his palace
gate in South Yarra.and refusing to be led by him
any longer—in other words, they may refuse to
join him in taking a chance which may prove to
mean political suicide. But the chapter of acci-
dents isn’t much to rely upon. In all probability
the Fisher Government will go down, and then
chaos will supervene for a while, and the people
• —the patient, ox-like people who only want to
Get Things Done—will be left again. There are
so many things to be done—the new military
forces to organise, the navy to build, the Northern
Territory to be acquired and settled, the two abso-
lutely necessary transcontinental railways to be
constructed, the progressive land-tax to be im-
posed and collected for the bursting up of the
awful big estate incubus, the apparently serious
shortage in the funds available for the payment of
old-age pensions to be made good, the State debts
to be taken over, the question of the transfer to
the Commonwealth of the control of all
industrial legislation to Vie dealt with, the
growing demand for unification and recon-
struction to be looked to, and a number of other
matters to boot. The country, with all this work
on hand, can’t spare time for the constant swap-
ping of portfolios, which threatens to be all the
business which will be done this year. At such a
time as the present no one is justified in hanging
up public affairs indefinitely over a matter of the
presentation of a ship by a country which doesn’t
want to give it, to a country which doesn’t care
twopence whether it gets the blessed vessel or not.
* * * *
The obvious fact is that Parliamentary Govern-
ment as now practised has broken down. It has
fallen over its own feet and got into its own road.
It is a failure, and a bad failure at that. Even
when things are being done, the procedure eats
its own head off through the innumerable multi-
tude of formalities. As an instance, a Sydney
morning paper once in a bitter mood explained
the procedure necessary to get any small Bill—it
may be a measure of one clause, and that clause
may be some old thing about dogs—through the
N.S, W. Assembly, and in this matter the N.S.W.
Assembly is no worse and no better than its
neighbors :
(1) Message is read from the Governor asking that
necessary provision be made ; (2) message is referred to
the committee on the Bill; (3) Minister gives notice of
motion for the House to go into Committee to consider
expediency of bringing in the Bill; (4) motion is con-
sidered ; (5) motion moved that the Speaker should leave
the chair and the House go into Committee ; (6) resolu-
tion is carried in Committee ; (7) motion that Chairman
leave the chair ; (8) resolution is reported to the House ;
(9) resolution is adopted ; (10) Bill is introduced and read
<6, first time; (11) motion fixing date of second reading;
(12) second reading debate ; (13) motion to go into Com-
mittee to consider the Bill in detail; (14) motion for
Chairman to leave the chair and report the Bill with or
without amendments; (15) motion that the report be
now adopted ; (16) motion to fix date of third reading ;
(17) third reading ; (18) Speaker reports that the Bill is in
accordance with the Bill as passed in Committee; (19)
motion that the Bill do now pass ; (20) motion declaring
the title of the Bill; (21) motion that the Bill be sent to
the Legislative Council for concurrence.
After all this is done the Bill goes to the useless
Upper House, which plods through much the
same course of procedure. And the amendments,
if any, made by the Upper House have to be
considered, with much formality and an intermin-
able crawling into committee like a caterpillar
and crawling out of committee like a beetle, by
the Lower House ; and the decision arrived at by
the Lower House has to be pawed over at great
length by the Upper House ; and sometimes there
is a lengthy conference between the two institu-
tions ; and very often the end of the session
arrives in the middle of the struggle, and all this
fruitless effort goes into the heap of unfinished
business and is lost.
* * * *
This is how things are when Parliament is really
working, with the addition that many of these 21
stages of procedure are commonly used, not merely
for the purpose of curnbrously considering the Bill—
which, as already mentioned, may be some unim-
portant old thing in one clause relating to dogs—-
but for purposes of obstruction with a view to
getting the other party’s Government out and get-
ting “our ” party’s Government in. In addition,
there is an incredible waste of time through con-
spiracies against the Government, counter-con-
spiracies on behalf of the Government, changes
of Government, adjournments to let the new
Government have a chance to formulate its
policy ; through needless dissolutions and elec-
tions, through long shuttings-up because a weak
Government is afraid to face the Legislature, and
through other tortuous intrigues. And all the
while thg people who Want Things Done, who pay
for Having Things Done, and who don’t Get Things
Done, are neglected and unconsidered. They are
so accustomed to not having things done that
they hardly seem to realise that anything is
seriously amiss.
* * * #
The Bulletin has said all this before, but with
the prospect of another long hiatus or interregnum
while politicians swap portfolios, and another
vast hanging up of public business, the time has
arrived to siiy it all again. Australia needs first
of all a simplification of Parliamentary pro-
cedure. Then it badly needs to make the Refer-
endum a part of its political policy, so that
the real opinion of the country on any urgent
question can be easily ascertained, and the ghost
of sham opinion which is raised by the daily press
can be laid. In the matter of the Federal capital,
for instance, the Sydney dailies declared so per-
sistently that N.S.W. wouldn’t have Dalgety at
any price, and that the State would sooner secede
than have the metropolis anywhere save at Can-
berra, that many foolish politicians really came to
believe that their only hope of political salvation
lay in making haste to join a movement that
didn’t exist. And in similar fashion the assurances
of the daily press that Australia would sooner fall
down dead than not present Britain with a Dread-
nought are driving foolish politicians to rush
headlong into a crusade which only exists in
the fervid imagination of that same daily
press. The Referendum would be a prompt
and simple means of clearing these gaseous
bogies out of the political path, and would
thus make politics much more workable than they
are. It is necessary to have the Second Ballot in
order to make Parliament a better reflex of popular
opinion. And,.above all, it is essential to at least
make an experiment with Elective Ministries,
appointed for a fixed term, in order to save the
ghastly loss of time which arises from the shuffling
around of portfolios, and in schemes, devices, con-
spiracies and cabals with a view to the shuffling
round of portfolios. On every question that can
possibly arise there must needs be in Parliament
a majority for or against. And if a system can be
adopted under which every question must be con-
sidered on its own individual merits, apart from
the side issues which now arise through the per-
petual desire to get the other fellow out of office
and to put our own fellow into office—if the fellow
in office is there for a fixed term whether his
measures are defeated or not then the
Parliamentary machine can hardly help
travelling at a greatly increased pace. It
is not necessary to make the experiment
of Elective Ministries a fixed and permanent de-
vice at first. If the community is nervous about
the result, the idea might first be tried for, say,
six years, things to revert automatically after that
time to the old party system, unless the Elective
Ministries law was re-enacted. Even the most
scared member of the community could hardly
object to a six years’ trial of the proposed policy.
* * * *
It seems a pity that there should be a stoppage
to grease the political machinery at a time when
progress is so essential. But there are so many
things to be done, and the old system seems so
pathetically incapable of doing them. Progress
has never been easy or rapid, because the anti-
quated political devices were always stopping to
fall over their own feet, always getting in their
own road, always coming to a dead stand in the
middle of the stream to change a blind horse for
a lame one, or a dead horse for an imaginary one.
And now that there are signs ahead of another
breakdown—the country hasn’t yet got started
after the last one—and another indefinite
stoppage, and another swapping in the middle of
the stream of a jibbing horse for a confirmed bolter
—well, if the community’s patience isn’t worn out
it ought to be.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
There is Only One Dalgety, and Carruthers
is its Prophet.
The Monaro district of N.S.W. (the table-land on
which stands Dalgety by the Snowy River, the
place that was selected, and may be again
selected, as a Federal capital site) held
its annual show the other day; and the
mere enumeration of the things produced on that
allegedly barren plateau and exhibited by the
allegedly non-existent inhabitants thereof filled five
columns in the local paper. There were sheep, of
course, also horses, pigs, cows, hens, and sundries
in great numerousness. In addition, however,
there were peaches, grapes, pears, and many other
kinds of fruit grown in the country which the
Sydney papers described as too bleak for human
habitation ; and the region where the infuriated
Teece, of the A.M.P. Society, declared vegetables
to be impossible, put 26 kinds of vegetable on
exhibition in large quantities, in addition
to wheat, lucerne, maize, etc., all of local
growth. The exhibits were very tine and large,
but the exhibition which N.S.W.’s ex-Premier
Cakruthers made of himself was the most re-
markable exhibit of all. After Wade’s Minister
for Agriculture had enthused over the splendors
of the district which Wade declares to be quite
unfit for the inhabitants of a Federal capital to
live in, and somebody named Miller had pro-
posed Carruthers’ health and made a touching
reference to the fact that a crop of Monaro wheat
had been known to reach as high as 40 or 50
bushels to the acre, Carruthers (the man who
originally declared a capital bn Monaro to be
beyond all possibility owing to the sterile and
inhospitable character of the country) upended
himself and spoke a piece. (Part of his little
oration has already appeared in The Bulletin,
but it is good enough to be worth printing twice): —
Mr. J. H. Carruthers thanked the gathering for the
reception accorded him. He noticed his old friend Mr.
Harnett, the “Man from Snowy River,” whom he
had seen in Parliament for 25 years past. (Applause.)
He thought that the way Mr. Harnett used to recite
that poem instilled him with a desire to come to the
“ roof of Australia,” and see the old place where they bred
the men from the Snowy River. (Applause.) Then there
was his old friend, Mr. Merrett, who with a bottle of
wine christened a peak which would for all time bear the
speaker’s name. (Applause.) He contrasted the life of
the man on the land, its freedom, and contact with the
bracing atmosphere, with that of the dweller in the city,
and expressed the belief that there was no part of Aus-
tralia that had such a splendid climate as Monaro ; and
not only for crops, but children. (Applause.) They
wanted an Experimental Farm in the district, which was
a good one for agriculture.
It might be reasonably supposed that the speaker
was quite a different man from the one who raved
for three years to the effect that bleak and barren
Monaro was wholly disqualified by Nature for the
site of a Federal city, but he was carefully in-
spected, back and front, by butchers and other
meat experts, who solemnly guaranteed that
he was the same Carruthers. Also he was iden-
tified by the police. Apparently the difference
lies in the fact that Joseph is no longer Premier,
and is no longer required, for the sake of his
billet, to sacrifice the interests of Australia to
those of the Sydney daily papers, which want the
Federal city planted so close to their own offices
that they will be able to command the circulation
and advertising business of the inhabitants. On
no other hypotheses can the amazing discrepancy
between the Joe that was and the Joseph that is
be accounted for.
»
Wade is Still Unsatisfied.
The latest news about the Federal capital is to
the effect that even now State Premier Wade
isn’t satisfied. Apparently W ade wili never be
satisfied. The Australian Parliament has done a
lot for the gratification of Wade, and it
has failed. In the first place it backed down to
him re the matter of the site, and agreed to put
the capital where he wanted it. Then it was
realised that if the capital of all Australia was to
have even the semblance of an occasional drink,
it must include those two notable trickles, the
Cotter and the Molonglo, and if they were both
included in a square bit of ground it would run to
about 2000 or 2500 square miles. So the bit of
ground was disembowelled, so to speak, and it
consists on the map of one long leg along the
Cotter, and another long leg along the Molonglo,
and the two legs are joined together at the top
by a bit of land like the handle of a carpet
bag. It is a ridiculous device, but it keeps the
proposed Federal territory down to 1000 square
miles—to oblige Wade. To further oblige Wadk,
who doesn’t want to give away anything valuable,
the land selected includes the most rocky and
barren stretches in the district. This is made
quite a feature of by the State Frights press ; on
April 21 the S. M. Herald dealt with it again in
a leader headed “Australia’s City,” and assured
all and sundry that the land—
however excellent for catchment purposes, is vert little
USE FOR ANYTH INS ELSE.
Yet, even though the fool of a Federal Parliament
has tried to oblige Wade by selecting, for the site
of the one city in all the world which is to be the
capital of a whole continent, a mass of dull and
useless rocks —even after that there is a dead bee
in Wade’s ointment. The selection couldn’t
avoid including the little town of Queanbeyan
with about 1200 people, and Wade says the
State can’t be expected to part with “an import-
ant place like Queanbeyan.” So now it is difficult
to know what can be done to satisfy the awful and
grumpy Wade. His views seem to be :
(1) The capital must be in New South Wales.
(2) It must be as close up as possible to the most
thickly-peopled section of N.S.W.
(3) At the same time it mustn’t contain any population
worth mentioning. It must be an empty area in the
middle of a fairly thick population.
(4) And as the Federal city must have some water, and
most watercourses of any size in fairly well-settled dis-
tricts have towns on them, and the sulky infant Wade
bars the surrender of towns, it seems that the only way
to please Wade is to make the capital perfectly dry.
And there is no guarantee that Wade would be
satisfied even then. In fact, the chances are that
he would find some excuse for being madder than
ever.
+
The Slump of Cook.
When Prime Minister Fisher declared his
naval policy Joe Cook, that weird political joke,
arose in great scorn and denounced the scheme as
straight-out lunacy. Anybody who knew any-
thing about the subject, Cook explained, was per-
fectly aware that a coastal defence scheme was
absurd ; and that destroyers were worse than use-
less. They could never hope to defend the coast
against those modern monsters of the Dreadnought
type; and “anybody who knew anything about
the matter” knew that the naval battle that
would decide the fate of the Empire and
Australia would be fought in either the North
Sea or the Pacific. The thing that
brought to an end the denunciation of
Joseph the Renegade was the giving out
of the English language. This modern embodi-
ment of Hannibal, Nelson and Julius Caesar
simply could not find words to express his derision
and contempt for the ignorance of Fisher and his
colleagues—especially one named Hughes, whom
he derisively described as “Admiral” Hughes.
There are occasions when it is better for turn-coat
politicians of the Cook stamp to trim, to hedge,
and to be vague and indefinite. And it is unfortu-
nate for Cook that the only subject on which be
let himself go and fluted to the top of his bent is
the one on which the Imperial representatives have
a right to give full utterance. Australian defence is
merely a part of Imperial defence, and on that
ground the Imperial representatives in Australia
are entitled to speak. At the recent annual
dinner of the N.S.W. Society of St. George in
Sydney, Governor-General Dudley and Admiral
Richard Poore both spoke on this subject; and in
doing so they biffed Cook and the Sydney Tele-
graph where they both lived. Admiral Poore
said that he had come to the conclusion that "a
torpedo flotilla and destroyers would be of tre-
mendous advantage to Australia. The moral
effect, in case of war, and the British navy
being withdrawn from these waters, would be
splendid, if an Australian squadron were ready
to defend the coasts. . . . The best defence
of the Empire lay in the power of in-
stantly taking the offensive.” And, of course,
the presence of an Australian coastal squad-
ron to beat off raiding cruisers or squadrons,
would make it easier for the Imperial authorities
to concentrate the big fighting ships where they
would do the most good. Them the Governor-
General proceeded to give Cook his finishing
stroke. Said his Ex. : “ Speaking personally, I
am entirely in favor of the formation of an Aus-
tralian navy. ... I believe that the time is ar-
riving when every part of the Empire must make
due contribution to Imperial defence. . . . How
are these provisions to be made ? Either by giv-
ing an adequate subsidy in money, or by a
contribution of ships and men of your
own. . . . To give an adequate contribution
you would have to set aside £2,000,000 or
£3,000,000. Is it likely you will ever be
able to get that sum without obtaining what is
impossible l —a voice in the management or control
of the policy. I believe you will get far more
from this country in the way of ships and men.”
Now the question is whether Joe Cook is pre-
pared to upend himself and denounce Admiral
Poore and Governor-General Dudley as men
who don’t know what they are talking about, and
to speak derisively of his Ex. as “Admiral”
Dudley ? Anyhow, after his cocksure howls of a
few weeks ago it is “up to” Joe Cook to either
do that fearsome thing, or admit candidly that he
is an ignoramus—a blatant skiter, who spoke em-
phatically on a subject of which he was entirely
ignorant.
■» - -
The Fruits of Industry.
A committee was recently appointed by the Labor
Congress of N.S.W. to think hard over proposals
for a Federation of Labor, and the committee
drew up an interim report and a draft programme
for the proposed Federation. Here is the beginning
of the report:—
OBJECTS.
That the objective of the federation shall be to obtain
for the workers the full fruits of their industry.
The thing stopped there, and left the matter as
undefined as Joseph Cook’s principles. For
what is “the full fruits of their industry?” How
much of the fruits is it proposed to set aside as
remuneration for the inventor, the man with a
genius for organisation, and the man with the
capacity for supervision. There is no talent
required to inherit and own land, <lnd let it out to
tenants, and raise the rents on every possible
occasion, and live on the unearned increment.
Any fool can do these things, and any fool does
them. The landowner isn’t industry; therefore The
Bulletin believes that land should be national-
ised, or else that the area of country land which
one man can own should be limited to the amount
which will furnish a good living for himself and
family, the area being varied according to the soil


and rainfall; and that, if the owner gives up
cultivating his own property and lets it, his tenant
should have the right of compulsory purchase at
a price fixed by the State. Again, there is no
talent required to inherit bank and insurance
shares, therefore The Bulletin believes in State
banking and State insurance. But invention, dis-
covery, the capacity for organisation and manage-
ment, must be paid for, and in many cases they
must be paid for at very high prices if the best
service is to be obtained. There is no reason why
the man who gets £lOO,OOO, or even £500,000,
for the discovery of a great mineral held, or
for a great wealth-producing invention, or a
man who gets £5OOO or £lO,OOO a year
for managing or organising an industry
should be allowed to invest his money in land or
in any other way that will give his incapable
descendants an ever-increasing lien upon the earn-
ings of posterity for all time. The reward
shouldn’t apply to incapable descendants, but the
reward of the man who actually does great things
should be magnificent. If there is no great
opportunity to invest the money at compound
interest then the fortune which the industrial
Solomon made will speedily dissipate in the
hands of the industrial Rehoboams who come
after, and will go back to the people from whom it
came; bqt Solomon’s own reward should be large,
otherwise Solomon will go elsewhere. According
to the Federal Statistician, the whole value of pro-
duction in Australia and Tasmania in 1907
agricultural, pastoral, mining, manufacturing, and
all the rest—was £107,000,000. If that were
equally divided it would represent nearly £4O
a year per inhabitant, or £2OO a year for
a family of five—father, mother, and three
children. If the useless landlord—not the one
who owns a moderate bit of land and works it to
the best advantage, but the mere superfluous col-
lector of rents—were eliminated, and all the
useless elements of the population were forced to
become producers, there seems no reason why
production should not represent £5O per head.
With new devices and new inventions there is no
reason why it should not in time reach £lOO per
head—equal to nearly £lO a week for every family
of five. And there is no reason why it should
not go on increasing indefinitely after that.
But if by ‘'the worker” is meant the
purely manual toiler, he can’t get the com-
plete fruits of all that production, for in-
vention and organisation must be paid for
at a handsome rate. Otherwise things will
get no further forward, and to go no
further forward is to go back—to the dogs and
the Devil. For though the invention and organ-
isation that we have to-day may be equal to pro-
ducing £4O worth of wealth per head per annum,
yet half a century hence they may be so obsolete as
not to be worth £5 per annum. There was a time
in Australia when, so far as history relates, the
worker did enjoy “ the full fruits of his industry,”
but for lack of invention and organisation he was
still a mere aboriginal, worth about ss. every 12
months, and followed by a lot of unregistered dogs
of the flea-bitten religion. Therefore, it is up to
the responsible parties, whoever they may be, to
more clearly define their position, which at present
is only a platitude. The mere land lord is superfluous,
bo is the mere usurer, bo are a host of mere com-
mercial parasites. But the inventor and the
organiser are indispensable, and they need to be
lavishly paid if the services of the best of them
are to be secured. Where do they come in, and
what is their share of the “fruits” ?
A Fleet and Its Tail.
A student of the Blue Water kindergarten sends
this :
Sir Edward Grey’s horrifying statement that Britain
needs to re-build the whole fleet should wring a wail of
protest from the British taxpayer, already tottering
under the mere cost of upkeep of and additions to the
floating ironmongery. The modern fleet is very like a
kangaroo—it has a long tail. But the Too has a big
advantage over the fleet, for his tail is his main support:
the fleet’s tail is an unreliable encumbrance which retards
its speed, but which has to be depended on more or less,
and trailed carefully across the oceans. The admirals
have to lose their sleep thinking out plans so that the
weak, slow old-timers may be. last into the possible row
and first out of it. Altogether the veteran ships are a
continual anxiety ; but, although Britain has an immense
number of them, the other nations also have their fair
share. In the official lists, the acknowledged tail of each
navy is called “older,” while the useful body part is
known as “ modern." The battleship anatomy of the sea
was lately set down as under; —
' Building or
Modern. Older. Laid Down. Total.
Britain ..41 13 6 60
Germany 20 4 7 31
France 16 5 6 27
Italy ..6 6 5 17
United States 21 4 8 33
Japan ..10 6 3 19
114 38 35 187
Of course there is now a suspicion that some of these
statistics are quite unreliable ; still they were latelv
accepted as correct. It is only the big, heavily-armed
and armored ships that count when two fleets that look
like two chains of smoky dots start to get within
five or six miles of each other. Then it is a
case of “Seconds out of the ring!”—and the sort
of things that are labelled “ Powerful,” but which are
merely “ Horrible,” scuttle clear of the impending crash.
So we get the battleship strength of the world as some-
thing well under 200 units—which estimate mav safely
cover the Dreadnought Maoriland threatens to borrow,
and the GotnothingN.S. W. is trying to beg. (Luckily, the
Yankee fleet got clear of Sydney Harbor before this craze
for collecting, battleships set in ; otherwise Wade might
have seized them even as his colleague Carruthkrs seized
the wire netting from the wharf.) Another standard of
comparison other than -the number of battleships may be
taken ; it is the total number of ships carrying guns equal
or superior to those of the Dreadnoughts. Only ships
aimed with these tremendously powerful weapons will be
able to penetrate, at a four-miles range, the llin. armored
belts that guard the vitals of the sea-giants. Ships whose
armament is not sufficiently powerful to pierce the Dread-
nought armor at that distance are liable to be hammered
to death—if they have the bad luck to engage one of the
modern fighting machines ; for their shells will be bursting
more or less harmlessly about the big brother’s ribs while
he is standing off and smashing them at his leisure.
Apparently, the only hope of the older ships is that, when
the great day comes, they may exchange shots with
equally decrepit tanks. On the gun-power test, Bull
does not show up so well; in fact, he shows up rather
badly. By 1911 he will have 12 ships fitted with guns
powerful enough to puncture a Dreadnought at four
miles; but Japan and the U.S.A. will both be on the
same mark with a dozen each ; France will be ambling
along with six or eight; and Germany will have collected
16—which will give it a lead of four on Bull, unless the
agitation in England forces the Government to
build four more; or unless Maoriland gives two
and bursts gloriously like a patriotic f -og, and
Australia gives two more and swoons ..way for
several years on Cohen’s doormat. But, although there
is a mad desire to rule out every blessed thing that isn’t
Dreadnought, it isn’t common sense to ignore the battle-
ships of the King Edward class, of which England has
about six times the number owned by William of Berlin.
All the scare naval estimates nowadays seem to be
based on the assumption that something very near
approaching perfection and finality has been reached ;
but, to the reasoning outsider, it looks the sort of ram-
shackle logic that is built for the political needs of a day.
The King Edwards and their near relatives (they
are of about the same age and gun-power as the
U.S. ships we admired recently) are sufficiently
well armed to argue with all the ships of the
pre-Dreadnought era at four miles range, and, as they
are in the proportion of six to Germany’s one, there
doesn’t seem to be any ground for nervousness on their
middle-aged account. If the big row ever does arrive, it
will probably divide itself into two parts. The first will
be a wild and awful struggle between the Dread-
nought pugilists or their successors ; and the second
will be a slightly less impressive conflict—or
series of conflicts—between the old-age pensioners.
The first is very likely to result in the ruination
of both sets of pugs, for they will probably be very
evenly matched in strength and numbers, and the victor
will most likely feel much too sick for further trouble,
but, whereas Germany has only a handful of old men of
the sea, Britain has a vast horde of them ; and, when the
mastodons have ceased their clashing, these will sally
forth with their whiskers streaming in the gale, and
the foreign venerables will fly appalled before them.
Britain certainly lias a long' naval tail, but there does not
appear to be any necessity to get hysterical about it. In
a world of tadpoles, surely it is an advantage to be the
fattest and longest tadpole of the lot!
An Evil that Toryism Mourns Over, but
Won’t Have Scotched.
Melbourne Argus handled the “ social evil ” in
gingerly fashion the other day, and wound up the
usual batch of grieved but strictly non-committal
remarks on the subject by saying, with a show of
decision: —
It is a matter that urgently-demands the attention of
the Government, with a view to new legislation being
proposed.
It is the sort of thing that papers like the Argus,
and that the people who follow the Argus’ poli-
tical lead, have been saying very firmly ever since
the oldest of us can remember. That they will
continue doing so till the Crack of Doom is quite
sure. It is also quite sure that if any Government
makes the slightest attempt to introduce legislation
to mitigate the said “ evil,” the Argus and those
who think with it will emit the same old shriek
of shocked disapproval which they set up when,
during the Dreadnought pother, someone
ventured to talk of Australian defence. The
most recent attempt which has been made in
Australia to legislate in regard to the evil which,
the Argus says “urgently demands legislative
attention,” was that of N.S.W. State Premier
Wade, who, some months ago, submitted a Conta-
gious Diseases Bill to the N.S.W. Parliament. It
wasn’t much of a Bill at best. All it provided for
was the treatment of apprehended criminals.
The step that was suggested wasn’t a particularly
revolutionary one—merely that no one who, on
admission to a State gaol, should be found to be
suffering from a certain appalling complaint,
should be allowed to mix with the world
again till he or she should have been
cured. Both the Sydney morning dailies had
written during the previous couple of months of
the horrifying results of the complaint which
Wade’s legislation was designed to check. An
evening paper had devoted a series of special
articles to it, and in the course of them had men-
tioned that Sydney was one of the half-dozen
most disease-accursed ports in the world. “ Fleet
week” was drawing near. The police were in-
structed to take action “for the credit of the
State,” and so hundreds of the better-known street
women were apprehended on trifling charges, and
locked up till the Yankee tars had gone. Every-
one appreciated the ghastliness of the trouble and
the desirableness of not merely tinkering with it,
but of dealing with it in comprehensive fashion.
And yet when Wade’s measure bobbed above
the horizon, which it did shortly after the de-
parture of the U.S.A. fleet, a scream of pious
dissent resounded from every corner of the State.
Christian Temperance Unions, Methody parsons,
Loyal Orange lunatics, local preachers, hot gos-
pellers, and a thousand other rabbit-brained
citizens and organisations sent petitions to Wade
and begged him, in the name of holiness, not to
interfere with the disease. The dailies started
talking uneasily about “ the liberty of the subject,”
and “experimental legislation,” and otherwise
poured chilled fluid on the measure. In the end,
Wade was constrained to give his measure to the
State under the harmless-sounding alias of the
Prisoners’ Detention Bill, and as such it does a
considerable amount of good work. It wastypicalof
whatalways liappenswhen Toryismand piety work
together. The complaint on which Wade desired
to make war is responsible for a goodly proportion
of the idiots, cripples, perverts, criminals and
other hopeless ones who afflict society. It is as
preventable as plague or the smallpox, and infi-
nitely more loathsome and deadly and far-reaching
in its effects than either. It strikes the innocent
even more than the guilty. Yet the churches
(the same religious element which labored to sup-
press washing many centuries ago) toil for the
preservation of the disease, and Toryism, which
is the Church in politics, is on the same side.

Again tlie Capital-Scaring Slander.
Sydney Telegraph weighed in (21/4/’O9) with an
unusually acrid assault on the alleged capital-
scaring effects of Labor in Australia. Its text
was supplied by Dr. J. Robertson, an amazing
Scotchman recently returned from abroad, and on
the strength of his remarks the papers said :
A good deal of truth is in Dr. Robkrtson’s statement
that British capital is not being put into Australian
enterprise, because the British capitalist distrusts our
Socialistic tendencies and Labor domination. London
can lend to the State Governments because the country
is pledged, but to Private Enterprise practically nothing
is coming. ... There would be many millions more paid in
wages in Australia annually, but that Labor implaca-
bility shuts it out. It is its own worst enemy, and de-
prives the working man of a large percentage of his
proper wages.
The Telegraph is one of those sheets that seems
chronically unable to understand that any country
can have capital invested in it, to its (the
country’s) advantage, unless the said capital has
been imported from abroad. A country, for ex-
ample, that is finding its own capital for its own
industries appeals to it (unless its printed words
belie it most villainously) as on the verge of dis-
aster. Whatever the invariably anonymous
British capitalist, whom purveyors of decayed
fish of the Telegraph and Robertson brand
consort with, may profess to think about the
local Labor menace, and whatever steps he may
say he is driven to take in consequence of it, the
fact remains that the indigenous capitalist (the man
on the spot, and the one, therefore, with the best
opportunities of knowing the correct state of
affairs) is by no means chary of sinking his cash in
Australia. The Bulletin can board a tram
outside the front door of its office, and
in a matter of six minutes can find itself at
the biggest warehouse south of the,line. In the
days when the Telegraph’s Tory and Foreign-
trade friends ran the §how in N.S.W., this estab-
lishment imported everything from abroad. To-
day its factories cover a larger area than its shop.
An immense fortune has been sunk in them.
They employ an army of white Australians at
white men’s wages. >And the owners are doing
well out of their venture, and don’t seem to worry
one iota about the Menace of Toil which keeps
the unspecified over-sea acquaintances of Robert-
son and the Telegraph in a constant state of the
jumps. It is but one example out of scores.
Everywhere in Sydney and environs, under Pro-
tection and advanced Labor legislation, tweed
and woollen and furniture and boot and pottery
and hat manufactures are springing up.
That is a fact that may be ascertained by
a cruise round the city and suburbs any
day of the working week. Capital (wherever it
has come from) has gone into concerns that have
lapped up the out-of-work so effectively that his
sort is probably harder to find in Sydney to-day
than in any other city of its size in the world.
The Telegraph buttresses its argument that the
British capitalist regards Australia as a doomed
country by the statement that London only lends
to its State Governments because “the country is
pledged.” And yet the London capitalist, who
lias already lent N.S.W. £88,000,000, and who
continues to lend further millions when
asked, is just as dependent for the safety of
his money on the integrity and sane behavior of
Laborites and Labor Ministries, present and to
come, as any investor in any private enterprise
concern in any of the States. The Telegraph
talks largely of the country being “pledged.” But
what is that likely to signify if its inhabitants and
administrators are the mad-headed Anarchists
that the anti-Australian section here and abroad
represents them to be. Nations and States,
mostly under anti-Labor Governments, have re-
pudiated their debts before; and, where their
Governments are the sort that Anti-Sosh says
Labor Governments are they will do it again.
Austria did it in 1811, and again in 1816. It
paid about 7d. in the £. The Russian Empire
declared itself bankruptin 1843. Spain and Turkey
have let their creditors in at different times, and
so, in the most impudent and wanton fashion,
have various States of theU. S. of America, whose
position under the Law of Nations is almost
exactly analogous to those of the States of the
Commonwealth. As Melbourne Age noted last
week :
Probably few people are aware of the immense amount
of money that has been lent to foreign nations that have
failed to keep faith with their creditors. British
investors, unfortunately, are largely concerned in such
transactions, and, in order to safeguard their interests,
an organisation, called the Corporation of Foreign Bond-
holders, was created in London as far back as 1868. Since
its institution, this body has helped in the settlement of
debts aggregating £1,000,000,000, and its report for 1908
shows that it has still under review a principal sum of
£16,000,000 owing by defaulting States, in addition to
arrears of interest thereon amounting to £30,620,000.
With that sort of object-lesson before him, the
knowledgable British money-lender, who is the
British capitalist, is unlikely to splash his
coin round in places where “Labor domin-
ation,” or any other domination, appears
to him to threaten catastrophe. The British
capitalist who was going to invest huge sums in
Australian industries, but decided ultimately that
he wouldn’t do it owing to “Socialism,” is a
nebulous person who is constantly mentioned by
the anti-Australian confidence crowd, but is never
produced. The British capitalist who is so sure
of the probity, decency and common-sense of the
Laborites, who are the coming administrators of
most of this continent, that he is prepared to add
to the already huge sum lie has sunk here by way
of loans, is a tangible flesh-ajid-blood citizen. He
stands as permanent proof that the people who
say that Labor domination and Socialism and
the rest frighten away the real Briton with real
money at the back of him are slanderous liars.
—4.
It is only about a week since N.S. W. State Insect
Wade inquired plaintively why the Australian
Parliament proposed to interfere in industrial
matters. All the States, barring Tasmania, had
machinery for the protection of the workers ; and
Tasmania, he was assured, would soon have simi-
lar legal gear to prevent sweating. It seemed to
Wade that the Australian Parliament was
really interfering where there was no neces-
sity. What did the Australian Parliament
want, anyhow ? Last Monday week a deputa-
tion from the Labor Congress waited on
Wade’s chief railway commissioner, Johnson.
The deputation requested that he would follow
the practice of the Public Works Department, and
insert a clause in all contracts insisting on a living
wage and prohibiting the practice of sub-letting.
Mr. Johnson said he was opposed to sweating, but
he would not insertany conditions into thecontracts
to prevent it. He would not interfere with Private
Enterprise. At the same time he would not encour-
age sweating. How he is to prevent sweating
if he will not interfere with Private Enterprise he
did not explain ; altogether, judging by his reply,
Mr. Johnson is a glorified example of George
Reid and his Yes-No. Johnson’s attitude
is that he was imported to make a com-
mercial success of the railways, and he
will not do anything that may inter-
fere with the process of lowering expenses.
If the men are not satisfied with the rates paid,
Johnson suggested that they should invoke the
Court; and if the Court ordered him to insert
such a clause as that suggested he would obey the
Court. Johnson gave them his word of honor he
would obey the Court. Johnson’s attitude was
somewhat of an answer to Mr. Wade’s plaintive
inquiry as to why the Australian Parliament
wants to interfere. The Commonwealth desires a
tribunal whose decisions shall apply to the whole
of Australia, and whose awards cannot be evaded
by shifting the scene of operations a few miles
from some metropolitan centre. Also it wants a
Court clothed with such powers that it will be
quite needless for Mr. Johnson to promise that
he will respect its decisions —a Court of such a
character that Johnson’s acceptance of its deci-
sions will be one of those obvious things which
nobody thinks of mentioning just on account of
their obviousness.
Premier Murray, of Victoria, bids fair to
be as bad a failure as Bent. During Easter
week he was spreading himself round his
electorate of Warrnambool, and certain land-
hungry persons drew his attention to the
Chocolyn estate, which has blocked the pro-
gress of Camperdown district since it was first
locked up to graze sheep. Chocolyn was sought
by Bent’s abortive Closer Settlement Board, but
the owners asked £3O per acre, and the Board
reckoned it was too much. But Murray
was told that, owing to Bent’s land-booming
policy, the estate would to-day actually bring
£33 or £35 per acre. Murray's interro-
gators hinted at some measure or policy
that would tend to reduce the Chocolyn
acres to their real value—based on their produc-
tive capacity —and remarked casually that, for
land-tax purposes, the place is at present valued
at £4 per acre, and pays £207 per annum ; where-
as under an honest valuation, Fisher’s proposed
land-tax would collect £2360 per annum. Then
the turncoat Premier roused himself and assured
the hungry settlers that “special legislation was
needed to deal with a case like this.” He didn’t
give any defails about this “special legislation” ;
neither did he hint at any intention to levy an
effective land-tax, and so save the Federal Parlia-
ment the trouble of doing his (Murray’s) work.
He merely notified the need for “special legisla-
tion,’’and fell asleep again.
From the “ Old Soldier ” :
The O’Sullivan told the people at gaud Mayor
Taylor’s Dreadnought meeting in Sydney what he would
do if he were Dictator in Britain. He would send the
British fleet to look for the German navy. In 70, when
the German navy consisted of only one ship of any
account, the French fleet went to the Baltic to look for
it, and also to bombard the fortress at Colberg. It could
neither find the fleet nor get within range of anything
worth firing at. Perhaps the O’Sullivan’s Admiral would
have better (or worse) fortune. Germany nowadays has a
fleet, and its coast defences are in order, so O’Sullivan’s
Admiral might not have the Frenchman’s luck.
After groping among the islands and mudflats
that line the whole German North Sea coast,
and getting half his ships aground, he might decide to go
round into the Baltic, only fo learn that the whole Ger-
man fleet had gone through the Kiel Canal into the
North Sea, and was working its wicked will in parts un-
known. Since the meeting, the O’Sullivan seems to
have been thinking things over, and has apparently come
to the conclusion that it is not quite good for his political
health to oppose the babor policy ; so he has written to
the Telegraph to explain that he is in favor of the Fisher
naval defence scheme for Australia, but he wants— OK,
so badly!—“ to present a Dreadnought to the British
Government as a testimony of the loyalty and good will
of the Australian people,” and “to increase (he fight-
ing strength of the British Navy when the crisis comes
with Germany in the North Sea or English Channel.”
O’Sullivan, like all the rest of the hysterical gang, evi-
dently sees visions of a “decisive battle in the North Sea.”
Battles are never decisive unless one of the fleets or armies
engaged is utterly broken and scatlered, and the other
has a reserve of force able to follow up its success. No
one can tell before it begins how a general action
will end, but it is pretty certain that both sides will
be battered into helplessness. That applies specially
to sea-fights. It is notorious that after Tsu-shima
all the guns of the Simian fleet were unserviceable ; and if
there had been only a small Russian reserve of ships
capable of putting up a fight, the tables might have been
turned. So well is that understood, that no prudent
commander will exert his full force, either by land or sea,
against an equal, or nearly equal, enemy if he can help
it. The more skilful commander can always refuse to
accept battle on terms that do not suit him. But now
the question is: If Britain goes on building Dread-
noughts, can it man them '/ German}’, with compulsory
service, and the marine population of the Baltic and
North Sea coasts to draw on, can hardly run short of sea-
men ; but Britain, with voluntarily recruited long-service
boys in its navy, and Chows and Lascars in its merchant
ships, is in a very different position.

The Downfall of the Creed.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
While the British firms in Hankow are merely branches
of houses in Shanghai, their Chinese rivals, who have
driven them out of the import trade, are Hankow mer-
chants with branches in Shanghai. There are about 11
of these big native firms which supply the Hankow
market with British cotton goods, and which have risen
on the ruins of British competitors.—“ The Yangtze
Valley and Beyond,” by J. P. Bishop, p. 6k.
Sha-shih is the Manchester of China, the adjacent court-
try being the greatest centre pf weaving’ in the empire.
Cotton cloth and silk fabrics are the staple exports.
Enormous quantities of raw cotton arrive to be woven at
Sha-shih into a strong, durable cfoth, which I saw all over
Sze Chuan, and of which at least .20,000,0001 b. are ex-
ported annually. Samples of this make, and of English
cottons, were frequently shown to me in Sze Chuan
villages—with a scornful laugh at the expense of the
latter. ... A weaver’s wages come to about Is. 6d, per
week. Can Lancashire compete with this?— lbid., pp.
91-180.
Vkry horrible to see is this Yellow industree—
Very sad indeed for Britain, where the Cobden
fetich dwells;
Our friend the gentle Chow
Starts to manufacture now—-
He can publish twills and cottons quite as well as
dreadful smells :
Hear the Briton at Shanghai—
As his rivals hustle by,
Hear him weeping o’er the ruins of his branch
importery ;
For his Chow competitor
Passeth onward with a roar,
Strewing China with the samples of his haber-
dashery !
Strewing China with his twills,
Made in “foreign devil” mills—
Sweating Hodge in distant Bury and in Bolton
far away ;
The merchant at Hankow
Preacheth “China for the Chow,”
And his junks go trailing westward crammed
with cotton goods to-day !
Hear the cursing lao-pan on the road to Sze
Chuan—
To the distant empire province up the broad
Yangtze Kiang ;
With the products of Sha-shih—
Towing onward earnestly,
Hear the howling of the trackers in the gorges of
I-chang!
See the bagman Chow a-sweat,
Tramping forward thro’ Tibet—
Selling hats and socks in Lhasa, where the Llama
folk abide;
He is loading up the yak,
P l l ing.softgoods on its back.
For the cheapness of his fabrics spreadeth daily
far and wide !
Paying one-and-six a week —
Growing fat and very sleek.
Thus the profits flow in torrents to the yellow
cotton man ;
Far away in Lancashire,
Bull is feeling very queer,
For the Chow hath rattled Cobden in Hu-Peh
and old Yu-Nan !
Very ominous indeed is this downfall of the
Creed—
The British creed of cheapness mouthed by
Cobden years ago;
If the Chow is cheaper still,
Woe to Britain’s loom and mill—
Woe to Chorley and St. Helen’s, woe likewise to
Clitheroe!
For the Chow is on the spot,
While the Cobden folk are not—
Yea, the Chow pervadeth Asia from Chi-Li to
Turkestan ;
When he knocks at India’s door,
Startled Bull will hear the roar
Of the Chow’s protecting partner, Brother Monkey
from Japan !
So the outlook seemeth blue
For the niddering Cobden crew—
And the joss of foolish Britain waxeth wet about
the feet.
If three tanners hire the Chow
For a week, then Bull must bow—
’Tis a horrible dilemma : How can Lancashire
compete 1

The cricket .season is just leaving the
wickets, and football is about making its first
kick for goal. But the game of life goes on for
ever, and those who would score well in it and
attain the goal of their ambition should Seek
judicious assistance from D.C.L., the scholarly
brand of whisky. The amber gleam of D.O.L. is
Wisdom’s Wink to the Wise.'*
