It was defeated on a show of hands, and squelched
when the Freetraders demanded a poll. That was
in 1906. Last month there was a meeting (the
49th) of the Association of Chambers of the
United Kingdom, and it was moved : “That this
country should release itself from its present
rigid system of one-sided Freetrade by broaden-
ing the basis of taxation and reforming its fiscal
system, and thus enable itself to meet foreign
competitors on equal terms.” The hard-shell
Freetraders tried in the first place to shelve
the motion. The majority insisted on a vote,
and the Tariff Reformers scored 46 against
31, another 32 members perching themselves
on the “neutral” rail. Nothing came of the
resolution, I localise its actual supporters were not
;v two-thirds majority of the members present.
But if there are more Reformers than Con-
servative Freetraders in the Chambers of Com-
merce to-day, it seems pretty certain that Re-
form will be a straight-out policy of Protection
when it comes to pass in the House of Commons.
The almost immediate result of the great change
when it arrives will be a popular outcry that
“ Hingland is ruined,” and a dire defeat of the
Government at next election. After which Hing-
land will discover that ruin spelt salvation rather
than damnation.

Concerning a Man Named Cobden.
Ip Saint Cobden (who was certainly canonised by
the great commercial class of England about half
a century ago) could be resurrected, he would get
the shock of his life when he proceeded to read
some of the leading English publications of to-day.
With a large proportion of the upper and middle
classes, the Graphic, for example, is regarded
almost as reverentially as the Bible. Twenty
years ago this big weekly would have laughed the
laugh of A. Peacock at any proposal to depart
from the principles of Cobden, and would have
asked, indignantly, what England was coming to?
But, in its issue of February 6, this same news-
paper had a full page of scare-pictures, with
headings like these -
AN OBJECT-LESSON IN FREETRADE.
A Great British Contract that Went to Germany.
How a £109,000 Order was Lost to our Workers.
The story is an interesting one from many aspects,
and it is exactly the same sort of story, and
preaches the same truths, as several that have
appeared in The Bulletin of recent years. This
is it: —
The Loch Leven power company, of Scotland, wanted
10,000 yards of welded steel pipes, 30in. in diameter and
in 20ft. lengths. Tenders were called, and were received
from both German and English firms. The German ten-
der was slightly under that of the English firm, and was
accepted. The accepted tender amounted to £109,000.
Commenting on the transaction, the English firm
which was unsuccessful (Thos. PiGGOTT and Co.,
of Birmingham) state: “It makes us wonder
whether there may not be something in Tariff
refprm after all.” And the Graphic mournfully
adds :
What can one say of the deal, except that it resulted
in £109,000 leaving this country for Silesia (where the
German manufacturers are), when, if the same fiscal
policy were pursued here as in Germany, we should have
made these pipes in England ; and of the money paid for
them, say £25,000, or a quarter, would have gone in
wages to the working-man? As it turns out, 5 per cent.,
or some such figure, is saved by the Power company, and
95 per cent, is lost to us. This short object-lesson goes
largely to explain why there is so much unemployment
in this country, and why the British working-man, who
was, till recent years, recognised as an aristocrat among
the working classes, is to-day becoming a pariah in the
world of industry.
It is ail an incredible sort of thing; and nothing
but the bald, actual facts serve to make the
reader sure that he isn’t reading a romance.
Britain is just now straining every nerve to hold
its naval supremacy against Germany ; and it
calmly gives a £109,000 order for work, and good,
payable work, to the very nation it is competing
with, so that there may be more money in Ger-
many with which to build great ironclads for the
conquest of Britain. There is nothing more
topsy-turvy in the crazy Wonderland invented by
Lewis Carroll.

The Unspeakable Turk.
It seems to be mere waste of effort to put the new
wine of Parliamentary Government into the old,
immemorial bottle of Asia. The bottle was never
built for that kind of liquor. Japan, it is
true, struggles along with a comic-opera Parlia-
ment, but the Jap Legislature apparently sur-
vives for two special reasons : (1) hardly anybody
has a vote; (2) the august and deliberative
Monkey has no real power, the influence of the
Emperor being sufficient to decide the course of
events when the alleged law-making body threatens
to uplift its tail with a show of independence.
Persia had a Parliament for a day; then it had mur-
der and massacre ; now it has bitter and ferocious
civil war. Turkey has its second Parliament on
hand ; the first one met precariously in 1877, and
hurst in 1878. The new Turkish Parliament has
just assembled, and already a mutinous army,
doubtless sooled on by Abdul the Damned, has
been howling at its door, and another army has
come to its rescue, and it only lives by aid of the
friendly violence of armed men outside. This
Turkish Legislature was the product of the great
revolution engineered by the Committee of Union
and Progress, and it is really only the shadow
of the Committee and the Committee’s soldiery.
A section of the army- joined the revolu-
tion, for the army was ill-fed, its pay was so
far behind that even if the forces had marched
back to meet their arrears their boots would
have given out before they arrived ; and the
officers were shabby and disreputable from
sheer poverty—this while Abdul Hamid blewed
about £7,000,000 a year on his personal expenses.
When the troops rose, Abdul climbed down so
swiftly that he dropped the last 20ft. and con-
cussed himself severely. The bruise is still visible
when Abdul is in the nude. The worst tyrant
on earth became an ardent reformer. In fact, he
reformed so violently that he was almost qualified
to be a member of the Labor party. For a time
Europe took this Labor Sultan quite seriously,
and reckoned that even the incurable House of
Othman had reformed. Then while the Turkish
Parliament was busy wrestling with the intrica-
cies of its foolish job, the imperial wild animal
rushed out of his cage with a large piece of raw
meat in his mouth, and the counter-revolution
commenced. There was a horrible slaughter
of officers attached to the Reform party,
and the usual huge massacres of Christians started
in Asia Minor. The bush or outlying Turk always
massacres a lot of Christians when he gets any
surprising news from the capital. If the news is
good, he butchers many Christians ; if it is indif-
ferent, he slaughters more Christians ; if it is
bad, he wipes out most Christians. But Abdul
seemingly miscalculated his strength. To-day,
the Committee of Union and Progress seems to
be again on top, and the Parliament still lives,
and Reform is still in full blast, and the
Sultan appears to be virtually a prisoner. But
he has been allowed to live and, at time
of writing, to retain his throne—a pro-
posal to replace him by his brother, a feeble,
elderly person, blighted by 30 years’ confinement,
fell through—and anything is possible while
Abdul the Damned lives and remains on deck.
The Committee of Union and Progress might
have made things secure if it had wiped
out the House of Othman, root and branch,
man, woman and child, even to its most
distant relative, but nothing short of that is likely
to bring about permanent regeneration in Turkey.
The tyranny which certain “Christian” Powers
so long propped up at Constantinople was a fear-
some thing. Abdul Hamid was almost a world’s
record for tyranny, faithlessness, suspicion, great
villainies and petty persecution, wholesale and
retail murder, torture, extortion, and all the
other known sins. By comparison with him
the Duke of Alva was a fairly good Christian,
and Nero was something above the average. Some
day, perhaps, Europe will present this monster
of iniquity with his portrait, done in boiling oils, as
a faint expression of its views concerning him.
Yet 31 years ago Britain took a stupendous
war hazard for the purpose of propping up Abdul
Hamid’s power; those were the days when
Benjamin, the Horrible Hebrew, was Prime
Minister, and under his guidance Britain accepted
Cyprus as a bribe, and in return it saved Mace-
donia to the Sultan—in consequence of which
some hundreds of thousands of butchered folk lie
dead at Britain’s door—and then Benjamin the
Bought came back to London, and waved his plug
hat at the multitude, and said that he had brought
peace with honor. And Britain has propped up
Abdul ever since, and talked a good deal about
his possible reformation. There are rumors that
even the Devil has an occasional inclination to
mend his ways and become a respectable house-
holder, and possibly rise to the dignity of church-
warden ; but no such report has ever circulated as
regards the curious Iniquity who climbed over a
corpse and a lunatic to attain the Sultan’s billet,
and who has been climbing over corpses ever
since. And many of these corpses—the remains
of suspected reformers who had died horribly
after days and weeks of torture—were dreadful to
look upon. The sight of them was calculated to
put even a strong man off his food.for a week.

“Australian” writes: According to S. M.
Herald (20/4/’O9), one Coulls, delegate from the
Barrier (N.S.W.) branch of the A.M. A. to the
recent Sydney Labor Congress, spoke thus con-
cerning universal military service :
When this country becomes fit to defend I will be pre-
pared to defend it. I’d sooner be shot in the back than
be compelled to defend it as it is. My troubles if any
nation comes to Australia, to capture it. I don’t care
even if the Japanese take it. That is because we couldn’t
live under worse conditions if any other country owned
Australia (dissent). I repeat that this country has not
treated me so well that I am going to take up arms to
defend it.
It is indeed a grievous pity that the Barrier
miners allow themselves to be misrepresented by
persons of the T. Mann and Coulls species. The
individual who is willing, as Coulls professes to
be, that bis wife and daughters, and his mates’
wives and daughters, should become the play-
things of a horde of conquering Asiatics seems
hardly human. He may be a man, but the evidence
is all to the contrary. And there issomething pathet-
ically ignorant and childish about the cry of the
individual who says that conquest doesn’t matter,
for “we couldn’t live under worse conditions”
than in Australia. Broken Hill may be bad
enough, but it is hardly even the top storey of
Hell; there are plenty of possible conquerors
whose rule would make a man speedily regret
that he hadn’t died in the last ditch, even in
defence of a galvanised-iron, drought-stricken
shanty at the Barrier. The people of N.S.W.,
and in a Federal sense the people of Australia,
have complete self-government, and the only
possible meaning of the Coulls attitude is that
self-government is a failure now, and is always
going to be a failure, and that a foreign auto-
cracy is preferable. It is a lamentable attitude,
and for the credit of the Labor movement it is a
pity that Mr. Coulls wasn’t immediately pre-
sented with his railway ticket for home, and
urged to take his pro-Jap opinions off the
premises, and informed that a white man’s union
could do without him in future.
The Yictorian Public Accounts Committee
has reported, in effect, that Thomas Bent got his
dissolution of Parliament last year by falsehood,
inasmuch as the Governor granted the dissolution
o.n Bent’s assurance that he had enough money to
carry on with, and the said Bent hadn’t enough
money. Bent says he gave the assurance under
the impression that he could lawfully use certain
cash balances, and the Committee says almost in
so many words that it doesn’t believe him.
Therefore, the Committee recommends that any
future Minister who follows in Bent’s huge foot-
steps should be disqualified for life from holding
office, and that any officer who signs orders for
payments not authorised by Parliament, as Bent’s
officer did at Bent’s instigation, should be liable
to gaol. Meanwhile, it seems that nothing is
going to happen to Bent.

“ In the Ranks ” was revived at Sydney
Palace last Saturday night. Either the play has
changed or the writer has undergone an alteration
since he first saw it at London Adelphi a matter
of 15 years ago. Then it seemed to him a faithful
reproduction of contemporary life. To-day this—
the masterpiece of G. R. Sims—fails to stir in him
one responsive chord. It has been modernised
and otherwise hacked about by the present pro-
ducer and his fellow-actors. Many a superfluous
jest and banal patriotic outburst is there that was
not there before. But, anyhow, the drama is a
poor one, acceptable only to extreme youth or
ultra-Dreadnoughtism.
Ned Drayton is militantly upright, despite
the fact that his father was a forger. Colonel
Wynter, rich and good-hearted but obtuse to the
verge of idiocy, is Ned’s guardian and benefactor.
He quarrels with Ned over the latter’s resolve to
marry Ruth Herrick. This is where Gideon
Blake, villain, comes in. Later, Gideon walks
off arm-in-arm with the Colonel, and Ned’s
chances of inheriting the aged soldier’s estates
retire to an outside price in the betting. Enter
Richard Belton, a bald-headed tramp, who claims
to be Ned’s father, and “ proves it” by producing
a letter (picked up “in Australia”) in the hand-
writing of Ned’s deceased mother. Ned reels at
great length with horror, and arranges to meet
his awful-looking and bogus parent in Dingley
Wood that night. As it happens, a band of
poachers has arranged to operate in Dingley
Wood at the time specified. An oppor-
tunity is afforded the management to pre-
sent a picture of poaching as practised in the
Cold Country. The poachers slink on with
Lee-Metford rifles, and reconnoitre nervously.
They then retire out of sight and proceed to poach.
Ear-splitting detonations are heard, followed by
the crashing to earth of heavy objects—pre-
sumably pheasants, but apparently condors by
the noise. It suggests to the onlooker
that Britain’s poaching system is on a
par with its fiscal one. Colonel Wynter,
owner of the bombarded poultry, appears. Belton
follows, and, with the only bona-fide fowling-
piece in the company, shoots the Colonel in the
neighborhood of the midriff. As the old man lies
weltering behind a stump, Ned steps forward and
hands Belton a bundle of notes. Even as the
stakes change hands Gideon Blake arrives, and
accuses the totally innocent Ned of instigating
the assassination of his benefactor. And so Ned
gets six months for assault, and emerges from
gaol a disgraced man, and is, in consequence,
driven to take the shilling and begin life
anew as a “ranker.” Act 111. presents
Ruth’s lodgings. Ruth has married Ned prior
to his trouble, and is shown expiating her foolish-
ness in the all-black garb of penury. Belton —
still bald—blows in, borrows a sovereign, departs,
returns very drunk, drugs Ruth’s tea, and is leav-
ing when Gideon eventuates. Belton confides to
Gideon that he (Belton) is not really Ned’s father.
Gideon hardly has time to observe “Ha!” or
words to that effect, when Ruth, who has heard
all, totters up-stage, and in a doped tone of voice
says “Thank God ! ” Belton straightway falls on
her, but hereabouts Ned dashes in. The rest of
the play is mitigated by a stirring scrap under
scratcb-as-cats-can rules between Ned and Gideon,
during which the combatants rollsix times towards
the O.P. wings, and then reverse and roll six
times in the Prompt direction ; also, by an escape
from a guard-room, and by alarms and excursions
in a barrack square. The end of Act Y. allures
the entire company to the lodgings of Ruth. The
first to materialise is Ned, fresh from the barrack
cells, and obsessed with a characteristically insane
project of fleeing the country with Ruth. Gideon
is the next entrant. While threatening Ned with
divers penalties, he is confounded by the appear-
ance of the Colonel, looking fit and well in a frock-
coat and gibus bat. Hardly has the Colonel
finished tongue-lashing Gideon when the British
army surges into the room. The captain of it is
all for Ned. He mentions that the latter’s dis-
charge has been bought. “ But,” he adds warmly,
“You are too good a man to be lost to the ser-
vice. I hope to grasp your hand shortly in the
capacity of a fellow-officer.” Enthusiasm runs
high as the Colonel notifies that he has “ used his
influence” (in some direction unspecified), and
that, as a result, Ned will be given a commission
immediately.
* * * *
Ifc is hard to say a kindly and at the same
time honest word concerning the acting in this
play. The two characters that most nearly
resemble human beings are the Captain Holcroft
of J. L. Lawrence and the Barbara Herrick of
Miss Rosemary Rees. George Cross (Ned Dray-
ton) and Miss Ada Guildford (Ruth) win applause
in virtuous rhles. The Gideon Blake of Wilton
Power is an undesirable whom we have met before
in a similar environment, and the same remark
applies to the Belton of Johnson Weir. As Joe
Buzzard, Wilton Welch supplies a mass of per-
fectly lamentable comic relief. Miss Maud Chet-
wynd is quaint as Mrs. Timmany, and others of the
minor characters are diverting.

Sydney Palace, on Saturday afternoon,
May 1, sees the first performance by the Play-
goers’ Club, of a new Australian play by J. 0. Lee,
entitled “Out on the Oastlereagh.” There is an
agreeable suggestion of the vast wilderness in the
title; and if the comedy-drama lives up to its
name, it should be a fair-sized success. The box
plan is open at Paling’s.

Time is rubbing the pristine gloss off “ An
Englishman’s Home” at Sydney Royal, and
in another ten days the building will be dis-
mantled and removed to Melbourne. Mean-
while, the shells of the foreigners remain in good
bursting order, and Mr. Brown continues to go
mad, and engage, on his own, the entire invading
army, amidst loud applause; nor do the British
volunteers omit to turn up opportunely in the
last act, and delight all but chronic Dingoes by
pointing their rifles at Prince Yoland and his
minions, thus conclusively proving the truth of
the old adage that Britons never shall be slaves.
Those who don’t want to be utterly out of the
conversational hunt at suburban tea-rallies during
the next few weeks will struggle forward and
view this play ere yet it is too late.
*# * *
A “curtain-raiser” is being rehearsed at
Sydney Royal. This is a brand of play that has
fallen into disuse here of late years. It is
a necessity in London, where food comes first
and the drama a bad second ; and stalls
and dress circle frequenters don’t start dinner
till the hour the average Australian play begins.
It has become a temporary necessity in this city
owing to the short dramatic measure that is sup-
plied by “An Englishman’s Home.” The public
has started muttering that it isn’t getting full
value for its money in this brief work, and when
the public takes that attitude it is as advisable
for a theatrical manager to placate it as it is for
the wanderer on the steppes of Russia to throw
his creditor (provided the latter is handy) to the
wolves that pervade those snow-clad regions.
* X st?
“10-2-4”: Sydney Telegraph, in a
second notice of “An Englishman’s Home,”
uses these uninformed words :
The extraordinary antics of the volunteer company
excite unrestrained mirth. The fact is, as has been
already pointed out, that the author has mistaken
caricature for satire, and has in consequence overshot the
mark. If, as has been urged, he meant to emphasise a
system of incompetency in drawing this picture, he has
ignored an elementary principle of dramatic art—namely,
that in aiming the barbed shaft of satire one should not
distort facts. This is a plain rule of controversy. The
British volunteer company at the Theatre Royal is merely
a group of helpless idiots w'ho cause derisive laughter.
There has surely been plenty of room for satirical com.
ment in the muddle of the British military operations,
as exemplified in the South African war, for instance,
without any necessity to rush wildly to the other extreme.
Such a caricature as Major du Maurier has contrived in
this scene of “An Englishman’s Home” might be all right
if the piece were burlesque, but, unfortunately, given a
place in serious drama, it detracts, to some extent, from
the value of the lesson he very properly wishes to convey.
To anyone who has met the British volunteer
personally, du Maurier’s picture appeals as in no
way overdrawn. It was accepted in England,
and it is still being accepted there, as a faithful
representation of a widely-known type. The
writer knew intimately, during three years
which he spent “eating dinners” at the
Inner Temple, London, members of a legal
(and therefore, presumably, more than ordinarily
brainy) brigade of volunteers. They were
capable enough at their own job which
was the law. But in the capacity of handy
men out of doors they were every whit as hope-
less as Captain Finch’s warriors now showing at
Sydney Royal. Two of these legal volunteers
stayed in the same house with me in the country
once. I found in the course of three days that
they could not harness a horse, and, in fact, were
hopelessly bewildered when faced with the
problem of putting a bridle on the animal’s head.
Neither of them had ever slept in the open in his
life. Neither was competent to cook a chop, to
build a presentable fire, to ride the quietest hack,
or to kill flesh, fish or fowl, unless the same
stepped forward, held out its head, and fairly
clamored for the happy despatch. This state of
things is pretty well universal among young men
of the British “middle class ” —the class, that is,
from which the volunteer must needs be drawn.
There is no analogy between his type and the
Australian, for the reason that practically every
Australian, no matter what his position in life,
habitually leads the out-door existence which
alone can give the non-regular soldier that self-
reliance which is required in the essentially out-
door game of war. Prominent Englishmen don’t
hesitate to acknowledge the utter uselessness of
the volunteer system. For example, an issue
of the London Daily Mail to hand last week
contained this: —
An influential meeting of the employers of London,
held at Harrods’ Stores yesterday, in support of the Terri-
torial Army movement, was enlivened by one or two
entertaining stories.
“ I am an old Volunteer," said Sir Melvill Beachcroft,
the chairman of the London County Council; “and I
have seen active service. The Fenian scare was on. I,
with some others, was set to protect the armory at Wool-
wich, on which the Fenians were supposed to have
designs. I had a rifle and several rounds of ball cart-
ridge.
“On a hedge near-by I espied the figure of a man. I
challenged him. No reply. I challenged him again.
Still no reply. I fired. The bullet grazed his cheek. He
got up then—and I saw that it was my captain, come to
see if I was doing my duty. Those "(added Sir Melvill,
provoking a roar of delighted laughter) “ were days of
enthusiasm in the Volunteer cause !"
Sir Edward Clarke, K.C., also became autobiographical.
“For twenty years I was in the Volunteers. I was
always a full private, more or less efficient. We went for
long marches, principally by train, to Wimbledon Com-
mon, and we fought sham fights at Nevvhaven. Although
we had our uses, I should not like to claim that we were
efficient according to modern notions.”
The British regular is still, in all likelihood, as
good a soldier as ever he was (which is to say he
is a very good soldier indeed) ; but the volunteer
is a howling joke, pure and simple ; and none but
papers like the Telegraph, which haven’t met
him, and live 12,000 miles distant from his
idiocies, dream of saying otherwise.
* * *
At Her Majesty’s (Sydney) “ Jack and
Jill ” still persist in their unscientific quest of
the element which is so scarce at the Canberra
capital site. In most places where people have to
go up hills and to other remote spots after water
typhoid is prevalent. Not so at Her Majesty’s.
Patrons will be glad to know that this resort has
a clean bill of health. Furthermore, the home of
Jack and Jill is a veritable riot of song and dance,
and leg and color and assault-and-battery humor.
“ Jack and Jill ” and the place they inhabit are
circumstances that should be encountered by all
citizens, other than Methody divines and deaf
and blind subjects, not once merely, but several
times.

Public interest; in the doings of “ The
Hypocrites,” at Sydney Criterion, does not wane.
The show has been running a month, and play-
goers still crowd to it in shoals, platoons and the
like, and indicate their approval in the usual
manner. An encouraging sign this, for indeed,
take it for all in all, “The Hypocrites” is about
the worthiest and best-acted dramatic effort that
Sydney has been offered in recent years..

The motor-theatrical-tour is a new thing: in England.
H. A. Jones’ play, “ Dolly Reforming Herself,” is running
round the “ provinces,” the whole company being carried
in a motor saloon car. Only one performance is given at
each town ; then the motor says “ toot,” and the show
hurries on its way with hope in front and a smell behind.
The Bulletin lias been sent an advance copy of
the Official Year Book of Australia for 1909. An
amazinv amount of valuable information is crammed into
its 1100 > and odd pages. Statistics are furnished for the
whole period of Australian settlement, viz., from 1788 to
190 S ; and for the years since Federation (1901-1908), de-
tailed figures are provided. Special articles deal well and
interestingly with such urgent subjects as the exploration
of Australia, the development of Australia’s trade with
Asia, the Customs tariff, and many more that the person in
the street and business house and office wants to have made
available in a form that admits of prompt reference.
There are ways in which this Year. Book stands head and
shoulders above any similar production in the world to-
day. Neither the United States nor the United Kingdom
possess such a work, and their statistics have to be chased
through a multitude of volumes. In some cases, too, the
chase is quite fruitless. For instance, so slipshod are the
statistics of the United States that there is no official
record of births, marriages and deaths for the whole
country, and the population estimates, except in the
census year, are mere guesswork. In fact, outside Aus-
tralia Private Enterprise mostly supplies the different
nations with the hard facts about themselves that they
need to know. This Australian Year Book is a sample of
what an enlightened up-to-date Government can do in
that line, and a better advertisement for Socialism could
hardly be imagined.*

WILLIAM FLEMING, Equitable Building, Sydney.
Playgoers who are particular about the
quality of the goods are promised something up
to professional standard at Sydney Criterion to-
morrow in the matinee performance of Rostand s
famous play, “ The Fantasticks,” by the Sydney
Stage Society. Madge Mclntosh, who will play
Sylvette, created the part on the London premiere
of this romantic and humorous work. Special
attention is being paid to the accessories of music
and effects, upon which the atmosphere of this
dainty drama so much depends.
** * *
The Welsh Male Choristers are going
melodiously and financially well through a
second season at Sydney Centenary Hall. The
units of this musical army are trained to a finish,
and the gentleman with the taffyish name of
William Thomas, who is responsible for their
unity and discipline, deserves medals on his chest.
Taffyland’s National Anthem, “The March of
the Men of Harlech,” part songs and popular
gems from operas, put the choristers and audience
on good terms at Saturday’s opening concert. A
change of programme nightly adds to the attract-
iveness of the season. Paling’s display the plan
for taking advantageous positions.
** * *
Bengough, the Canadian cartoonist, will
open his Sydney season at Sydney Centenary
Hall, York-street, on May Ist. Bengough is
said to combine rapidity of work with startling
realism, and to have the saving grace of humor (a
thing most cartoonists are woefully deficient in)
largely developed. His entertainment will con-
sist of a lecturette on “The Artfulness of Things
in General,” illustrated with playful sketches
that will show the advantage of possessing the
open eye in order to realise the unsuspected fun
that lurks in mathematics and other dry subjects.
Altogether, according to the advertisement,
Bengough is an entertainer out of the general
class of sketch artists.
** * *
Miss Millie Hughes, a soprano bird whose
warble is being trained by Mr. George Grimm,
will vocalise for the public on Friday next (April
30) at the Sydney Y.M.C. A. Hall. A group of
well-known artists will assist. Plan at Paling’s.
Also, and furthermore, Mr. Vincenzio de
Giorgio is a pianist of note who has
recently come to reside in Sydney. As a
foreblast of moderate fanfare he will give an
invitation musical recital on Thursday evening
next in the Y.M.C.A. Hall. His biggest piano
morsel will be Beethoven’s gigantic concerto in
C minor. Likewise, to continue the list of musi-
cal events, Slapoffski’s operatic proceedings are
coming along, and Sydney will shortly be treated
to a handsome production of Verdi’s tragic “Aida”
and Don zetti’s wailingly romantic “Lucia,” in
the Town Hall. Mrs. Slap, heads the women solo-
ists, and Andrew Black and Orme Darvall the male
contingent. These grand operas, with a chorus
of 200 and orchestra of 60, will be more than a
novelty to Sydney.
** * *
Wirfch I3ros.’ Circus is still booming along
in George-street West (Sydney), and ringing fre-
quent changes of programme on its patrons. It
takes a lot of ingenuity to show the lion and the
tiger and the bear in new r6les, but somehow the
thing is done. .Recently there was an unre-
hearsed comedy act (from the standpoint of
the bear and not the elephant). The useful
Indian beast of burden was standing with
his coat-tail brushing against the bear’s
cage, and his appendage waved gently to the
rhythm of the band. The bear became
interested. He protruded one soft paw, and,
catching the tail on the swing, gently drew it
into the cage, examined it, and then tried a bite.
A scream of anguish,from the elephant did not dis-
turb him. He purred soothingly, and went for the
tail still more. Apparently an elephant’s tail is a
tender part* and during the few minutes that
elapsed before the hot iron was applied to the
bear’s nose to make him release his hold, he made
quite a course. Now that elephant appears
with his tail in a sling; and the night writer
was there he heard one wise bumpkin explain-
ing to another bumpkin who was not so wise that
that was the spot where the ivory grew ; and he
bitterly denounced the cruelty and rapacity
of the Wirths for extracting ivory from
the living elephant. One of the finest circus
turns writer has ever seen is the “Diavolo”
musical act. The bull fight has been worked up
to a high pitch of perfection ; and the spectacle of
the small dog that, done up like a bull, tries to
bellow like one before he charges the trembling
toreador is a sight never to be forgotten. As
said before, the circus is quite up to the high
standard set by its proprietors. The only thing
that is not up to the standard is the band. It
has an imported sound about it.
** * *
Chung Ling Soo still holds the centre of
the Sydney Tivoli stage, and the rest of the shovy
goes on beneath his mighty shadow. Mere
singers and dancers are very under-sized spuds
alongside the silent miracle-worker. The part of
the entertainment that isn’t Chung is contributed
mostly by Tom Wootwell, the two Bells, Cull Pitt,
Irving Sayles, Baby Watson, and the two Prices.
Yeotha and Captain Grade are also among those
present. On Friday (May 7) a matinee is to be
given in aid of the distressed Dreadnought Fund.
** * *
On seeing “The Sign of the Cross ” for
the nth time—it has been running for a profitable
week at Adelaide Royal—one is struck most by
the skill with which the author has presented the
character of Mercia. She is not on at all in five
of the play’s 10 scenes. She is talked about much
more than she talks ; it must be One of the easiest
leading-lady parts on record—to learn. Yet it is
only on analysis that this is discovered, for she
seems to pervade the play, and fairly shares its
interest with Marcus, who has the centre of the
stage in eight scenes. In a strong cast perhaps
the most notable are Winter Hall—a fine
Moorish-looking Tigellinus—and Percy Walshe,
who makes Nero more of a Caesar and less of
a derelict from the Inebriate Retreat than is
usual. As for Julius Knight, he is always sound
and commanding. One takes him for granted,
which is, after all, the highest compliment. In
the Martin Harvey cavalier-drama, “ The Breed
of the Treshams,” he has another big chance as a
rather battered Bad-Man-with-some-good-in-him-
after-all—easily the most popular type of charac-
ter in fiction or drama. The Cherniavskis are
again at the Town Hall. They are fine musicians,
and even their affectations give pleasure to a
large section of the public, and pfesumably to
themselves; so why complain ? At the Tivoli,
two Japanese experts are showing (to adapt
Ichabod Bronson) what wrestling would look like
if it didn’t look like the way it does look.
“Diana of Dobson’s,” produced at Her
Majesty’s on Saturday, is both new and fresh to
Melbourne. Of manyromanticcomedies of English
“class” life and London senciment, which have,
or haven’t flourished in the foreign atmosphere of
Australian theatres, “ Diana ” is perhaps the most
originally and whimsically humorous. Also, in
the humor of its romance and socialistic sugges-
tions, it is charmingly feminine. It is the work of
an authoress (Cicely Hamilton) who is a practical
suffragette, and a student of shop-girl character
in a city where the term “sleeping in
is generally understood, and “sleeping out” is
a recognised penalty of poverty. Apart from
Diana, the first act presents five different types
of sleepers-in at Dobson’s, and although they aie
not all of them as convincingly presented as they
were in London under Lena Ashwell’s manage-
ment —the writer speaks from personal recollec-
tion of the scene—and the girl in the pmk
“nighty” doesn’t rise from her undergarments on
her bed as she did at the Kingsway Theatre, and
the exceeding width of the stage weakens the
illusion, the first act of “ Diana ” at Her Majesty’s
is nevertheless a very forcible piece of feminine
low comedy which sets the house roaring. It was
characteristic of the woman playwright that her
first care was to give the boys a treat in a picture
of girly dormitory life. In the Melbourne per-
formance of this scene the best sample of typemg
is submitted by Miss Edith Lewis as Miss
Smithers.
* * * *
The plot of the comedy is outlined in the
foregoing jingle. The entertainment is mostly in
the light didactic dialogue, and the three contrast-
ing phases of the extraordinary Diana rather
mar the characterisation. Only two persons beside
Victor Bretherton are seriously concerned with
the career of Diana during her fleeting hour of
luxury in Switzerland. Mrs. Brough, who
got a great welcome on Saturday, plays the
orthodox matchmaking matron with more
ease than subtlety; whilst Gregan McMahon
cleverly gives an air of truth to his
sketch' of the familiar titled shopkeeper and
vulgarian, a character that is commonly played on
extreme farcical lines. Thomas Kingston is the
chappie who suffers the rude awakening, and
changes from a professional fortune-hunter into
an amateur “ cab-chaser.” His is a likeable,
though feeble performance, marred in its effect by
an atrocious tweed suit. The plot demands that
he shall be chappily tailored for the enchantment
of the supposed widow, yet the suit he urges
must strike her as being worth about o2s. bd., in-
cluding an extra pair of pants. As regards
clothes, Miss Brune also is somewhat at fault m
the final scene, where her.outfit suggests that
she might have paid another week s ren.t
with it before getting ejected from her
lodging. Otherwise her playing of Diana—the
very British antithesis of “ Sunday ” and “ The
Girl of the Golden West ” —is a revelation of un-
expected restraint in the acting of Miss Tittell
Brune. True, she could do with a further in-
fusion of reposeful manner, nor is she less artificial
than usual in her lapses into sentimentality, nor
does she even nearly attain the dramatic height
of a Lena Ashwell when she scorns her Gussie,
but she is often remarkably effective in situations
where a keen dramatic sense of the character is
as necessary as a talent for stage business. Alto-
gether “ Diana of Dobson’s ” is a most interesting
show.

At Melbourne Princess’s, “Sweet Kitty
Bellairs” still pursues the sunlit path of effective
rendition and elegant drapery; and, as antici-
pated, is proving a most effec-
tive medium for the Nellie
Stewart boom which play-
goers could feel was in the
air. It is here now. The
Old World atmosphere of
beautiful but wicked
Bath, and the gorgeous
clothes thereof, have
taken quite 20 years off
the age of Melbourne’s
old favorite; and the
playgoer who sees her
after long separation,
arrives there to find
himself back in an at-
mosphere of long ago.
The atmosphere of long
ago is always effective
when it is set off by
good clothes and an
artist of Nellie Stewart’s
consummate skill. The
play may not be truth-
ful, and may make holes
in the history of morals ;
but that doesn’t matter.
It creates an atmosphere
of charm and beauty,
which should be the chief
end of all drama.

Royal is in its last phase,

for the last time, tied

book, the co. will
steal away to the
Rickety Isles, where
Joe Ward holds
high carnival while
his credit lasts.
At Melbourne
King’s the glori-
fied, not to say
canonised, edi-
tion of Viscount
Nelson is still
shimmering in
its moral loveli-
ness, and causing
EDWIN BRETT as the
Musical Comedy Colonel ,
Melbourne Royal.
people who see nothing but change and degenera-
tion around them to remark that only the good
can fight. Anderson has never been averse to
putting a streak of horror into his bellovvdramas,
and The Bulletin would suggest that when the
last week arrives the drama should be improved
more in accordance with the actual facts of the case.
Two fine, spectacular scenes might be introduced.
One would be Lady Hamilton’s well-known night
dance on the inverted punchbowl, with Nelson,
full to the neck of the original contents of the
bowl, gazing admiringly, as he used to do, through
the clouds of tobacco smoke at the rather plump
dancer, who was usually garbed only in her hair for
that act. The other tableau might represent that
weird scene in which the dead bodies of the
butchered Neapolitan patriots followed in the
wake of Nelson’s flagship, and nearly drove that
cold, determined man to drink. Altogether, it
seems to the writer that the author of “ The
Mariners of England ” has not made as much as
he might of such excellent material.

DIANA OF DOBSON’S.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Among 1 the slaves at Dobson’s (“ cutting ” draper, he, and
sweater)
Who slept upon the premises in dormitories bleak
Was Miss Diana Massingberd, who dreamed of something
better
Than a dreary life of toiling hard for two half-crowns a
week.
Now there came to Miss Diana in the ordinary manner
A formal lawyer’s letter, then she tilted high her chin,
And her world grew bright and sunny, and it flowed with
milk and honey,
For she’d got a bit of money, and a mind to “do it in.”
She bought some pretty dresses, like the folk of rank and
fashion,
Did Diana (late of Dobson’s) when she passed a month
abroad
As a wealthy widow lady ; and she raised a tender passion
In the heart of Victor Bretherton, the offspring of a
lord.
But when the month was ended, and her money all
expended,
And she told the languid chappie that she wasn’t worth
a cent,
He was glad he’d not embrac’t her, so she scorned him as
a waster,
And, mockingat his weakness, back to poverty she went.
Then Victor, thinking sadly of the girl who spoofed him
gladly,
Formed a sudden resolution to go working for a while,
With the hope that he might show her—for he loved
Diana badly—
That a toff can earn his living in a common manly style.
But he rarely earned a tanner ; it was even as Diana
Had assured him ere they parted at the Engadine Hotel.
On a bench by London’s river, where the night wind sent
a shiver
Through his liver, he was dozing when a funny thing
befell.
Diana, faint and footsore, came and roosted there beside
him ;
They recognised each other, and the bitter truth he toid
That he couldn’t earn a living ; but she didn’t now deride
him,
She let him squeeze her gently, for the night was verj T
cold.
He discussed his “ paltry income’’from the standpoint
of the gutter,
Though to marry not for money he had formerly been
loth ;
And her heart began to flutter when she heard her hero
mutter :
“Six hundred pounds per annum will suffice to keep
us both.”
With a bob the “copper” lent him, Victor bought some
bread and butter ;
O’er a loving cup of coffee, from the stall, they plighted
troth.
“A very far-fetched story,” says the critical fault-finder.
“ A comedy of morals,” says the writer of this rhyme.
If the rebel woman toiler left her wretchedness behind
her,
And played at wasting money for a gorgeous little time,
She was wiser in her folly than the creatures melancholy
Who slave and save their pennies till their sordid
course is run.
In the dull and cheerless present one may distil odors
pleasant
From the treasured recollection of a gambol in the sun.
And the idle, feeble chappie, with a chappie’s one am-
bition :
Shut out from honest sympathy for such as earn their
bread:
He learns from Miss Diana that a man in his position
Isn’t bound to be dependent on the woman he may wed.
There is matter in the story for each Democrat and Tory
Of ordinary intellect to swallow and digest—
For the sinner and the kirker, and the waster and the
worker —
In this tale of how Diana “ blewed ” three-hundred of
the best.
VICTORIA.
O.K.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“ Sceneshifter” : “An Englishman’s Home,” if it does
nothing else, cruelly caricatures the volunteers of old
England. Some years ago (in 1897), when Harry Connor
was here with “ A Trip to Chinatown,” he put up another
piece called “ A Milk White Flag.” It was a very power-
ful satire on the weak points in Uncle Sam’s State militia.
The author, Henry Hoyt, held up to public ridicule the
“ clubbable ” phase of militia life on a strictly peace
footing. It was alleged that men really joined for the
pleasure of belonging to a fast club, and in order to get
among a lot of gamblers and be privileged to live on
fashionable principles in a whirl of cards and decanters.
The gorgeous uniforms, as compared with the plain clo’
of the regular soldiery, were bitterly emphasised, as was
also the amazing preponderance of officers over privates.
In fact, there was only one private, and he was the drink-
waiter. The officers were in scores and hundreds, and the
club had medical certificates ready for the whole lot of
them in case of war, declaring their inability to rush to
the field of battle. They all had fatal diseases, and some
of them had nine diseases. The only individual for whom
no medical certificate was kept ready was the private, or
drink-waiter. When the General presented these troops
with a white flag he told them it was one they should
never desert, “ even on the field of battle.” And they
said they wouldn’t. This cruel satire raised Cain in the
States, but nevertheless the piece was a great money
maker there. The joke was lost on this side of the
Pacific.
“ J.” : “An Englishman’s Home ” moves elsewhere
after next week. It hasn’t boomed in Sydney to any
extent. I think one of the main reasons is that the great
majority in every audience doesn’t understand it. Half
the allusions are caviare to those who haven’t lived in the
Cold Land, and a goodly proportion of the jests are not
accepted as such in this country because they deal with
matters of (to us) ordinary everyday occurrence. Take,
for instance, the familiar relations that are shown to exist
between volunteer Captain Finch and his men. That
would be regarded as screamingly funny in England, Ger-
many or Austria, where service discipline causes the officer
and the private to stand on different planes. Here where,
for the most part, no such distinction exists, the onlooker
finds it hard to grasp what the dramatist is driving at.
The satire contained in the mania of Geoff, and his.
friends for “ sport ” cuts no ice amongst us. Ninety per
cent, of Sydney playgoers would condemn the man as a
crank, a Socialist, and a speaker of subversive doctrines
who dared to decry cricket, racing or football, and the
amazing local interest that is taken in them. Above all,
Australians can’t accept the perfectly sincere picture du
Maurier has drawn of the British volunteer. They are
unable to believe that this much-boomed warrior is as
incapable as he is painted. Actually one of the
morning dailies ascribed du Maurier’s work in
this regard to the regular soldier’s “jealousy”
of his volunteer brother-in-arms—as who should
talk of Jack Johnson’s “ jealousy ” of the ban-
tam champion of the Undertow Beach Swimming
Club. In searching for a good word to give “An English-
man’s Home” last Saturday, Sydney Telegraph saidl'
“The Sydney audiences are laughing, forgetting that it it
meant to teach a bitter truth. However, Brown’s defend
of Myrtle Villa in the last act captures them, so perhaps
in the long run the play may have a sobering influence
upon the careless, pleasure-seeking Australian.” Surely,
in itself, an awful instance of incomprehension. Brown’s
coat-removing act hits the gallery where it lives, and
thus fulfils the object for which it was introduced ; but it
is the frankest sort of claptrap and humbug. Barring
the above, about the only genuinely rapturous “ hand ”
of the evening occurs when Finch says “ Damn. ’ But
then the tremendous and gratifying effect of the word
“ Damn ” on the stage is sure wherever Shakspeare’s
tongue is spoken.
“ Snell ” : I wish I could sell hams, or jams, or Ameri-
can boots as these theatrical managers can retail their
wares to a humble public. People who wanted to see
Nellie Stewart on April 10 prowled into town in the dark,
damp, drowsy hours of early morning a fortnight pre-
vious and stood sound a Collins-street door like a
drove of intimidated sheep till coupons were dealt
out to them. Then they stood round the door
ao-ain in a second version of the subdued and
simple jumbuck, till permitted to enter and realise on
their coupons. At which point, after hours of weariness
and waiting, about half of them found there was nothing
to realise, because all the sitting, standing and hanging-
on accommodation was already mopped up. Now', if the
public would only fall out of bed to rush for coupons,
enabling it to purchase my splendid poems, “ Crown Svo,
cloth gilt, gilt top, 55.,” it would go far to restore my
faith in the sanity of man.
The Bulletin’s Adelaide dramatic critic writes : “ Har-
rison O.” (in B. 15/4/’O9) has “caught me bending’’—to
use the latest London phrase for getting one in a
dilemma. He quotes with approval my remark that
“ The Girl of the Golden West ” has “ the most unneces-
sary fourth act ever seen on any stage ”; and then he
ones on to describe that act as containing the cowboy
kindergarten scene. But that is the third act at least,
it was ?n Adelaide, and the play is not likely to have been
structurally changed since. It is a long and important
act, foolish as the comic relief part of it may be, and the
play couldn’t exist without it. What I deplored w 7 as the
final act, five minutes of anti-climax, showing the hero
and heroine alone on the plains. “ Harrison O.’s” com-
pliment must therefore be regretfully returned to him.
Meanwhile, let me add to the collection of unnecessary
climaxes the king of them all. It is what naturalists call
a “sport.” It only happened once; still, it happened.
The Julius Knight co. was going to play “ The Darling of
the Gods” in Adelaide, but something went wrong and it
couldn’t be done. Yet the great Apotheosis scenery was
on hand, and it seemed a shame to. waste it. So, after
Marcus and Mercia had gone to the lions, they turned up
again in Cloudland, spectacularly reunited—they who
had never parted, unless they were eaten by different
lions—in a Japanese heaven 1
LINES INSPIRED BY A HORRIBLE RUMOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The continued success of “An Englishman’s Home at
the Theatre Royal, Sydney, has led the J. C. Williamson
management to send Mr. Priestly Morrison, the producer,
to Adelaide, to rehearse the Julius Knight Company for
the piece, which will be played in Perth by that organisa-
tion. Mr. Knight will appear as Brown. —News item.
Ah no ! Not that, my Julius.
Don’t do it, I implore.
Why should you ? Fine “ fat ” parts exist
Everywhere, by the score —
R6les that would give your beauty scope :
They’re to be found galore.
I could not stand your classic face
Rimmed with the whiskerings
That Mr. “Myrtle Villa” Brown
Affects. It anguish brings
To one who holds that you should sport
Not whiskerings, but wings.
I sit and brood how .you will look
If you decide to wear
That dreadful coat which Brown tears off.
His tie, his lack of hair —
And, Julius, the grief I feel
Is more than I can bear.
For years I’ve watched you, sword in hand,
Lay hefty foemen low ;
None fought more recklessly than 3'ou,
None struck a shrewder blow.
And shall those arms, that brain, descend
To learn diabolo ?
Shall you who loomed above the herd
Of mummers on the stage,
So that you seemed like some 3'oung god
Turn callously the page
Of youth’s bright lexicon, and don
The dun, drab garb of age ?
Pray Heaven not 1 There are some things
That set one’s teeth on edge.
(Reid in grave mood or Cook in gay
Are instances); I pledge
My word that you—if you play Brown-
Will do a sacrilege.
N.S.W.

SOCIETY
After all, Providence is good—sometimes.
A bright example of this fact was in evidence on
Thursday morning last week, when a ferocious
fire gutted the Post Office stores in George-street,
Sydney. The premises are in practically the
same building as The Bulletin office, and
separated only Il3’ a brick wall, built in alcoves.
The stores were packed with clothing, insulating
material, sealing wax, much stationery, and many
highly inflammable chemicals ; and the four top
storeys of the building were a solid furnace. The
dividing wall in several places cracked under
the influence of the fervid heat; but the
Fire Brigade, with splendid .courage and
fine skill, made a good save, and con-
fined the conflagration to the Post Office stores.
The Bulletin rises to acknowledge its gratitude
to the officers and men. It also rises to thank
other newspapers in Melbourne and Sydney, and
the proprietors of many printing establishments,
who kindly telegraphed and wrote offers of
assistance with regard to publishing the paper and
the magazine. Fortunately, owing to the bright
example above mentioned, and the pluck and skill
of the firemen. The Bulletin and Lone Hand
machinery is intact. The proprietors also thank
the many correspondents who have expressed
their sympathy in this paper’s great loss, and
hasten to assure them that, for the before-
mentioned reasons, there is practically no loss.
All the same, it was as close a dodging of destruc-
tion as any well-regulated family newspaper and
magazine cares for.
REASSURING.
OLD LADY : “If I should insure with your com-
pany and die within a month, what claim
would I have ?”
LIFE INSURANCE CANVASSER : “My dear lady,
insure with our company, and if you drop
dead to-morrow you simply present these
papers at our office, and we pay without a
murmur.”
The Sydney lawyers had good pickings
in the two family Equity suits of the Horderns.
There were four “silks” and three “scuffs”
engaged, besides two firms of the lower branch of
the profession. As the arguments ran into days
the “refreshers” were numerous, besides the big
fee that went with each brief.
Most of the late Camperdown borough,
now absorbed into Sydney municipality, was
originally the historic Grose Farm (the holding of
Thomas Laycock), and the estate of Major
Foveaux. Governor Phillip leased 30 acres to
Major Grose, afterwards Lieutenant-Governor.
That lease extended to the site of Prince
Alfred Hospital. Thomas Laycock obtained
28 a.cres, running up to near St. John’s College.
Major Foveaux’s lease is now in part
occupied by Prince Alfred Hospital. After a
time the leases fell in or were resumed, and the
ground was set apart for an orphanage. The
orphanage authorities bartered it back to the
Crown, whereupon the Crown let it out in 30-acre
blocks for grazing, and on one of these blocks the
genial Dan O’Connor learned, as a barefooted boy,
how to handle sheep and chase cattle.
“G.” : Joe Ward’s munificent offer on
behalf of Maoriland to borrow the money for a
Dreadnought— “or two, if necessary” (frantic
cheers)—which has brought about a straightening-
up of the Civil Service, a feverish casting out of
the Government pumpkinologist, and the retrench-
ment of the Under-secretary for Post Holes, all in
the cause of economy, recalls that the Rainy Isles
have probably put up a record for experts during
the latter years of their financial Bacchanalia. If
anybody had skill in the making of pancakes, and
persistency enough to make himself a nuisance, he
was sure to be absorbed as a specialist in one of the
multifarious departments of the country. There is
a poultry department with experimental stations,
which leave a handsome deficit after paying the sal-
aries of the Professor of Hens, and the Commissioner
for Giblets, who is alleged to have started the wage-
earning game as a snake-charmer. Then there is
a fisheries department which has been dabbling
in fish ova long enough to have every drain-pipe
in the Dominion stocked with sea-serpents. Nor
does Ward’s Dominion lack inspectors of fisheries,
who put on green goggles and dive hundreds of
feet under the sea in pursuit of their duty. More-
over, the amazing place owns a tourist depart-
ment, the functions of whose experts are not to
he spoken of in a serious paper. Even the
primary schools were being converted into kinder-
gartens and hobby-shops and bear-pleasaunces, to
the destruction of the three “R’s”and the en-
ragement of the teachers. And now the want of
money has come down like a wolf on the fold and
a hawk on the fowl-run, and Massey, the leader
of Maoriland’s attenuated Opposition, is able to
crow and flap his wings, and say “ I told yer so.”
Meanwhile the number of departments has been
reduced from 34 to 16, and discharged experts are
spreading like a blight on the land.

From a London exchange :
Indignation was the dominant note at the meeting on
Tuesday of the Drapers’ Chamber of Trade, when the
question of the charges made by the Rev. R. J. Campbell
as to the morality of female shop assistants in London,
and more particularly in the West End, was fully
discussed.
Campbell, who is one of those rarities, a tho-
roughly democratic cleric, had stated from the
pulpit as a new and hideous discovery a thing that
most people accept as a truism outside the pulpit—
viz., that a big proportion of shopgirls in the
Cold Land are driven to supplement their scanty
earnings with the (also scanty) wages of sin.
Further, Mr. Campbell said that girls in cer-
tain establishments were “encouraged to go
wrong to earn their livelihood.” No disin-
terested person with experience of London
life will question these two allegations. Two
classes of London millinery establishments
possess girls who have to keep themselves with
what they earn outside legitimate business hours.
The first class is made up of those comparatively
small shops which pay their hands inadequate
wages and close their eyes to what the damsels do
in their leisure moments. The London play-
wright who was responsible for “ Diana of Dob-
son’s,” recently produced here, handled this brand
of business house—albeit gingerly, as was necessary
for presentation before an English-speaking public.
Dobson’s “ young ladies,” who drew ss. a week,
“ less deductions,” weren’t shown in the process of
leading the gay life ; but it would be hard for any
rational person to get away from the view that,
perforce, most of them did lead that sort of life.
There is another class of London shop—the ultra-
“smart” establishriient, where a hat is apt to
cost ten guineas, and a fur stole a hundred or so—
that won’t touch the young lady assistant of
austere virtue with a 40-rod pole. To this estab-
lishment the male citizen of affluence repairs as
frequently as does his female relative. He comes
there to buy gifts and to approve any new female
“ find ” the management may have made. The
papers of the Sporting Times and Winning Post
type, which are faithful reflexes of life as it is
lived in their orbit, teem with merry references
to the girl from So-and-So’s, who visits Brighton
or Ostend from Saturday till Monday by way of
keeping body and spul together. Her wage is
often fairly high—sufficient in many cases to buy
the gloves she wears when off duty. But of
course it isn’t nearly enough to pay for the
state she is expected to keep up for the
credit of the house. The privilege of meeting
that liability appertains to a “gentleman friend,”
or two or three. The “indignation” above-
noted of the Drapers’ Chamber of Trade is flag-
rantly bogus. The drapers have to protest
against Rev. Campbell in order to save their faces
before the section of the public that doesn’t
understand modern business methods, or the
whereforeness of them. But they wear their
tongues in their cheeks even as they are protest-
ing, and the huge majority of Londoners know
it. And the huge majority of drapers know that
they know it.
"TAE BE PREECISE."
THE MACTACISH ( with interest): “ Yon stoukin’s ye're wearin’ are nae the real MacGregor
tartan. Hand up yere fut an’ I'll explain it tae ye.”
About the most down-trodden tribe in the
community—mercantile clerkdom is its only pos-
sible rival —is asserting itself slightly in S.A.,
where a Governesses’ Association is being formed.
The State’s own large staff is not complaining ; it
works pretty hard, but it knows its hours and its
duties. The wail and the kick come from the
household slaves of Mrs. Fatwoman (or Mrs.
Cocky, as the case may be). The incompetent
person, who professes to teach and can’t, will be
weeded out of the association. On the other
hand, there may be some redress at last for the
girl who can teach but can’t do washing; yet,
having hired herself out to do the former, is
bullied into doing the latter as well. But there is
never likely to be any adequate redress for such
cases as one now made public, where a refined
woman was sneered at and blocked in every pos-
sible way when she struck for an occasional bath,
and was compelled to sleep in the same room with
her four charges—two of them heavy snorers—
and all doors and windows closed. The criminal
law makes it impossible to fall adequately on an
employer of that character.

A movement for supplementing suburban
public libraries with picture galleries has been
started in Melbourne by Charles Atkins.
ex-president of the Australian Chamber of
Manufactures. Prahran is to have a loan
collection of pictures in a room at the Town
Hall, Mi-. Atkins having communicated his
enthusiasm to a number of other local resi-
dents with pictures worth exhibiting. Ihe
collection w ill be arranged by an artist and
introduced to the public in a duly impres-
sive manner, and the inevitable result will be
an all-round municipal rivalry in loan col-
lections if this pioneer display is a popular
success. . In wdiich case, of course, pictures
will be presented and bequeathed as W'ell
as loaned to municipal galleries as time
goes on. Also the unfortunate Australian
artists who pine for want of patronage will,
if they have any commercial instinct, hang
their unsold canvases in the nearest town
hall, instead of practically hiding them front
public view. The artistic section of any
community can be easily enlarged by giving
the people further opportunities to acquite
the habit of discriminating between respec-
table paintings and disreputable oleographs.
Melbourne thus far is mostly lacking m
even the low taste in art, which prompts
the small but hopeful householder to spend
half a crown in an art union. If the subur-
ban town-hall gallery idea should incite
the mayors and corporations to organise
a system of periodical picture raffles it will be
another little blow to unadulterated Philistin-
ism. The spectacle of a dozen fat councillors
coming to blows over the choice of Australian
pictures for their next art union would be
rather improving than otherwise. Their wrang-
ling at present, is wholly confined to questons of
drain pipes and such unaasthetic matters.
OPPORTUNITY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O thou who stand’sb expectant at the gate
Which opens on to Lands of High Intent—
I know not if thy ways are kindly meant ;
I only know that Anarchy’s thy state 1
Some fools who see thee beckon bar their door
And scuttle back to mediocrity,
Turning to cobbling shoes as patiently
As though Thou had’st not passed in days of yore.
The angels oft their pearly teeth must gnash
Within their solitary Paradise,
Watching thy pranks, which really are not nice,
As of the Universe thou mak’st a hash.
The males of that Angel Community
Would roast thee for the dowdy female saints.
Thou lead’st to Heaven, the while—so run their
plaints—
“ Thou keep’st the pretty ones, base Opportunity ! ”
N.S.W.
HELEN JEROME.

Time taught me altruistic lore,
Although in outlines dim ;
And quite enthusAily I swore
To think of Her and Him.
Now, Dear, upstairs, a little cot
Pour chubby limbs d-> fit.
Toe rest of Grammar is forgot—
I do my wmrk for ft.

Every Woman Should Know
That Sir Charles A. Cameron, M.D.,
ex-President of the Royal College of
Surgeons, has testified to the absolute purity
of the materials employed in the manu-
facture of Sunlight Soap, and declares
that he himself uses Sunlight Soap.
What every woman does not know is, that
in common soaps she frequently pays for
adulterations at the prices of oils and fats.
Try Sunlight Soap next wash day, and you
will see that Sir Charles A. Cameron is
right—he should know.

The Deadhorse movement struck a very-
unresponsive audience at Newtown, Sydney. In
the town hall of that big suburb the mayor and
two other people gathered themselves together
one night last week to enthuse. They waited long
and earnestly, and two other people arrived.
Then the mayor closed the meeting, and these
five people went home, and the door slammed.
♦ * * *
It isn’t often a person has reason to re-
joice over being “shot in,” but a Melbourne man
is just now in that mixed condition. The other
day an elderly woman climbed into the box at the
City Court, and swore that the young man in
question, whom she had nursed as a baby and had
known all his days, had violently and shamefully
assaulted her that* day week. It was, she said,
dark at the time of the episode, but she was too
well acquainted with him to make any error. It
seemed a clear case for committal; in fact, the
accused didn’t seem to have a hope. But just then
a constable got up and stated that he had arrested
the youth on another charge early on the day in
question, and that he had passed the evening and
night in the North Melbourne lockup—and the
case collapsed. The assaulted ex-nurse hadn’t
even the manners to apologise.
“ D.” makes a few observations :
I see that Sydney’s returned wanderer, Dr. J. Robert-
son, says that in his travels abroad he found no ferry
services to equal those of Sydney. It makes me think
that this particular medico didn’t look very hard, or else
that, for some obscure reason, he is biassed in this regard.
I have covered a good deal of fluid in my time, and in
doing so have sampled a number of ferry-boats ; and
with a clear conscience I say that, barring the appalling
vessels that ply on the Thames (my acquaintance with them
runs to various trips between London Embankment and
Greenwich), and further barring a proportion of the Seine
(Paris) ferry-boats, there are no more expensive, uncom-
fortable and indifferently-appointed craft to be found
anywhere than those which carry passengers about Port
Jackson. The ideal ferry-boat plies between San Fran-
cisco (U.S.A.) and the towns across the bay. It is about
four times as wide as any similar vessel in Sydney harbor.
The seats are broad and absolutely comfortable. You
may get most modern necessaries, from a boot-shine
upwards, on board. Its cleanliness is amazing ; so is its
speed and absence of smell. Excellent boats are those
which are to be found on the Italian lakes. They supply
good wine, food, etc., at a ridiculously cheap rate.
The memory of provender supplied on a vessel that once
bore me from Como to Bellagio lives in my memory
yet. As to Sydney’s ferry boats, I will defy anyone
to sit for half an hour on their narrow and unyielding
settles and not feel anguished, and inclined for profanity.
They are insanely dear. A penny is about twice too much
for conveying one across the narrow strip of water be-
tween Circular Quay and Milson’s Point. Eightpence as
the price of a trip to Manly and back is extortionate.
These things are proved by the bloated and ridiculous
dividends of the ferry companies, whose monopolies en-
able them to demand what fees they will. As to con-
veniences on board, it is impossible for the mere male to
get so much as a decent wash and brush-up on the most
luxuriously appointed boat in the harbor. As to
cleanliness, the best one can say about the steamers is
that some are cleaner than others. In my view, the craft
plying to North Sydney constitute one of the strongest
arguments in favor of the immediate construction of a
bridge or tunnel between the city and North Shore. Also,
I hold that the sooner the State takes over the contract
of conveving citizens to the more distant harbor suburbs
the better it will be for the said citizens’ comfort and
banking accounts.
That humble individual, John Hop, seems
to be doing his duty by Australia in the matter of
increasing the population. A recent police depu-
tation to Premier Murray mentioned some
remarkable families in the Victorian bobby
force. Sergeant Arthur, of Warrnambool,
gladdened the Premier’s heart by remark-
ing that, though there were only six con-
stables in the potato district, they had
among them 38 children. Senior-Con-
stable O’Loghlen owned up to nine child-
ren, and another copper with a lot of
halfpence was Senior-Constable Brady,
who claimed no less than eleven Australian
natives. Premier Murray was so impressed
with these figures that he promised to see
what he could do about raising the police
salaries all round and handing out more pensions.
The Dead Letter Office has “ been there,”
and it vigorously scoffs at the perky man’s cock-
snreness, and calls out “ Liar” to the growler who
knows well that bad administration in Melbourne
is to blame for all the disappointments at the
letter-delivery windows. In Queensland alone,
last year, no less than 147,359 letters, 16,153 post-
cards, and 53,480 papers found
refuge in the Dead Letter Office.
In the letters and packets money
to the extent of £3816 16s. 3d. was
obtained. The office, having got
inside the envelopes, or read the
“ If not delivered, please return ”
instructions on the outside, was
able to right most of this busi-
ness. But no less than 916 letters,
containing £621, were so vaguely
addressed outside to “ Mr. Smith,
Australia,” or something of the
sort, and so conspicuously devoid
inside of even the writers’ own
names and addresses, that the
owners could not be traced. No
fewer than 1194 articles were posted
without any address at all, while
those wrongly addressed numbered
18,757. The office followed up a
number of the complaints. In 35
cases the missing letter turned up at
the writer’s home ; a nurse lost one
on the road to the post office; three
were found in the writers’ pockets
after a dust had been kicked up at
the p. 0.; one was unaddressed ; 55 were
wrongly addressed, and the contents of 19 were
found in the Dead Letter office in the same state ;
16 had been duly delivered, but had been mislaid
after deli very ; and a postal note —the contents of
aletter declared to be lost—was found in a book,
where presumably the addressee had left it. The
Department owns up to 64 cases of carelessness.
So it isn’t one-tenth so casual as the public which
complains of its alleged casualness.
* * * *
The early morning canter or sprint, with
a milk-cart in the lead and a motor-car in full
chase, is becoming a familiar feature of Mel-
bourne’s daily life, and is likely to continue so
until the law provides a stiff course of stonebreak-
ing, without the option, for frequent offenders.
Scarcely a day passes without milk adulteration
cases being heard at one court or other. Mostly
there are three or four a day, but the minimum
fine of £lO usually handed out by the honorarium
fails to stop the rascally trade. The cheerful,
noisy “Milk-oh!” used to reckon on getting caught
about once a year or so, but since the inspectors
took to using a motor the odds have been sadly
reduced ; so now, when the milk fiend sees a party
in one of these machines coming along in a hurry
and gesticulating to him, he swiftly drives up the
nearest right-of-way and capsizes his can of
ordinary mercantile milk in the gutter, or
heaves it into a backyard. That only means
a £5 fine for “obstructing the inspectors.”
One such youth miscalculated badly the other day.
Being hard pressed, he pulled his prad up along-
side a fence and heaved the can with its contents
bodilv over ; then he drove down the lane to meet
the inspectors, relying on the householder to
accept the “ milk ”as the price of silence. Unfor-
tunately, a woman happened to be just inside that
fence feeding her fowls. The can landed partly
on her foot and partly on the neck of her best
Leghorn pullet; consequently, while the “Milk-
oh!” was cheerfully passing the time of day to
the inspectors and assisting them to samples of
his “ good ” milk, his left ear received a visit from
a Leghorn pullet, propelled by a limping woman
in a bad temper. The explanations were con-
siderable, but “Milk-oh” was observed to smile
cheerfully as he departed. He had saved his boss
a fiver, less the cost of the fowl.
The career of an Armenian gentleman
named Terence Callaghan, of Richmond (Vic.),
should be worth watching. He lately scored his
seventh conviction for selling watered milk, or
milky water. His fines already totalled £33, and
as the added water this time amounted to 24 per
cent., the Court stuck another £2O on. There
might be some nice wagering as to which limit
Terence will reach first —the £lOO fine or 100 per
cent, of water. It is satisfactory to reflect that
he can’t go beyond the latter limit, anyhow.

“If you don't promise to marry me I'll shoot

“ It’s all very well saying that just now, but you

A very dead philanthropist has just been
heard from in Adelaide. In 1876 William Parkin,
M.L.A., and otherwise well known, endowed a
benevolent fund for Congregational- churches,
with the proviso that it was not to start active
benevolence till its annual income
reached £lOOO. At that time £12,000
or so would have been capital enough
for the purpose, and it seemed that a
very few years of accumulation would
get the fund to the point at which it
could commence distributing its in-
come. But interest fell, and fell,
and dropped, and tumbled. For
years the fund, despite accumula-
tions at compound interest, got
further away from its goal instead
of nearer to it. It was a stern chase
—the fund after the Congregational
Church —and at times the Church
was so far ahead that all hope of
overtaking it seemed lost. But at
long last the income has touched the
£lOOO mark, and the distribution
commences. And quite probably it
will hardly have commenced before
another fall in interest will make it necessary to
stop again and accumulate some more.

One of the private residential estates
which Bent doubtless would have purchased for
the Vic. Government went begging at auction
last week. This is the property (seven acres of
land with a mansion on it) situated at the corner
of St. Kilda and Domain roads. Melbourne. It
strikes the casual passer-by as being one of the
best building sites in the metropolis, but is appa-
rently too near town to be a tempting suburban
spec.' Bent threw out hints rc the converting of
this estate into a Government herbarium in con-
nection with the adjacent Botanical Gardens, and
but for the scandal attaching to his other land
purchases, this might have been quietly added to
the list. The Murray Government last week de-
cided not to spend the proposed £20,000 or so
as a first outlay on a herbarium ground. Two
days later the rejected property was put up for
sale by the executors of the late owner and passed
in again. The house which nobody seems to want
was built for Major-General Hutton of yore, and
the seven acres were the scene of some festive
garden parties.
“Johanna”: Nomadic as the Arabs—
so does the modern Australian threaten to
become. The tentmaker, that philosophical
soul, finds more and more work to do.
Tentmakers and • bootmakers develop a cyni-
cal philosophy all their own. However ob-
scure and poverty-stricken a cobbler you will
always find him a thinking man, and a cynical
one. The feet of men are clay, however godlike
they manage to appear to a coterie of worship-
pers. Until recently the tentmakers have been
comparatively few. But the sleeping-out craze
has gone far when it has induced the natty
suburban well-to-do to encumber his several
square feet of carefully-mown lawn with a
sedate toy tent. The sleeping out and the
week-end tent has been a strong institution
for several seasons. Now, at the sea fringe
all round the bay, are the lines of canvas.
Some enterprising boarding-house perpetra-
tors have given up stable dwellings and
have grown a mushroom-like colony of small
tents, with an immense canvas dining-room
as a nucleus. In the ti-tree scrub there are
streets and rows of stretcher-furnished tar-
paulin, let for weeks or week-ends. Easter
ended the summer crop of tent-dwellers,
and the owners count their gains. I heard
half-a-dozen girl shop-toilers (those in-
tensely coiffured and smart-skirted maidens)
tot up the “engagements” of the Easter go-
as-you-please. There were seven senti-
mental engagements, with every prospect
of matrimonial culmination, out of fourteen
tents —or fourteen engagements out of
seven tents, I’m not sure which. The surf
bathing that is thrown in with the tent
life has a strong lien on the market.
Plump, chic, sun-browned girls dabbling
about in hysterical fear and the cutest
thing in flimsy bathing dress, appeal
to the brawny, brave, masculine de-
fender. Melbourne suburban beaches
are many and of long stretches —the
councils, or protecting corporations,
here have not yet had to resort to
“regulations” in aquatic gear as
in the elbow-jostled surf crowds
on the comparatively few harbor
beaches of Sydney. Melbourne
maidens have scope for stronglv-
imaginative covering. Scarlet and
brevity are the favorite combina-
tions in the fancy of the plump,
nut-brown maid.

Under the Neitenstein regime,
prisoners in N.S.W. gaols receive
some very thoughtful attentions.
When he approaches the end of his
sentence, Burglar Bill is questioned as to
what sort of a billet he would like to
fill on getting out, and what part of the
country he would like best to live in. Then
Mr. Neitenstein exerts himself, and stirs up
outsiders to provide the suitable job. When
old Keck was Governor of Darlinghurst his
kindness took the form of allowing the prisoner
who was near the end of his term to go about
Sydney during the day, that he might, if pos-
sible, take a job by the forelock. It was very
good of Keck, but the results were poor ; a clob ■-
cropped citizen who asked if a billet would tie
available in two or three weeks’ time, and who
fled alarmedly in the direction of the gaol when
offered an immediate job, hadn’t much chance.
THE MIDDLE MAN.
In bubonic plague the intermediary, coming between
the rat and man, is the flea. Householders should clean
their houses of it.— N.S.W. Board of Health's chairman.
Maoriland has just had its attention drawn
to the fact that it has no legal machinery to pre-
vent a doctor who is hopelessly insane practising
his profession till he does something to bring him
within the grip of the law. In this case, a doctor
was tried for murder, and at the trial it came out
that his “ eccentricity” was well known, and that
quite a number of people had prophesied that
some day he would “shake things up.” But he
continued to practise till, one quiet evening, he
killed a lady and shook up the corpse, and now
Maoriland is inquiring whether any more mad
doctors are running: round among people who are
fully aware of their “eccentricities.” Some
people even propose that doctors should be
periodically inspected, like other cattle.
THE LAND OF THE GAY AND GRIM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
London is perhaps the spot where one can oftenest see
the extremes of poverty and economy, contrasted with
lavish expenditure.—Pall Mall Magazine.
It’s oh ! to be in London now, with the springtime scents
around ;
To sit in the dear old penny chairs, with your feet on the
greening ground ;
To 101 l in your dear old taxi-cab, or bump in your dear old
’bus,
And wolf in baste your farthing fish, or your early
asparagus.
It’s oh ! to lounge in the playhouse bars, with a earriage-
and-pair outside,
Or to smoke your fag in the sixpenny seats, with a long
warm beer supplied ;
It’s oh ! to see wild life in town on the shavings of half-a-
jim,
In the town where a thousand are glad and g'ay, and a
million are mostly grim.
It’s oh ! to savor the dear old slums, and gloat on the
dear old scenes
Where the housewife hargles for mouldy bones, and
judges the margarines ;
To drive to the gilded stoking-halls where they have
stuffed fowl for lunch,
Or the dear old crowded dining-dens where the sad scone-
snatchers munch ;
To back up your cart with your East End tart for a tup-
penn}' afternoon tea,
Or motor down for a five-o’clock to Brighton beside the
sea.
It’s oh ! for the bowl of London life, to lap at its
bubbling brim,
Though only a thousand are gay and glad, and a million
are mostly grim.
Arise, then, sons of Australian Fat, for the voice of the
Spring we hear ;
Strike camp, and make for the land of bliss in the youth
of the English year 1
Come on, we’ll gather our travelling-traps, and give
three cheers and go
To the lovely isle in the northern seas where the Free-
trade orchids grow !
We’ll leave our native shores awhile ; but, when the fogs
come down,
With a swag of suits and socks and ties, as worn by the
Court and Crown,
We’ll seek our sun-warmed South again, and murmur a
prayer for him
Who dwells where the thousands are glad and gay—and
the millions are gaunt and grim 1
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.
