UNCONSCIOUS HUMOR.
HIS MOTHER (reprovingly): “ Come here, Willie.
You know Satan always finds work for idle
hands, so I'll get you to clean up the yard
for me.”
The Queensland tick, after a battle royal
with Ma State’s dairy-inspectors, has passed over
the Tweed River and has put in an appearance on
the upper reaches of the Richmond River, where
the big Cow cheques come from. Nothing seems
to interpose between the tick and the whole of
the eastern seaboard, save its alleged dislike of
the climate and scenery further south. The tick
question is a subject of contention between
N.S.W. and Queensland, each blaming the other
State for its neglect and inefficiency when engaged
in shooing the animal off the premises. The Ma
State, indeed, is wailing at the inefficiency of
State quarantine in all its aspects, and is beseech-
ing the Federal authority to assume control of
quarantine matters. Which is singularly unlike
Ma in her general aspect.
“ Cict ” : The ignorance of some people
occasions me pain. Ido not refer, in the present
instance, either to Joe Cook or to the aboriginal
tribesmen of Outback who have not yet bumped
culture, rum, and the missionary. Rather do 1
indicate (1) “Flinderino ” (B. 4/3/’o9)—he who con-
tends that the pewit alias pevvee, is the most
frequent bush bird in Australia —and (2) “ Myrtlea
who alleges (B. l/4/’O9) that the leatherhead holds
the biscuit for numerousness. Where, I ask these
false-alarm naturalists, do they place the parra-
keet, the green-leek, and the grass-parrot ? In
fact, where, for that matter, do they place the
whole damn family of the parrots ? “ Myrtle ’ is
several leagues ahead of “Flinderino” in his
nomination ; and yet for every leatherhead that is
loose in any part of any of the Australian States
there’s at least 900 parrots. Moreover, while the
leatherhead is indeed a rara avis in Riverina, the
parrot is in divers millions in every corner of the
continent. Also the white cockatoo has a2oto 1
opportunity of pulling off the ribbon against
either the pewit or the leatherhead.
“ J.Gr.” : “Torkin’ erbout corpsus bein’
found in old shafts,” remarked Bill from Scotch-
man’s. “ Six of us wus oncet workin’ a claim on
the Durham Lead. We ’ad a ’ole down erbout
70ft. afore we struck the gutter. It was a dam
wet shaft, too; we ’ad ter keep balin’ erbout five
canvas tanks to one bucket of dirt. An’ from
Satiday till Monday the bloomin’ ole would get
erbout 40ft. of water in it. We ’ad driv along the
gutter a couple er hundered feet, when, one Mon-
day mornin’ we took er look down the shaft
an’ saw that some fool ’ad tumbled down an got
drownded, or sooisided, or been murdered an
pitched down. Any’ow, tlier ’e wus down our
shaft, only ’is boot-’eels erbove the water. We
told the boy who druv the horse in the whip
ter gallup like blazes into the township
fer Brown, the bobby. I says we’d better
git ’im outer the water, but the others said it was
no blamed use as the bloke was ded. Presently
Brown galloped up. ‘What's ther matter? e
says; an’ when we told ’im, he says we oughter
fished the ded-’un out. Brown got narked when
we says we left that job fer him. ’E says e
thought it wus sooiside, as the corpse must have a
stone tied to ’is neck. Brown took off ’is ’elmit
an’ coat, an’ said ’e supposed ’e ’ad ter go down,
an’ we lowered ’im. When he got down to the
water, ’e grabbed ’old of the corpsus’ leg. Then
’e dropped it like er hot spud, and yelled ter us
ter pull ’im up. When ’e landed, ’e swore at us
somethin’ awful, an’ said he ’oped we would all go
ter ’ell. It turned out that the stiff-’un wus only
our old wheelbarrow, which we left in the drive
on Saturday afternoon, an’ ’ad floated out on the
w&ter an’ riz up the shaft when the water riz, the
tips of its ’andles bein’ just erbove the water.
But they wus the ded ring fer a pair of ’eels
belongin’ to a corps of a sooiside or a murder.”

yet they does ketch only few fish, and tiny wans
at that. Th’ thing, fer tu study is

in th’ wrong place, if yu had th’
hist bait in th’ worruld, an’ lashins
ov it. Whin Oi had me selec-

be G-unongjugruwah, below
Widgietalongwidya, I co’t a

hardly lift tim out ov th’
river!! The banks wor
powerful steep an’ slip-
perier than a lying lawyer’s

noble to the water’s idge
and then I dhraws him up

I pulled him up and up ; an’ whin
his gills wor livil wid me ears, there
wor yarrds, fate annyhow, ov his
tail dhragging on th’ ground.
Shure Oi was that he wuz the

in me shpring carrt ta Jugiomr an’

ta th’ ’Bidgee cod. If fit hadn’t been forr thim,
and forr me bein’so fond ov fishin, tis a warrum
woolgrovver Oi’d be this day. Yis !
The Tweed River (N.S.W.) cow cocky is
feeling anxious about paspalum, the grass he
swears by. He had seen it wipe out weeds of
various kinds ; even buffalo grass couldn t stay its
progress over the face of the earth. He thought
that nothing in the heavens above or in the earth
beneath could make it fight hard for its existence.
But a weed, locally called Dunbible weed, is now
threatening cocky’s paspalum. This weed gets its
name from the fact that it was first noticed at
Dunbible, near Murwillumbah, during- the time
the Lismore-Tweed railway was being built. Ihe
seeds of it are supposed to have come there in a
consignment of chaff for the horses employed on
the railway construction works. The shire council,
five of whose six members are cow cookies, lias de-
clared it a noxious weed, and the whole population
is called upon to shoot it on sight.

“ Mallacoota ” : A paternal Victorian
Government some time ago undertook a campaign
of extermination against the fish-eating cormorant.
Threepence a head bonus was given, and in some
districts cormorant hunters did well. The birds
have regular roosting places. What the average
shag really likes is a high tree at the head of some
creek or inlet, and where a shooter could get a
dozen or so in a line the hunting paid, lhis
satisfactory state of affairs continued until an
enterprising south-of-Gabo chap got a cutter and
went out among the islands with which the
Southern Ocean is dotted from Bass Straits to
East and North of Wilson’s Promontory, lhe
cormorants have their rookeries and breeding
places on some of these bleak islets, and the
hunter averaged about £l4 a day for a while.
Then the staggered Ministry withdrew the bonus,
and another promising industry was busted.
“ Tally walka” goes to a Dreadnought
meeting :
The latest in our township is the appearance of the
Dreadnought complaint. Five or six of the more badly
diseased requisitioned the mayor to call a public meet-
ing and he did it. After he had personally started the
ball’ rolling, a little old man mentioned something or
other about Ole Hingland in a very low, awe-inspiring
whisper. He became very badly rattled on account of
the audience helping him to forget most of what he
intended to say, and sat down amidst great enthusiasm.
Then a warrior, with moustache bristling*, said desper-
ately that an Englishman is worth two foreigners ; after
which he assured us he would lay down all he possessed,
and even his life, if it comes to war. He had a sale a day
or two later, which leads me to think that he leaves for
Germany immediately to tear a Dreadnought up by the
roots, and bring it home via Pooncarie and the Darling
River. Next a business man worked off some energetic
remarks about Germany, and he knocked some sparks out
of William before he sat down. He omitted to mention
that a great part of the contents of his store were im-
ported from Germany, and that a quota of the money
paid therefor is helping to build the German Dread-
noughts. He felt sure that when the Japs come along
Fisher’s three “Insect destroyers” will be about Port
Pirie or somewhere unhandy. It didn’t seem to strike
him that 23 (not three) “ Insects” at Port Pirie will any-
how be more useful to us in such a case than a Dread-
nought in the North Sea. The drunkest man in the
audience apologised for Fisher by interjecting pretty
often that we must make a start some time or other.
Our kid-banger followed with some well-worn yap about
sentiment and gratitude, and then produced his trump
card. He reckons we’ll save our necks by giving a
Dreadnought to England. This was a very diplomatic
argument for these dry and dusty parts, as the neck is
pretty well indispensable, and it went pretty close
to relaxing* the grip I had on my own bob.
Had he followed up this neck argument I believe
a quid would have been .collected. He very
unwisely side-tracked, and made a move for the door, say-
ing a couple of children would give us an item. It was
only a ruse to get away, because no item turned up.
Taking advantage of a painful pause which occurred
about this time, the second-drunkest man made a dive
for the platform and safely arrived. “ Mr. Chairman, 1
have much pleasure in proposing and seconding that this
town does the same as all the other towns have done in
the present industrialspute in Broken Hill and send
some money to the strikers.** (Loud applause.) His
Warship made clockwork-like motions with his
hands, and ruled the speaker out of order. The
strike-pay advocate made an adventurous journey,
back to his seat. A resolution was put to the
meeting by his Warship for a show °f hands in favor of
opening a Dreadnought presentation fund. Nineteen
out of the 87 present hoisted their beer-lifters, and his
Warship remarked desperately that the motion was
carried unanimously. It required a nervy man, but he
went there to do the job, and he did it. On an invita-
tion being made by his Warship for donations, two bob
was collected from an old chap in the front seat, so it s
to be hoped he .saves his neck. We finished up with
“Gor* Save,** and scooted hard for our groggenes at 10
p.m.
“Coda” (from Lawler’s, W.A.) : I am
late in contributing my quota to this kookargu-
ment, owing to the delay in the construction of
Australia’s transcontinental railway- Let me say
without preamble that I have known many Jacks,
and none of them cared a guffaw whether his meat
was dead or alive. The notable thing about the
kook is not so much what he eats as the amount
he eats. I had a young one once, and was accus-
tomed’to cut his meat up for him, being under the
delusion that if I didn’t he might get dyspepsia
or something. One day I took about a pound
of steak to his perch (half being for him, half for
my wife’s pup), and, cutting it in two pieces, I
put one piece aside while I divided the other
into what I considered negotiable chunks.
When I had finished, I looked around for the first
piece. Alack, it was gone. Jack, the bird whose
digestion I was protecting, had, scoffed it whole.
There was something wrong with the Jack that a
recent Bulletin contributor said he saw leavinga
dead cow the other day. My Jack would not
have left the animal. He would have swallowed
it. horns and all.
LIFE IN A BUSH TOWNSHIP.
Scene at intervals in main street. A most
valuable dog has been poisoned. The residents
are endeavoring to resuscitate it. It has been
dosed with salt, also tobacco. Its tail has been
removed, ears lopped, smoke blown up its nostrils;
and though there are still some 27 more remedies
to apply, grave doubts are entertained as to its
recovery.
SAW PIT GULLY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
By twilit jungles, where steep spurs ended,
All lonely and wild and strange,
Where songs of waters and birds were blended.
Ran Sawpit Gully through gorges splendid,
Under the Moonbi Range.
There came the teams with their jungle treasure,
Skidding from north and south ;
There swung the saws with a rhythmic measure,
And days of labor and days of pleasure
Were ours at the sawpit’s mouth.
Ripping the logs and the planks asunder,
Spitting out jets of dust,
From early morn till the sun went under
The steel fangs humm’d through the rich red plunder,
Torn from the hills august.
The dust made cushions—in bags we stuck it;
Our beds were of feath’ry fern ;
Our dinner bell was a leaky bucket,
We dropped our tools when the waddy struck it,
And carried up “ bits to burn.”
We fared full well on a long plank table,
Women and girls and men,
Under a roof with no wall or gable,
And never was better in fact or fable
Than tea in our pint-pots then.
The whispering nights in the firelight found us
Gathered before the camps,
While dingoes howled Tong the scrubs that bound us,
And here and there in the gloom around us
Twinkled the firefly lamps.
Then a jolly girl did enchantment render
O’er all did those hills enclose ;
And five of six did betimes attend her,
For her soft eyfes rivalled the bluebell's splendor.
Her mouth was a fresh red rose.
Some sought her hand ’neath the starry glory,
And one on a day of rain ;
All five at last told the olden story,
And four went forth when the glens were hoary,
Nor came to the camps again.
Ghosts of days that are long gone over,
Passing in dumb review,
At places linger where they discover,
Down the way of the reckless rover
His fairest of heart-flowers grew.
And long they linger on Sawpit Gully,
That only the thunders change,
Where nought is now that can spoil or sully
But phantom saws that at night sound dully
Under the Moonbi Range.
N.S.W.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.

“ W.J.A.” tells about a Chow :
About eight years ago The Bulletin published a par.
concerning the most cheerful Outback funeral on record
—that of Ah Cat, an optimistic Chinaman, who be-
cpieathed £lO to the local band to play rollicking tunes
en route to the grave.
I performed on a slide trombone on that occasion, and
helped to blare out “ When Johnny Comes Marching
Home,” “The Girl ’e Left Behind ’im,” “The Cat Came
Back,” and suchlike inspiriting ditties. The temperature
was 109“, a cloud of dust obscured the procession, and
having blown ourselves as dry as Og’s bedstead, we had
sundry drinks at the expense of the estate on the way
home.
The other day came the queer sequel to the affair.
After all these years, a casual Chinaman blew into the
Never-Never (where he came from nobody knows) and
proceeded to collect the bones of the departed yellow
heathen for transmission to China, in accordance with
ancient custom. He fastened to a long-handled shovel
and got to work on the grave of Ah Cat, but he was dis-
turbed by an angry female—for it appears that the late
demented had left a white widow and family, who
strongly objected to any interference with the remains.
The authorities here stepped in and restrained the casual
yellow pagan—virtue being thus rewarded and the villain
discomfited.
These casual yellow men periodically turn up at coun-
try towns on the bone-accumulating business. The
practice is simple. The dry bones are gathered together
in a “soogee” bag and taken to the nearest billabong.
There they are scraped and washed. The bone-gatherers
are not particular where the washing is done. On one
occasion, at Brewarrina, we saw a couple of them sluicing
a bag of bones in the identical spot where we drew our
drinking water. As the Darling was low at the time, and
not flowing, we passed them along with great violence
and profanity, and shot the bones into outer darkness.
On another occasion, on the Lachlan, the washing was
done in a lagoon where a dairyman got his water supply.
The said dairyman didn’t seem to mind, and, before I
could persuade the local council to get a move on, the
offenders and the bones were back in China.
THE ONE
DRAWBAOK.
“ Wot I hobjeks ter about this ’ere tram trailin’
is, Mrs. Muggins, that yer never knows who
yer'll 'are ter sit alongside hof.”
“ Huffin-Guffin ” : How some men will
struggle for a free and independent crust rather
than work for a boss ! This letter is from a miner
at Mount William (Vic.), where there was a rush
a few years ago. The miner has several times
attempted to get away, but after working on
wages for a while the call of the free life, the
bush, and the pure mountain air was irresistible :
lam still in the tunnel. The shareholders let us the
mine on tribute. I have not had a penny since January
[written 19/3/’o9]. We have our dirt stacked, and are
waiting for water. I have had rabbit every day since
February 6. We robbed a wild beehive, and so are living
on rabbits and wild honey. . . . I don’t get much to
read. Have not seen a Bulletin for a long, long time.
Wish I could see it. Will when it rains, and we can wash
our dirt.
So the circulation of The Bulletin will increase
by at least one when it rains.
AFTER THE SMASH.
SYMPATHETIC STRANGER: “Are you very much hurt, my dear sir?”
IRRITABLE VICTIM : “ How the devil can I tell till I find my glasses? ”
“ S.T.S.” : Did James Dee really build his
haystack over and around a hut (B. 11/3/’O9) ? It
seems an expensive kind of foundation, even when
one is going to sell the result by its estimated
tonnage. As James doubtless got the idea from
Lever’s “ Handy Andy,” an Irish novel once im-
mensely popular, he might just as well have used
it without trying to improve on it. Andy, who
was supposed to be a simpleton, sold his haycock
to the Squire as it stood ; and it stood mostly on
a rock, as the Squire’s men found when they tried
to cart it away. Andy’s defence was the best of
the joke. “ Sure,” said he, “didn’t the blessed
St. Pether found the Church on a rock ; and why
wouldn’t I be founding my cock of hay on a rock?”
* •* *
“ Jimmie Pannikin ” : Dannie Arkins
often begged of me to go out to his farrum on the
Goodradigbee for a few days’ fishing. I never
could get the time. One day he met me in Bina-
long. “Now, lukit here,” said he, “th’rivir is
in splendid thrim forr fishin’. O’ive ’ad free
extree rooms added to' the place ; so there’s plinty
shlapin’ accommodation. Oi’ want ye to come out
this day fortnight widout fail. Oi’ll have tucker,
lashin’s ov whisky, fishin’ loines, hooks, sinkers,
baits, ivviry t’ing that’s wantid. All ye have
ta do is ta bring yirself an’ a couple ov yir
frinds along—the Police Magisthrate forr
wan ; he’s a foine fellow, tho’ he found me
two quid not long ago fer righteous behavior
last Show Day. Oi’ll have th’ fish yarded
up in a shtretch about haff a moile long.
Oi’ll put woire-netting at wan ind, and
fix up a narrer inthrance about haff a mile
up above. Now do coine an’ thry yir luck
an’ moi whisky.” I made all necessary
arrangements. Two days before the ap-
pointed date I got a letter from Arkins :
“ I am sending you these few lines hop-
ping you are well, as it does not leave
me at present. Three days ago the river
ran banker. It’s still running, being
yards higher in the middle than at the
sides. It carried away miles of me
fencing and drowned 200 weathers.
But come out with your frends, and
we’ll go shooting rabbits, looking for
fences, and drinking whisky. It’s a
poor heart that never rejoices. Glory be,
it might have turned out worse.” I did
not go. My fishing luck is cruel.
“A.H.8.” : Of all the thousands of
methods of starting a jibbing horse that have
come my way, the most effective I know is to
pour cold water on the stubborn equine’s rump.
I first saw it tried on the Mossman, in North
Queensland. The cocky who made the experi-
ment had the swiftest drive of his life during the
next ten minutes. Let some stuck-up Aboites try
this scheme, and report progress and the velocity
thereof.
* * * *
“ Bushie ” from a very far out part of
North Queensland: —
I have, just read your article on “The Northern Terri-
tory and other Large Matters ” (B. 4/n/’O9), be-
sides others in former issues. Keep right on, rub it in,
insist, insist. High Protection, compulsory military
training, Australian navy, immigration, and close settle-
ment. The damphools who run this show must be made
to see our danger as you appear to do. I live on the
verge of it. Twenty-one years in the Gulf and N. Terri-
tory, and I know the emptiness of this vast land. I never
heard “Gor’ Save” or “ Soldiers of the Queen ” once
during the Boer row. I have not seen a dozen men
together, or a railway line, or a steamer since 1896, nor
have I slept in house, pub, or shanty during that time,
and I’ve not stopped in the one place either by a long
chalk.
Those facts will help you to see the emptiness of the
country. Much of these wastes (?) would he excellent
rice-producing districts, and the Chow and Jap know it.
Any tropical plant would grow in many parts ; and the
mineral country is only scratched.
Go stronger ! Advocate free agricultural holdings for
a period of 100 years all along the coast country —with
personal residence. Get the population in the country
and we can hold it.
“ L’Fou ” : “ Huffin-Guffin ” writes re the
vanishing Glenelg (11/3/’O9), and has dark sus-
picions about the ffiling-up of other Australian
rivers. There is ground for those suspicions. A
stream under my notice (Tarwin River, Gipps-
land) seems to have the same disease. Years ago
there were many diving and swimming holes along
its course. Now you’ll strike sand or mud (if you
don’t strike a snag) at a depth of 3ft. anywhere
(summer level). As this trickle used to run
through dense forest (it is nearly all cleared now),
“ Huffin’’ is probably right in his theory about
the bank-binding virtue of the timber. The de-
cayed branches, etc., of slaughtered trees in the
river bed are also active agents in collecting soil
and debris and thus clogging the channels. Verily
the tree-murderer has a lot to answer for. By the
way, does “ Huffey ” know what becomes of the
lost water, anyhow ?

“Henry Phelthafcfc ” : People who send
their nearly grown-up offspring into the world
without a knowledge of the relative values of
right and left bowers deserve to be smitten
heavily with the vocabulary. Moreover, it is
time that the information was imparted in our
public schools. Consider the awkward position
in which young men reared in ignorance of our
national game are placed. I remember once, at
the Black Plains Hotel, we could only muster
three. A stranger was lying on the couch. My
mate twisted round on his oil drum. “ Make up
the corner in a game of euchre, ole chap ? ” he
said. The poor fellow fidgeted uncomfortably.
“Can’t play,” he confessed feebly. My mate
was so taken back’at the man’s ignorance that he
forgot to choose his words. I’m sure he didn’t
mean to hurt the feelings of the young fellow.
“Can’t play?” he repeated. “Gawd love me,
where ’ave you bin dragged up ? ”
KEEPERS OF THE KEYS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
By the scattered lights on the northern capes.
Where the tramps go to and fro ;
By the windy cliffs of the eastern gates,
Where the wild cross-currents flow ;
And the shifting sands of the southern bights.
Where the roaring tempests blow ;
By these, and the western scarps of stone.
With their waters white and green,
And the billowing hills of eucalypt
In the land that lies between—
We have made a pact with the white-skinned race
Whereby we shall keep it clean.
We are sworn to this for the white-skinned race.
At home and across the seas,
To keep the blood from a dingy taint
As we guard it against disease ;
Of a right we are strict at the seaboard gates
Of a right, for we keep the kej's.
We have brothers out where the four winds go
Brothers, and next-of-kin,
Of various speech and divers minds
Yet all of them white of skin.
They find no bickering at our doors ;
We nod, and we pass them in.
Of a right we shall keep our country clean ;
Of a right we shall keep it white ;
We hold it in fee for our clean-limbed sons,
From the Gulf to the stormy Bight.
We may not barter the future’s claims,
And if we must fight, we fight 1
So this is the pact from the Eastern gates
Across to the Western seas,
And through from the Gulf to the stormy Bight,
The pact that our race decrees—
That we shall be strict at the nation’s door
Of a right, for we hold the keys !
VICTORIA.
C. G. A. COLLES.

taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, xohether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.

An Aged Man’s Hideous Escapade at the Victorian Home
for the Aged and Poor, Royal Park, April 8,1900.
A veteran sailor climbed up
On the garbled roof of the Home,
And along the parapets and tiles
Like a cat did roam.
The warders endeavored to capture
The daring climber bold,
Clad only in his nightshirt—
Upon the roof in the cold.
Letting forth a loud yell, like
A wild animal in pain,
The eccentric old joker
Scrambled down again.
He had not forgotten
His rough, dare devil ways—
When afloat on the deep—
In his hardy young days.

thusiastic for print Ejayem : There are holes in your
argument. (1) State ownership and State leaseholds may
be all wrong if the State leases the land to Fat Men in
large areas at a nominal rent. (2) Private ownership may
be all right if there are rigid laws to prevent any owner
possessing more than a moderate area, and to give every
tenant the right to purchase at a moderate price. In
France, Belgium, and Denmark, despite private owner-
ship, the laws have created such a subdivision of land that
the process can hardly go any further and yet leave the
farmer a living area (in Belgium they have got the living
area down so fine that more than half the farms don’t
exceed 2£ acres). The Northern Territory is almost
entirely State-owned land, yet there you find stupendous
estates larger than a German principality', leased for two
generations at less rent per square mile than the
Belgian farmer pays in taxes for his 2J acres.
You can make either system a curse or a bless-
ing, just according to how it is administered...

The two handles are connected with a pump
(not seen by the visitor,), which supplies water
for the Governor’s shower-bath. The above
illustrations explain the modus operandl more
lucidly than could a whole library of print.
The visitor, by a slight effort, gets a Vice-Royal smile

forms an act of real utility.
Intensely loyal citizen, who gave £175,000 to the

handles after pumping 18gals. of liquid loyalty.
And instead of such a simple and business-like contrivance
as this, we have the Exs. shaking 1000 people by the hand,
and worn to rags at the end of the ceremony.

“ Jacq ” : Joe Bible was the appropriate
name of one of the bookmakers at the JJaylesford
(Vic.) Paster sports. It was hot and dusty, and

spiration of the sacred volume were set at rest.
Mr. James Harry Peacock, father of the
Victorian ex-Premier, Alexander the Kookaburra,
celebrated his 80th birthday last week. Alexander
didn t inherit the laugh from his parent. He in-
vented it.
The Himpenal things that British states-
men do live after them : the good is oft interred
with their bones—in a Marc Antony way of
speaking. On the tenth anniversary of Gladstone’s
death his statue in the London Strand attracted
just one little memorial wreath from an unknown
rememberer; whereas Beaconsfield, the father of
Jingoism, who died in 1881, draws more floral
tributes to his statue on every anniversary of his
death than a fashionable prima-donna on the first
night of a new opera. The one premier
Dia for the patronage of prosperous pos-
terity ; the other didn’t. Benjaman
rseaconsneld was a Jew whose foreign
policy was ever shaped in the interests of
Cohen, and whose home policy was directed
to the safeguarding of hereditary Fat.
All Jews admire his memory, and Fat
both Jew and Gentile, can afford to be
generous to it. Besides, he didn’t make a
special feature of his personal and political
integrity, like Gladstone, of whom
Babouchere said : “I don’t blame the old
fox for keeping an ace up his sleeve, but
I do object to his always pretending that
God Almighty put it there.” Now posterity
m the bulk refuses to canonise any poli-
tician who trimmed his own hat with a
halo.
Died in Paris recently, aged 85,
the Comtesse de Chabrillan, better known
as Celeste Mogador, who had been away
from the public eye for a very dragged-out
period. About GO years ago she was one of
the stars at the Bel Mamille, and after-
wards appeared in Parisian circuses and
music hails. In 1854 Lionel de Moreton,
Comte de Chabrillan, became infatuated
with her, and married her. When he was
appointed I rench Consul in jVlelbourne
she came out with him, and lived in the
Victorian capital for some time. Subse-
quently she wrote several plays and novels,
winch didn’t attract much notice, but when
she set about writing her memoirs the
1 ( rench Government interfered and pro-
hibited their publication. Celeste, appar-
ently, knew too much.

series. Donaldson’s Government lasted 80 days
Cowper followed and lasted 37 days, and Parker
came next and hungiout something over U months.

Chair of Volubility in the Vic. State Parliament,
Martin Hannah, member for (Jollingwood, would
be returned unopposed. No one would dare to
nominate against him. If anyone did, he would
lose his deposit. Martin’s garrulity is an eternal
affliction. On every subject, at all" times, and at
every opportunity, Martin rises and has a sav as
long as the dreams of youth. He out-yells" the
average gale with ease, Martin has one rival in
loquacity in the place wherein the multitude of
councillors means muddle— i.e., McCutcheon, of
bt. Hilda. But though McCutcheon comes next,
the ship of his conversation is hull down on the
horizon in Martin Hannah's wake.
.Ramsay Smith, President of the S.A.
1 üblic Health Hoard, says there is so much work
waiting to be done in Australia that no
offer would tempt him back to Great Britain.
He expressed regret that Prof. Bragg, after
speaking so vigorously concerning the need
and the scope for scientific research work
here, should have succumbed to the first
big offer from an English Uuiversity. Ram-
say Smith is Premier Price’s great stand-
by ; the Government consults him about
numberless things. And Ramsay Smith
really does know a lot about numberless
things. At Edinburgh he took prizes in the
arts, science, and medical courses ; was pre-
sident of the University Philosophical
Society, assistant Professor of Natural His-
tory, and Examiner in Zoology and Geology ;
and he was' examiner for degrees in medi-
cine and surgery for the Edinburgh College
of Surgeons and Physicians. Also he is a
Colonel in the Army Medical Corps, a
Fellow of the Scotch Educational Institute,
a musician, and he sat as an expert on the
Scotch Fisheries Commission.
Detective Roche, Acting-Chief of
N.S.W. force, is a firm believer m football as
an aid to the detection of crime. Men who
are wanted may dodge detection for months
at other periods of the year—even big cricket
won’t bring them out. But with the foot-
ball season their caution and cunning relax,
and they emerge on a Saturday afternoon;
and presently they forget their disguises
and yell m their natural voices. Also they per-
spire so profusely that it is only necessary, at
the interval, to hand them a scrap of newspaper
with a football paragraph on it, and ask their
views on the person who wrote it, and a thumb-
print can be secured right away.
Mentioned that Bishop Pain, of Gipps-
land (Vic.), may succeed to the Archbishopric of
Sydney. Bishop Pain was formerly incumbent at
Cobbity (N.S.W.), then for some years he shep-
herded at St. John’s, Darlinghurst, and in 1902
he was elected to toil in the episcopal vineyard of
Gippsland.
The recent decease of William McCulloch,
of Melbourne, left an orihce in the directorate of
the Melbourne Tramway and Omnibus' Co., and
W. G. Sprigg, secretary to the co., has resigned
his ancient job in order to-fill it. Sprigg has
been in the tramway business for about 40 years,
and was secretary to the old Melbourne Omnibus
Co. before he pushed a secretarial pen on behaif
of the present concern. Personally, Sprigg is
well preserved, dapper and irreproachable; he
affects the kind of glasses which balance pre-
cariously on the nose, and wears a sensational pair
of unbridled side-whiskers; and is altogether of
that type that does everything that is respectable,
and nothing that is “ un-British.” Years ago he
was said to be the idol of all tram men, but,
fortunately, the disgraceful libel was killed before
it had time to take root.
“ Westerner ” writes :
Staniforth Smith, of Papua, understands the gentle art
of advertising. When he isn’t dining with cannibals (in
the daily papers) he is shooting excessively large animals,
or diving for pearls. His latest advertisement relates to
some remarkable walking feat. The Administrator is a
bustling man of affairs who doesn’t believe in hiding his
light in a cellar. When he was Mayor of Kalgoorlie
(W. A.) he got more press notices than G. Reid or Tommy
Burns. Then he stood for the Senate in 1901, and flabber-
gasted his opponents with full-page advertisements in
every little rag in the West. It paid, too, for Smith
topped the poll, though he was the last man to take the
field. As soon as he became Senator, the enterprising
Staniforth proceeded to reach out for a better job. He
toured the outlying regions of the Commonwealth, where
the nigger grows wild, and in due course was rewarded
with his present picturesque billet. All the same, Stani-
forth is a smart fellow, and would have made a fortune at
book-canvassing if he hadn’t taken to politics.

“ 0.K.” from Melbourne :
It was a staggerer to many people to read in The Bul-
letin (8/4/’O9) that “ Leonard Grim wood, who died in Mel-
bourne last week,” was “ a champion pigeon slaughterer,
a dabster at driving trotters, something of a boxer, and
more of a billiardist,” and “ one of the best-known
sportsmen in Murray’s capital.” As a matter of fact, tins
Len Grimwood, who, seemingly, is now dead, was known
to the sporting world of Melbourne as a curious old nuis-
ance. He haunted Kirk's Bazaar in Bourke-street, and
the pavement thereof. His claim to publicity lay partly
in the tradition of his having had something to do with
trotting horses in the long- ago ere he settled down to
chronic us< lessness, hut mainly in his relationship
to brother Sam Grimwood and their father before them.
His champion pigeon potting and other achievements
must have been performed in the writer’s mind. In short,
Len Grimwood was not even a has-been in sport. He can
be truthfully described as a never-was. The obituary
notice of Len was correct enough in its reference to his
brother Sun, who migrated from Melbourne to Westralia
some years back. Sam was a bookmaker in a large way,
and a horseouner in a small way during the ’Bo’s. His
personality commanded respect, and what time he was
chairman of Bowes’ Tattersall’s Club in Melbourne, he
ranked as boss billiardist at the Victorian Club. Sam, in
his prime, was an all-round sport as well as a bookmaker.
He beg-an as a jockey, and was earning a livelihood in the
saddle when his father, the original Tom Grimwood, kept
the Rose, Shamrock and Thistle tlotel in Bourke-street,
fully 40 years ago. The old man made a lot of
money as a building contractor in the first
place, and subsequently as a pub-keeper and
general speculator. P rhaps lie also lost some of it
in the roaring Ws and ’7o’s, hut, anyway, he was a man
of brains and enterprise, was Tom Grimwood, and a capi-
tal pigeon-shot in the days when ruthless murder of
trapped blue-rocks came under the heading of justifiable
birdicide. T"m did his best to start a new industry in
Melbourne. He discovered, or dreamed of discovering,
gold at Collingwood Plat, where his friend, John Ander-
son, had opened a pub. As promoter and chairman of
the Collingwood Plat Gold Mining Co. he labored hard in
flannel shirt and moleskins to attract a rush to Oolling-
wood, and for a time Anderson’s pub paid splendid divi-
dends on the capital invested. Collingwood Plat, how-
ever, was found to be non-auriferous country, and Tom
Grimwood’s mine fell into disrepute. It was shrewdy
suspected of being a delusion and a spoof.
THE RESERVE FORCE.
The Lord Mayor of Sydney said he was determined that
in this part of the world we should show our intention
to support the Empire.
LORD MAYOR: “By-an’-bye your pa will come
out of the pub; an’, remember, you’re got to
support him safely past that wicked man
round the corner.”
SWINBURNE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Despite Swinburne’s expressed desire that no burial
service should be read at his grave, the rector of Ben-
ch inch lead passages from the service, to the great indi***-
nation of some of the mourners.— Cable from, London
April 15. ’
Thy kingdom, shall pass , Galilean ; thy dead shall go
clown to thee de&cl.—Hymn to Proserpine.
Dead earth upon dead clay !
The spirit passed away
Cares not what droning
Voice plies with nasal zest
Its trade above his rest—
Chaunting, intoning !
In coffin and in urn
The dead have naught to spurn
Nor mouth for laughter ;
Green bud and withered leaf
Bring neither joy nor grief
To him hereafter.
He is the Dead, who goes
Outward where leaf to rose
Blossometh never ;
But pallid Proserpine
Leans, and with lips divine
Ends all endeavor.
No need to chide or chafe
For him who now lies safe
From scorn or weeping;
Let the poor, zealous rite
Beat vainly on the night
Where he is sleeping.
Vainly beside this ground—
Bent to the scourge, thorn-crowned,
Your gods, time-smitten,
Themselves would need to pray ;
Are you more strong than they,
Rector of Britain ?
Out in the darkness far
That knows no sombre star
Nor failing paean,
Vainly your mumbled creed
Shall bid him now take heed,
Old Galilean 1
Ends in unending night '
All sorrow, all delight
Tasted, untasted ;
The hymn your mild mouth sings
Ends with all other things
Dried, spent and wasted.
He shall not start nor stare
For all the troublous prayer
Around him pleading.
Your tongue shall fall and rust,
Your lips be blown to dust.
He lies unheeding.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Tasmania, the smallest and sleepiest of the Six
Sisters, arrives last with its revenue returns. The com-
plete Commonwealth figures for the first nine months of
the past two financial years are as under :
Nine Months’ States Revenue.
N. S. Wales .. £10,660,402 £10,151,796 Dec. £508,606
Victoria.. .. 6,312,495 6,113,276 Dec. 199,219
Queensland .. 3,283,012 3,395,326 Inc. 112,314
S. Australia .. 2,705,789 2,576,926 Dec. 128,863
Westralia .. 2,485,396 2,343,706 Dec. 141,690
Tasmania .. 757,457 692,915 Dec. 64,542
Tasmania’s contribution to the general anguish is very
creditable considering its size ; it suggests that the Island
of Dreams and Dodery could do an impressive deficit if it
were a little larger. The figures for March show a shrink-
age of £8554 as compared with last year. Income Tax
returned £1224 less, and “Other Receipts” £7596 less
than in 1908. Land Tax panned out £1065 for 1909, as
against £5ll in March, 1908 ; and Railways earned £25,799
—or £lB6O more than last year. “Sale and rental of
Crown lands ” realised £4443—£573 more than 1908.
Commonwealth refund was £25,081, which is £1969 less
than the pocket money handed out for the corresponding
period of last year. The five Sisters surrounding solvent
Queensland make a rather low-spirited group.

The National Bank, which led a rather quiet and ordinary
life for a number of years, makes a great expansion this
half. The expansion seems almost too great to be quite
real, and the idea arises that in recent periods the insti-
tution has been applying some of its profits to the
writing-off of the last remains or final tail end of the old
depreciation, and that, having now concluded this pro-
cess, it is able to let its visible profits loose, after the
manner of a released balloon, and being let loose they
immediately proceed to rise. This, of course, is mere
conjecture, and is advanced as such. Sixteen years ago
(on May 1, 1893) the National Bank temporarily sus-
pended, through the sins of others, and it is interesting
to note some of the changes since then :
Half-year to Half-year to
March, 1893. Sept'., 1909. Increase.
Capital £1,000,000 £1,498,220 £498,220
Gross Profits .. .. 123,529 137,383 13,854
Net Profits .. .. 51,456 59,170 7,714
Addition to Reserves 1,456 21,715 20,259
Decrease.
Reserves .. .. 600,488 207,975 392,513
Dividend .. .. 50,000 37,455 12,545
It may thus be safely asserted that in every way the
National Bank of Australasia is stronger than it was
before the unmerited disaster of 16 years ago. Its
capital is larger. So are its gross profits and its net
profits; it is more cautious over its dividend, and it makes
a far larger addition to its reserves than it did in the old
times. Certainly the reserves are smaller on paper, but
there is some reason to suppose that they are real. Sixteen
years ago, by reason of insane public borrowing, and the
insane land booming of a multitude of bogus land banks
and other disreputable institutions, all values were so
hopelessly fraudulent that no reserves were wholly real.
As regards another aspect of the case, the
National Bank makes a good showing:—
Net Profits in Proportion to Shareholders’ Funds
(Capital and Reserves).

Mar., 1907.. 517 1 ~ Mar., 1909.. 619 7 „
It is a very handsome increase, and taken into considera-
tion with the expansion in other directions, as shown by
the next table, which has taken place during the last 12
months, it is still more notable :

The advance in liquid assets is worthy of special con-
sideration. The National Bank’s liquid assets now re-
present about 37 per cent, of all its liabilities to the
public, which is a guarantee which should satisfy even a
fairly exacting creditor.

Tne Colonial Bank had a hard row to hoe after the great
disaster of 1893. It was never a boom bank like the
Commercial of Australia under the Gyles Turner manage-
ment, yet it had to suffer for the sins of others. But
good and steady management brought about a great
recovery, and to-day the Colonial is one of the most re-
spected institutions in Victoria. Its profits are not exactly
sensational, but they are at least respectable.
Profits Per Cent, on Shareholders’ Capital and
Reserves.
Sept., 1905.. £7 1 6 p.c. Sept., 1907.. £7 2 4 p.c.
Mar., 1906.. 7 711 „ Mar., 1908.. 8 7 2
Sept., 1906.. 7 6 9,, Sept., 1908.. 8 6 4,,
Mar., 1907.. 8 3 8 „ Mar., 1909.. 8 7 9 „
The Colonial Bank used to be noted for cutting its cash
reserves rather fine ; it had a yearning to have the money
out earning interest, and seemed to regard the coin that
lay in the strong room to be security against a sudden
emergency as so much interest wasted. But just now
there is an uneasy feeling that the recent burst of pros-
perity may have seen its best days ; the slump in copper
and lead, the conviction that the drought must be almost
due again, and the hazy war scare that hangs persist-
ently around the premises, all point in the same direc-
tion. Therefore it is gratifying to note that the Colonial
B.mk has made a great increase in the amount of its
liquid assets as a preparation for any possible emergency.
The figures which follow are evidence on this aspect of
the case :
Mar., 1908. Mar., 1909.

The liquid assets now' represent about 34 per cent, of all
liabilities to the public, which may be regarded as a very
safe and reasonable figure.

The rise of the Royal Bank of Australia has been a really
wonderful circumstance. It was a very small, young,
fresh bank —a baby bank, in fact —when the smash hap-
pened in 1893. It was then the only bank with its head
office in Melbourne which didn’t suspend, and brutal
people said that it only escaped because it was so small
that its flurried shareholders couldn’t find it. But the
Royal, which had only one office and was, as already
mentioned, quite a baby bank, only smiled and went on
the even tenor of its way. It paid no dividends for some
years, but that was inevitable. It also wrote off some
capital, but that was unavoidable at a time when boom
values were disappearing like smoke. But it pegged in
steadily; it might be said, without straining the truth,
that it bogged in. The management was excellent, and
the solidity' of the little institution came to be recog-
nised. When it issued new shares to replace the
capital that had been lost it got a big premium. Its
reserves are now a good many times larger than they'
were before the 1893 disaster —in fact, they’ nearly repre-
sent all the capital that was lost about that time ; and
the dividend is larger than it ever was in the old boom
days. Also the bank begins to reach out for business in
places where it was formerly unknown. Some further
particulars are appended :
Mar., 1908. Mar., 1909.
Notes in circulation .. £13,036 £14,121 Inc., £1,085
Bills in circulation, etc. 152,742 36,305 Dec., 116,437
Depositsatinterest .. 779,022 813,524 Inc., 34,502
Free deposits .. .. 493,512 469,214 Dec., 24,298
Liquid assets .. .. 651,656 566,278 Dec., 85,378
Advances .. ..1,020,326 1,130,257 Inc., 109,931
Premises, etc 66,287 66,289 Inc., 2
The fact that the liquid assets have decreased and that
the advances have greatly expanded, shows that the Royal
Bank, in common with many others, lias found that there
is a steadily increasing demand for money, and has done
its share towards meeting the demand. Yet the liquid
assets represent some 42 per cent, of all liabilities to the
public, so the position of the Royal is unusually strong.
The profits, as compared with capital and reserve fund,
show a slight diminution, but for that the recent large
increase of capital may be fairly regarded as responsible.
It takes a little time for the returns from new capital to
show themselves.
Profits Per Cent, on Shareholders’ Capital and

The profits are distinctly above the average. Also, the
proportion of profits carried to reserves is much greater
than in the case of most banking institutions.

These results are exceedingly good in every respect. The
Bank of Adelaide, which was never among the recon-
structs, is now far larger, stronger and more prosperous
than at any previous period in its career. It has always
been a reputable institution ; even in the worst of the
boom days it side-stepped the breath of suspicion in a
marvellous fashion. And all the time it continues to
grow. The latest report shows one new branch and two
agencies added to its ramifications.
Some further details are as follows :
Mar., 1908. Mar., 1909.
Notes in circulation ..£185,540 £195,802 Inc. £10,262
Bills in circulation, etc. 297,452 273,343 Dec. 24,109
Deposits .. .. 3,090,618 3,524,813 Inc. 434,195
Liquid assets .. ..2,226,230 2,299,971 Inc. 73,741
Advances .. ..2,006,688 2,573,465 Inc. 566,777
Premises .. .. 84,707 81,230 Dec. 3,477
Deposits have increased considerably ; but advances
have bounded ahead a little faster, and are in front by
£132,582. The £73,741 addition to liquid assets is a good
way behind the £163,259 of the ’O6-7 comparison. Still
the liquid assets represent nearly 54 per cent,
of all liabilities to the public and the Govern-
ment, which is something so amazingly above
the average that any' further addition would be
superfluous. Part of these assets—“ money at short
call, £710,000” —may represent Government funds
which are liable to early withdrawal, but even if
this item were left out the position would still look
remarkably strong. The Bank of Adelaide holds
£724,920 in Government securities alone, which
about equals its paid-up capital (£400,000) and reserve
fund (£325,000) put together. The year’s profits repre-
sent almost exactly 10 per cent, on the average of the
shareholders’ funds (capital and reserves). Upon all
these and various other matters the institution has every
right to congratulate itself.

The Bendigo Co-operative Society, rising again from the
sea of disappearance with its balance-sheet in its mouth,
has nothing very cheerful to relate. The latest accounts
cover only five months, which partly explains the
fact that it takes so much less money to pay a 5 per cent,
dividend than it did at August, 1906. The other reason
is that in the interval the paid capital has dropped from
£7635 to £7lOB. With a net profit of £7B, after paying
for depreciation, and the guaranteed dividend, the pay-
ment of the ordinary Is. in the £ bonus to purchasers
was plainly impossible. The following table shows some
things that have dropped and some that haven’t:—
August, January,
1906. 1909.
Mortgage .. .. £B5O £B5O
Owing to Merchants .. 4498 7,148 Increase £2650
Overdraft (less cash).. 14 1,384 ~ 1370
Stock 8523 10,536 „ 2013
Due by Customers .. 3895 5,162 ~ 1267
Capital .v .. .. 7635 7,108 Decrease 527
It isn’t a good showing. There are less profits, less sales,
less bonus, less capital, more owing to merchants, more
owing to the bank, more stock on hand to eat its head off
in interest, more due by customers (which means more
liability to bad debts), and a stationary mortgage.
The main trouble seems to be that the co-
operators won’t co-operate. There are 1393 shareholders
in the society, yet the grocery department, with the
second largest tally of customers, had only 1067 —“many
of whom are not shareholders.” Bread attracts 1418 ; but,
with the grocery figures in view, it would be vain to
assume that the number includes all the shareholders.
The drapery, with 590 customers, and the boots with 593,
are the two places the shareholder seems to stay away from
most consistently. The chairman said he thought it was
“senseless folly that people should put money into a
society and then let it dry rot.” No reason appears to be
forthcoming as to why the shareholders withhold their
custom. The bread and boots show “fair profits”; the
grocery and drapery are on the debit side. It is the first
time grocery has shown a loss. Also £258, which the
society might have collected in discounts if it had had
the means to pay cash, was lost because it hadn’t the
means to pay cash. The bakery department, which is
still paying, is expected to be a losing concern next half-
year owing to the rise in the price of flour. If this
happens there will apparently be only boots to rely upon.
It is a sad story of co-operation gone wrong.

Of the half-year’s profits, £25,000 has been set aside as
provision for rebuilding and depreciation of securities.
This represents an increase of £lo,oooover the usual h ..f-
-yearly appropriation for this item; however, for t.m
period just ended the regular £lO,OOO addition to me
reserve for the equalisation of dividends has been
dropped. This fund now stands at the even £lOO,OOO,
and as the populace seem to be more enthusiastic than
ever about beer, and the profits show no signs of shrink-
ing, the directorate have put it aw'ay for the brick that
moulders and the stock that slumps. The total of
£279,080 is thus made up of plain ordinary reserve,
£lOO,OOO ; reserve for equalisation of dividends, £lOO,OOO ;
reserve for rebuilding and depreciation, £50,000; and
undivided profit, £29,080. The usual £5OOO has been
written off to provide for depreciation of plant and
machinery. The profits continue to climb upward, and
the present half-year is £3Ol above the previous high-
water mark of March, ’OB. Another view of the situation
is this :

As will be noticed, “profits on brewing, etc.,” has hopped
up £2272 since last year ; and, when you compare it with
the £132,377 of 1900, you are inclined to agree with the
hard-working comedian who remarked, “ Beer is money.”
No explanation of the tremendous increase is given—
simply the figures are produced. The co.’s debt to the
public has moved up to the extent of £9680, and “ proper-
ties, goodwill, plant, etc.,” have increased by j. 23,044.
The following comparison puts March, ’OO, shoulder to
shoulder with March, ’O9 :

The liquid assets have increased by approximately 50 per
cent, in the short space of nine years ; and all the time
the dividends have been falling out of the beer-pump
with a regularity beautiful to behold. The only peg
whereon the carping critic can hang his hat is the intangi-
ble thing in Table 11. called” Goodwill.” It is impossible
to gauge its size, as it is tangled up with properties and
plant. The amount ought to be (1) stated, (2) written off.

This co. seems at last to have arrived at the conclusion
that the game isn’t worth the interest. The’oB report
breathed a faint hope that the directors would be able to
“ make satisfactory arrangements for the works to be
revenue-producing during the coming season, although,
owing to the continued absence of rain, the prospect at
the present time is not encouraging.” Now the ’O9
report arrives with the information that the hopes of last
year came to nothing ; likewise it states “ the funds of
the co. being practically exhausted, arrangements have
been made with the debenture-holders whereby sufficient
money has been advanced by them to meet current ex-
penses until the works can be either leased for the
coming season or disposed of.” Judging by the co.’s
experience, the chances of the directorate doing
either are not rosy. The £15,000 worth of deben-
tures which constitutes the skeleton on the roof
of the unemployed works were originally payable in
1906, but were extended to 1909 ; apparently, the holders
are not agreeable to renew for another term, but will help
the co. to hang out until the possible purchaser happens
along'. The co. does not appear to have ever paid a divi-
dend ; of late years ic has earned half a crust now and
then by leasing the works ; but the nominal reserves have
been dwindling pretty steadily for about seven years,
until the management is now constrained to admit that
things have arrived at bedrock. An illuminating sentence
of the report is that wherein it is stated the interest on
the debentures for the year ending March, ’O9, has not be
paid ; you can’t induce the mereiy-human debenture-
holder to go away and call again in another three years
under such circumstances. However, itisdoubtful whether
there would be any profit in so doing. The record of the
last few years is mostly rust and decay ; and the outlook
—so far as can be seen—is more decay and more rust.
Tiie plain bald statement that they are going to try and
sell the concern would seem to indicate that those in-
terested are weary of sitting in the wilderness waiting for
a good season to send the flocks and herds clattering up
to the front door like the live stock of Noah immediately
prior to the Deluge. In the dim, remote future, the way-
back freezing works may be a paying business; but it is
a future too faint and far away for the man who has in-
terest to meet.
“ H.G.”: An eloquent commentary on the pre-
sent control of fish throughout the Eastern States is the
estimate of one authority in respect to the catches of the
Federal trawler. So far the Endeavor has done splen-
didly. The returns, considering the time the trawler has
been absent from port, have be. n equal to those of the
North Sea —the richest fishing ground in the world —but
so utterly crooked and badly organised has the fish supply
of Melbourne and Sydney become that it would not pay a
private company to start trawling even on this basis.
What, for instance, is the reason why the people of
Sydney do not obtain barracouta for a few pence each, as
do those of Melbourne? The'couta is a blessing to the
poor residents of the southern city, where a fish big
enough to provide a meal for a family of five may
be bought for 3d. or 4d. Granting that the
’couta is a coarse fish, still there are many who
like it. Even if the ’couta were retailed at 6d. it'would
provide Surry Hills, and Pyrmont, and Botany with cheap
food, and should leave a fair margin of profit after pay-
ing carriage from Melbourne, for it must be remembered
that 4d. is the maximum retail price on the hawker’s
barrows in Melbourne, and is charged only after much
middle profit has been paid. To start with, the Fisher-
men’s Union of Queenscliff, where the 'couta are mostly
caught, restricts the catch to a boatload a day. When
two members of the union have filled their boat
they have to knock off work, even though the
sea is stiff with the fish, the object being- to main-
tain a uniform price in the Melbourne market. This
restriction means a lot. In certain seasons the supply
of ’couta is practically illimitable, and 10 or 12
boatloads a day could be caught by each pair. On top
of this, the Queenscliff railway service does not suit the
times of supply, and the fishermen have to send their
catches to Melbourne by the bay excursion steamers,
which have a monopoly of the business, and charge mono-
polist freights. In the city, the union does its own
auctioneering, but it was shown a few years back that
when fish did not bring a certain price, the union office
had a way of destroying the surplus rather than allow
the price to come down in open competition. All things
considered, it is probable that ’couta could, at times, be
sold in Melbourne and made to pay at Id. apiece, while
the maximum should never be more than the 4d. now
charged. There are occasionally outcries about restraint
of trade; but, in respect to the ’couta supply of
Melbourne there is nothing but restraint of fair trade on
the part of everybody who handles the fish. Private
Enterprise could not inaugurate a system of ’couta supply
to Sydney, owing to the fear that one of the other links
in the chain would turn dog. The States of N.S.W. and
Victoria might organise a Fish Department, headed by a
Minister for 'Oouta, but they are too busy yapping at the
Commonwealth to trouble about the wants of their
people. Only the Commonwealth itself is left, and as,
sooner or later, the pqp er must be obtained to supply
the catches of the Federal trawler to the people of the
seaboard, it should at the same time consider the possi-
bility of reducing- the excessive cost of living to the
poorer residents of Sydney by bringing the r couta to their
doors. If the trawler itself happens across a ’couta
ground close to Sydney so much the better.
* ’* m *
The recent Labor Congress showed no super-
fluous wisdom when it carried a clamorous motion for the
abolition of first-class carriages on the railways. The
reason assigned was that the second-class carriages re-
turn all the revenue, while the firsts are merely a non-
paying concession to the bloated rich, which looks a very
impressive statement until it is reduced to figures. Then
a certain doubt arises. For instance, during the year
ended 30th June, 1907, first-class passengers in N.S.W.
paid £503,555, and second-class passengers £984,124. To
earn this money there were employed 204 first-class and
411 second-class carriages ; also a miscellaneous welter of
special cars, sleeping cars, composite cars, and brake vans,
which last, in some cuses, are also partly passenger cars.
This welter it isn’t very easy 7 to sort out. But taking the
number of plain first and second class vehicles, it cer-
tainly seems that the first-class box on wheels earns its
bread and cheese quite as well as the second. As to the
story about first-class carriages being empty while their
less luxurious brethren are crowded, anybody who sees the
morning suburban trains come into Sydney' with even the
platforms of the first-class vehicles crowded, knows better.
Even if the first-class were used solely by the enemies of
the worker, there is no reason why these enemies should
not be permitted to pay for luxury in railway travelling
any more than for luxury in food or clothes or public
entertainment. To be logical, the Congress should pass
a resolution deploring the existence of any other than
sixpenny meals, and call upon Prime Minister Fisher to
reduce the accommodation at the Hotel Australia to the
level of the Salvation Army Refuge. Further, it should
demand that the theatre should be all gallery at Is. ad-
mission money. This idea of demanding that the bloated
capitalist should be compelled to travel cheaply so that
he may save money and bloat further seems an ill-judged
business.
* * * *
Nowadays when Solemn Lee, N.S.W. Minister
for Works, is met bv a deputation which wants a railway,
he works off a dreary'gag to the effect that “pending a
settlement of the financial relations between the Com-
monwealth and the States ” nothing can be done. This
means, of course, that the Wade-Lee Government is
seeking to raise a g-reat popular clamor against the
financial proposals of the Australian Ministry, and it
begins by' refusing new railways (which it would probably
have refused in any case) and hinting that they would be
granted but for the Federal Government, and that they
may' yet be granted if the Federal Government proposals
are knocked out. It is a mean dodge, and a thin one.
When N.S.W. was a separate State with a small revenue,
it built its railways out of loans. When it become a
Federated State with a greatly-increased revenue, it
built its railways out of loans. Under its hard-borrow-
ing State Premiers revenue has nothing to do with
railways. And these people whose line was refused last
week on the strength of the old gag should note that the
Wade-Lee Government has undertaken to pay, if neces-
sary out of the State Treasury, about £1,000,000 for a
Dreadnought. If that matter didn’t require to wait
“ pending a settlement of the financial relations between
the Commonwealth and the States,” a little back-blocks
railway need not wait either.

P’ran (Vic.) Council is the hero of what a daily
paper calls “an adventure in municipal economy'.” A
while ago it erected a rubbish destructor for the disposal
of the local garbage ; and as the heat was all going to
waste, the council decided to have it turned into elec-
tricity. Thereupon the council erected the necessary
plant and turned out electricity', which, by a masterly-
stroke of diplomacy, it sold to a Private Enterprise
electric light co. at less than Id. a unit. Meantime, the
same co. sells back the same electricity in the form of
light to the council at something like four times the price.
So the council sells its electricity at, say, a copper
to show the intelligence of its municipal economy,
and buys it back at about fourpence—apparently to show
the economy' of its municipal intelligence.

The Callow Revolving Band Screen gives
promise of revolutionising the system of ore classification
in Australia, as it has already done in America, for all
classes and conditions of concentrating ores, with vastly
improved extraction in the mills. At Broken Hill it has
been especially successful. It is an unqualified success as a
fine wet screen, and this is what all the mining world has
been seeking; it is also equally effective on the coarse rafts
from Cornish Rolls. With it there is no excessive wear, no
clogging of the mesh ; it has an enormous capacity and a
small water consumption. The fundamental principle of
this machine is a travelling band or belt of screen cloth
stretched on a drum over which the ore and its carrying
water is spread by means of a distributing apron of
special design. The drum passes over rollers, and the
belt is caused to revolve continuously at a speed adapted
to the nature and quantity of the material to be screened.
The machine is fully protected V>y patent rights. For
further particulars apply to the Austral Otis Engineering
Co., of Hanna Street, South Melbourne. This firm are the
manufacturers and sole agents for Australasia.* [2

dusty little township on the cattle plains of Ohinemun ;
to-day it is a prosperous city'. But, b.v the irony of fate,
not one of the surviving original prospectors of the pro-
perty retains a pennyworth of interest in it. When first
worked in the surface levels of Martha Hill the main ore
body was believed to be unpayable. There was then no
cyanide treatment, no method of dealing profitably with
low-grade ores, and the owners were finally glad to let
the mine on tribute. Even the tributers could not make
it pay, and the property was purchased by Thomas Henry'
Russell for £3OOO. To date this immense low-grade pro-
position has yielded over £7,000,000 worth of gold and sil-
ver, and paid £3,500,000 in dividends. All this in 16 years.
The distributions since 1893 have been on a regularly' in-
creasing scale, and that vear 3s. was paid ; next, 4s. ;
then Bs. till 1900, when half-a-sovereign w'as handed out ;
in 1903 125., 1905 135., 1906 155., 1907 165., and this year
with the bonus £l. The shares to-day are worth over £9
a-piece, and buyers of a few' years back have nearly doubled
their money'. Present annual distribution means £500,000.
Waihi is rapidly' forging to the front among the world’s
big bullion producers. There are six main shafts, and
some 16 lodes are being worked, among them the Martha
and branches (the original name of the mine), Empire,
Royal, Edward and Welcome. No. 9 level (1000 ft.) has
been responsible for the best recent developments. A
notable point is the large scale upon w'hich development
work is done. There is no necessity’ for the tallest man
to stoop in his passage through the labyrinth of drives
and crosscuts ; many of the former have been so widened
and ore has been taken down from such a height that
they’ look more like railway tunnels than anything else.
In some sections of the Welcome lode stoping is proceed-
ing on widths up to 120 ft. To show' how immense are the
principal ore bodies, it may be mentioned that in many
instances two parallel drives have to be taken along
the lode on the same level to facilitate working. In-
dications are that values persist with depth. The ore re-
serves are estimated at a million and a half. This is on
a conservative basis. Figures weary, but the Royal lode
for 1700 ft. averages 30ft. in width, the Empire the same
for 1200 ft., and the Martha reef is 90ft. wide for 1800 ft. ;
the distance in each case being the limit opened up. The
El ward lode in the south-west crosscut at No. 8 is 75ft.
wide, assaying £lO per ton from wall to wall. Gold,
curiously enough, is seldom seen in the ricoest Waihi
ore. The full crushing capacity at the three mills is 330
head of stamps.
* * * *
A new tin-field (alluvial) has been lately dis-
covered near Pine Creek, in the Northern Territory. Tin
ore is found for nearly six miles along the creek, hut, on
account of excess of water, little can be done at present
to test and decide the capabilities of the field. At pre-
sent, some 15 or 20 men are at work, up to their knees in
water, stabbing blindly' with shovels for the wash, and
making up to £1 a day at the work. The tin ore
obtained is mostly black, with some brown, ruby', amber
and W'ood tin. There is said to be a bigger and better
find of alluvial tin made some six miles from this locality’,
at a place which is to retain its native name of “ Umbra
Warra.” It is recommended by the warden, who paid a
visit to the field, that the place be proclaimed a new
goldfield, as provided by Clause viii. of the Northern
Territory' Mining Act (1003), so that, for thre years at
least, it will he closed to aliens, and open only to Euro-
pean miners. Meanwhile, Chinese have been notified that
they'will not be permitted on the field. It is expected
that Umbra Warra will carry' at least 100 white men,
who will make wages and perhaps a trifle over. It is a
poor man’s field, but absolutely useless for the Southern
miner, as all claims will be pegged before he can arrive.
