The Queensland Mines Department reports for
1901 a decreased mineral production. The total value is
£3,844,487, a deficiency of £287,677. Gold and silver
together show an improvement of £18,330, arid the above
drop is due to lower values of minerals “other than
gold." For example, the copper conten’s of Mount
Morgan ores were worth £9224 less than in 1907, although
the output was 1600 tons greater. The lower prices for
wolfram and antimony have played havoc with the pro-
duction of these two minerals, the latter being almost en-
tirely'neglected. Regarding the various fields, Mount Mor-
gan’sgold output was worth £640,879, outof atotalforthe
State of £1,975,554 ; while its copper, valued at £375,409
was also about one-third of the total. Copper from Mun-
gana was worth £104,102, and the silver lend ores from the
same place £L20,000. The silver lead and copper ores
from the Silver Spur Mines at Stanthorpeyielded £24,257.
Oloncuiry produced £36,624 more than the previous year,
the total being £94,737. The contribution of the Explora-
tion Co. towards this sum was the value of 916 tons copper,
1180 oz. gold, and 5050 z. silver. Mount continues
to ship small parcels of rich ore, and several other shows
are accumulating ores for future treatment.
Various statistics contained in the above report
arc of interest. Of 173 casualties 33 were fatal, 107
serious, and 33 of minor degree ; while in 1907, 15 were
fatal, 110 serious, and 16 minor. The death-rate per 1000
in 1908 was as follows: Metalliferous mines, 2'l (0'84);
coal mines, 1‘79 (0 - 00); all mines, mills and reduction
works, 1’61(0'70). The figures in brackets are for 1907,
which was an exceptionally good year. The Mt. Morgan
disaster and three fatal accidents in coal mines have in-
flated last year’s figures. During the year 16 new mines
were opened, 10 old ones were re-opened, and 43 were
closed down. The figures for development work are :
shaft sinking in gold mines, 12,797 ft. ; in copper mines,
10,571 ft. ;in other mines, 3305 ft. Drives, crosscuts, and
other developments were : In gold mines, 87,231 ft. ; in
copper mines, 27,771 ft. ; in other mines, 8415 ft. The
general result is a falling off, except in the case of
Cloncurry.
The affairs of the Queensland Copper Co. at
Mount Perry (Q.) are far from satisfactory, and it is more
than likely that the smelters will be temporarily closed
down before long: The deal in connection with the New
Anniversary mine at Herherton was not completed, and
the formation of a new company to work the mine is in
the hands of Lovedale, of Mount Molloy'; whose scheme
has been accepted by the syndicate. He proposes to
form a company with 40,000 £1 shares, 25,000 to be issued
at 2s. With a 10s. bonus, making them paid up to 12s.
The Mount Molloy' Co. is to underwrite 15,000 shares,
leaving 10,000 available for the public. The vendors
receive 12,500 full,v-paid-up shares only on the under-
standing that work is to be started forthwith. A number
of the smaller surrounding properties are included.
* * *
Good work is being done on the Glassford Creek
(Q.) mine for G. C. Wilcocks and partner, who have under-
taken the development of the property'. The work is
being carried out under the direction of Finlay, the
underground manager of the late company. At 70ft.
below the “ Blue Bag ” drives have been put in north and
south, and on the south end the ore exposed for a distance
of 25ft. has shown values from one to 10 per cent, copper,
and as high as 6dwt. of gold. The average is probably
between 3£ and 4 per cent, copper and 3dwt. of gold.
Development to date is at least encouraging.
* * * *
From Charters Towers (Queensland) a chorus of
praise is sounded by sharebrokers, warden and State
Geologist on account of the Day Dawn reef, which, taking
the east and west shoots together, has proved payable for
5000 ft. Total dividends run into something like
£2,000,000. The eastward extension of this reef is a mat-
ter of great consequence to Charters Towers. There
isn’t, however, a scrap of proof to uphold so many fine
anticipations. The end of No. 7, which is the most
easterly extension of the Day’ Dawn, is in a barren face.
The end of No. 5 level, driven 300 ft. in the East Mexican,
is also barren. The Brilliant P. C. should have seen
something of the Day Dawn in its deepest workings, but
the formations met with were exceedingly poor. Poverty,
of course, doesn’t prove that these formations were not
the Day Dawn formations. There is a chance that in
sinking shafts something may be found, and quite a
ripple of excitement was caused when the Brilliant Block
and Brilliant St. George struck formations and small
quartz veins, and when Clark’s Brilliant shaft entered
likely-looking country'. But the latter is nearly 1900 ft.
east of the payable Day Dawn workings, and the distance
is a big jump, considering all the intervening slides and
crosscourses and changes.
* * * *
Bonnie Dundee, Charters Towers (Queensland),
made enough profit during its last half-year to pay £6OOO
in dividends. It has now a small fixed deposit, and its
last crushing of 332 tons for £2060 having turned out
well, it is able to pay another sixpenny dividend. Some
of the stopes are getting exhausted, but the improvement
in the top level and No. 3 is prolonging the life of the
mine. All the same, shares do not advance much. They
would probably do so if the winze going down below
No. 4 met with heavy mineral. No. 4 is also to be ex-
tended eastwards, and may' possibly get better stone.
* * * •
Silver Spur, at Texas (Queensland), is close
enough to the border to take its supply of coke from Ash-
ford (N.S. YV.). Few people know that there is a coalfield
at Ashford on the Severn River, so quiet does this old
settlement keep itself. Silver Spur is 30 miles from the
coal mine, and the coke is to he used to run a water
jacket furnace. The main shaft is down 420 ft., and a
large lode has been exposed in the south drive. Stoping
is going on at the 300 ft. level, and the old reverberatories,
which use up so much timber, are unequal to the task of
breaking all the ore. It is expected that 100 tons of ore
per day will be smelted.
•* * * *
Brilliant Extended, Charters Towers (Queens-
land), has declared another sixpenny dividend, and has
£4OOO on fixed deposit. This mine has driven some of its
levels with fair average values of mineral stone, which is
generally split into hanging-wall and footwall sections.
On the other hand, it is well to remember the broken
and unpayable parts of this reef, which are the reason
why only nine dividends have been paid during the long
life of the mine. What used to be called the Deep
Levels (now Brilliant Deeps) also proved hundreds
of feet of wretchedly poor levels, and it re-
mains to be seen whether the rich patch met
with at a depth of 2413 ft. will develop into a payable
mine. The fact that this shoot of stone is many hundreds
of feet from any other payable reef makes it exceedingly
difficult to identify. From its general characteristics, it
has been called the Brilliant, hut, strictly speaking, only
one main fissure can be called by the one name, and
formations which are sometimes very' wide apart are
reallv separate reefs.
‘ * * * *
Queensland Exploration (Cloncurry) seems to
be forcing the pace, and only an exceptionally-rich mine
could withstand such a compulsory' system of produc-
tion. Nearly all the sulphide ore is coming from between
the 250 ft. and 200 ft. levels. Last month, (March) 2100
tons of ore and fluxes returned 213 tons of 58 per. cent,
matte. The ore seems fairly easy to smelt, because only’
323 tons of fluxes were used in smelting 1777 tons of ore.
Every effort is to be made to make up a good return for
Aprii ; furnace bottoms, flue dust, slag, etc., are to be
smelted in one of the reverberatories.
Developments of the Great Australia (Q.)lode at
300 ft. vertical are being anxiously watched. So far only
niullncky low-grade stuff has been met with. The winze
which is going down to meet the drive is also showing
poorer prospects. The rejoicing because some of the re-
fractory quartz gangue was replaced by lime —which
made the ore easy to treat—has given place to disap-
pointment now that the lower levels have become mul-
locky'. It is quite possible, however, that conditions will
change again. Bore hole prospects point that way ; but
they are not much to depend upon.
* * * *
At the MacGregor copper mine (Cloncurry,
Queensland) a good deal of importance is attached to a
seam of calcite (lime) passed through in sinking the shaft.
Because calcite replaced some of the refractory quartz in
the Great Australia lode (Queensland Exploration) and
made smelting tolerably easy', a theory is built up that
the excess of quartz and silica found in most Cloncurry
mines will, as depth increases, change into an easily-
treated calcite gangue. But there isn’t much to found a
general theory on, and the MacGregor, at a depth of
220 ft., has still far more silicious ore and
quartz than it likes. The shaft at 339 ft. is
in a hard quartzite bar, and the seam of cal-
cite overhead is only a fe.w inches thick. At
310 ft. along the north drive assays average 5 p.c.,
and the crosscut that was put in to the west to
prove the width of the ore body has passed through 17ft.
of low-grade silicious ore. No doubt the rich ore, as des-
cribed in the report, will help to sweeten some of the low-
grade stuff, so things are not so hopeless as they might
be. MacGregor had something like £30,000 a while back,
and some of it is invested in Etheridge railway' deben-
tures. This group of mines requires a smelting plant, and
there is much mine development to be done, so a good
deal of money will be used up. It rather staggers one,
however, to find that last year no less than £lB,OOO was
spent.
Mining matters at Wyalong (N.S.W.), which
have been in a depressed condition for some time, now
show signs of a revival. The True Blue Co. has again
struck excellent mineral ore at the 1200 ft. It should yield
from soz. to 12oz. gold per ton. At Neelds indications
are more promising than they have been since the firm
started prospecting at the 900 ft. level for the lost chute
that was worked down to 300 ft. with such satisfactory
results in the early days of the field. Mr. Pittman, Chief
Inspector of Mines, has been on a visit, and from an
inspection of the Blue, Neelds, and other properties is
satisfied that Wyalong has got a kick or two left.
* * * *
Kangiara (N.S.W.) talks of sinking the shaft
another 100 ft., and is working out a scheme to smelt its
lower-grade ore, to lessen the heavy cost of hand-picking,
bagging, carting, railage, and other returning charges
from the smelting works. So much has been said about
the richness of the ore that it is just as well to consider
some of the costs. Picked lots of ore have been sent away
to Cockle Creek and Port Kembla. A list of seven ship-
ments make up 346 tons, and the agreed value of this
ore, including gold and silver, is approximately £l2
per ton. In all, 110 tons of lead ore averaged
55 per cent., and gold and silver bring up the total value
to £l2, w'hich, by the way, is about the same as some of
the Broken Hill concentrates are worth. A more recent
shipment gave 58 per cwt. lead, 230 z. silver, and Idwt.
gold. Kangiara has only' to increase its shipments of
this rich ore to make substantial profits, but tonnage is
not a strong feature, and Kangiara doesn’t take outsiders
into its confidence and say how long it takes to get out
110 tons of picked ore. Obviously it wouldn’t take long
if all thfe 6ft. of ore mentioned in the reports w'as the real
article. A glance at the pay-sheet would astonish some
of those enthusiasts who fix their faith on assays.
Great Cobar (N.S.W.) produced 479 tons of
copper for month ending March 7, and this is easily a
record for the new' plant. Gold and silver output shqws
a considerable falling off. This company does not publish
tonnages, but the output of copper indicates something
over the 20,000-ton mark—which is about 3000 tons above
the capacity of the old furnace plant. Continued troubles
with the new furnaces are put down to weak foundations.
Initial breakdowns were contributed to by a habit the
settlers got into of bursting because the expansion
magnesite brick linings had not been provided for. The
evil has since been remedied, but as things still continue
to go wrong, the last published output is likely to remain
a record for some time.
* * * *
C.S. A. Mines (N.S.W.) have shut down for six
months. The company has developed a large quantity of
basic ore, but its copper values are too low'. Its
prospective value is due to the fact of it being a
suitable flux for the higher grade silicious ores of the
Cobar district, and in this respect it is unique. The
co. has still a fair area of ground at the southern end of
its lease to prospect, and has been hoping that Gardner s
Block would assist by driving north. However, the latter
has kept religiously away from the C.S. A. boundary.
North Kangiara (N.S.W.) has been successfully
floated, and a start will be made at once to prospect the
ground. Surface indications are similar to those on
most other pieces of ground in the neighborhood. The
particular virtue possessed by this area is that it blocks
off the northern end of the Kangiara mines, and may
contain a continuation of the lode worked in that pro-
perty. The boundary' is 500yds. away from the nearest
workings, quite a considerable distance where minerals
are concerned. Developments will be watched with in-
terest by shareholders in the parent company'.
* * • *
Harden Gold Mine (N.S.W.) is preparing plans
for a new mill, and should make a very considerable cut
in milling-costs. These have appeared rather high in the
past on account of the plant being out of date.
• * * *
The last crushing of the Carrington United
mine (Queensland) was 614 tons. It returned bullion
worth £1933, and 75 tons of concentrates valued at
£IIS6, w'hich facts explain the interest that is once more
taken in these shares. After the disastrous experience
with its smelting plant, this company' found itself left
with low-grade ore and an overdraft of £13,000, the latter
representing the price paid for an insight into the smelt-
ing business. The plant at present consists of an
inefficient set of reverberatories and a Huntington mill
equipped with Wilflev and Card concentrators. The dis-
aster was assisted by the fact that the shoot of rich ore
w'hich had been followed from the surface slipped across
the boundary into the territory of the Nos. 1 and 2 East
company. The latter has only' a small area, surrounded
by the big company’s lease, and, as the shoot continues to
bear east, there is now little reason to doubt that it will
once more enter the Carrington United Mines property.
This means mining at a considerable depth, hut the
Towers men will certainly be on the track of the
lode if values continue at all promising. Present
milling operations are giving fair returns, but the
amalgamation and fine crushing of a gold-copper ore is
never likely to yield any extraction to boast of. It has
been demonstrated that the values cannot be economi-
cally' recovered by bulk smelting. Excess silica will have
to be removed by concentration. Roasting matte to
blister is also a procedure which will probably be omitted
from future smelting campaigns. As the overdraft has
been wiped out some profit should be made in future,
always provided the company' does not have another
carouse over the first patch of ore that is above ordinary'
grade.
***■*■«
Very few almonds show in the toffee at Wal-
halla(Vic.). Long Tunnel is opening up a nice formation
at the two deep levels. But w'hile the Board is content
to be told that the stone is “ payable ” or carries “ nice
gold,” the battery' has to be waited on to ring out results.
Poor Extended, next door, has no luck ; 150 tons for
500 z., 120 tons for 440 z., and so on, are records of low'
dirt. The first stope at 2900 ft. has been rushed along, and,
with the second stope opened, better grade quartz may
be available. If it goes -J-oz. it will only just pay' expenses.
The future turns on the stone betw’een the two deepest
levels and what maybe cut in the 3000 ft. crosscut. The
Extended people build on the old North Long Tunnel
ground, but The Bulletin has again and again warned
them that the £120,000 spent on that mine in the past
only' gave calls and sorrow. There is talk about the
Board meeting the shareholders to discuss the situation.
The chances are that the shareholders will rather cuss
the situation. Still, it would be well to meet. And it
would be still better if shareholders get an outside
mining man to tell them how they stand.
Great Southern, Rutherglen (Vic.), paid its
sprat every month so regularly that there w'as a rustling
of gowns when the break came. When the width of a
gutter is given at 800 ft. or 1000 ft. most folk think the
whole of the wash is pay'able. This little fiction can be
burst. Most of the gold is on one or other bank of the
old river, and if there has been a swirl or a curl, or a
tributary coming in then—(l) the gold may be scoured to
be deposited farther away, or again, the gutter may be
enriched. At the Duke United the tributary near the
shaft saved the mine for a while. At the Great Southern,
above the north branch level, the bottom is uneven and
patchy, facts that account for poor returns. Generally, the
gold lias fallen down to l2dwt., against working expenses
lldwt. This is a shrinkage of 6s. per fathom in values,
leaving so small a margin of profit that divs. become
next, to impossible. The company has a reserve of £4500,
which isn’t half enough. Low-grade ground occurs in
every' deep lead, and as the public isn’t inclined to pay
calls just now' a certain proportion of profit ought to be
bagged for future fodder. Some excuse exists for
dividend paying) as only £47,000 has gone back to share-
holders, against £99,000 capital paid by them. Still,
business is biz, and the sensible men who run the mine
ouvht to know that a margin of under £5OOO is not a
sufficient protection against calls.
* * * *
Next door to the Great Southern, the Great
Southern Consols is posing as a horrible example of what
oughtn’t to be. Shares lost 33 per cent, of their value
and set the world wondering. That is, except those sec-
tions of the world which had know ledge that the mine was
in an awkward plight. The old trouble—hustling out
wash when it was green, heavy ground and costly work.
Shareholders cannot afford to let this last relapse pass by.
So many nice things have been said and written for the
last five years about what the Consols would do that
what the Evening Noose calls a Coronial Inquiry is
w'anted over someone’s carcase. Have managers been
pushed into a corner to get out wash, or have they
failed to let the Board know the danger of tackling wet
ground? These are two out of a host of questions that
need to be answered. What drew attention to the drift
W'as the anxious faces of a little coterie of deep lead men
who don’t affect the Stock Exchange except when things
are buzzing one way or the other—mostly the other.
New Long-Thought-Of mine, Rokewood (Vic.),
is going to be the Dashed-Poor-Thought-Of, unless some-
thing radical is done. According to the directors, the
legal manager walked off with the books. Although he
was hunted after till the morning by an anxious chair-
man, neither books nor man, it is alleged, could be
found. This is not mining of the right sort, and petty
cash cannot be expected to stand such an extreme strain.
The co.’s auditor has reported that, out of a total of £522
received from the sale of shares, £179 cannot be traced.
He also alleges that the bulk of the co.’s banking was
carried on through the late manager’s private account.
With the co. in such a hole it is not neces-
sary’ to go into the merits of the mine. But
it is necessary to ask how directors let a bank account be
operated on in such a way. There should he no private
accounts in company matters. The first thing a Board of
Directors has to do is to check up the bank, next to
check up the bank book, to see that the scrip ledger is
0.K., and that there is a proper record of calls. These
points settled, the watching of expenditure is easy’.
* ■» « *
Gwalia South (W.A.) held its half-yearly
meeting at Kalgoorlie on the 15th inst. Chairman Yeo
expressed satisfaction at the outlook, and reckoned that
if the new makes of ore recently exposed out west proved
to be as good as expected, the life of the mine—which is
now endangered on account of the Sons of Gwalia getting
the main shoot at a shallow depth—would be indefinitely
lengthened. He asked the meeting to back up the
Board as regards its action in withholding the dividend
until May, and, as no one took an opposite stand, he was
entitled to assume that it did so. No questions were
asked, and the two retiring directors were re-elected
without opposition. On the day of the meeting the co.
had £5900 in hand, £1547 bullion reserve, and a fully-
equipped mine.

BUCHANAN WHISKY—
LAIEC & WHITE
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, April 27- 1909,
My Dear Arinl
Ernest H. Shackleton cannot be fully apprecia-
tive of the importance of being Ernest. He gets
bushels of letters asking for his autograph, and
nearly every person whose name begins with S,
or ends with “ton” writes to claim relationship.
Most of his interviewers and most of the people he
casually meets must be a trial with their trivial
questionings. Personally speaking, I led off
(when I met him) with these queries :—•
(1) Was it very cold near the South Pole?
(2) Is pony-meat nice?
(3) Did you bring back many penguins?
(4) Will you go down there again?
He chortled and showed some snapshots of penguins.
One was of the chesty bird standing with his bill
pointing skyward as he crowed for a mate. His
solo would presently fetch a spinster penguin, and
by-and-bye, when the pair set up nest-keeping,
Mr. Penguin would feed his wife as she sat on the
eggs. He brought her prawns and the like,
carrying them in his gullet, into which she dipped
her beak. “Are the birds monogamie?” asked
the naturalist present. “That we could not
determine,” replied the Great Iceman. “We
frequently saw a male penguin feed another bird’s
wife, but we could not say whether his motives
were absolutely disinterested.”
“Charity thinketh no evil,” murmured the
naturalist.
“True, And the bird he fed may have been
his widowed niece.”
Shackleton resents the “ delusions ” (the word
is his) that he is a hero and that time must have
hung heavy on the party in the long Polar night.
They were never idle, and earned every ounce of
their seal meat. There was somewhat of a
return to the primitive—he would not call it
retrogression, but as to pining for the absent dear
ones, well, the dear ones left in the centres of
civilisation did the pining. They had some time
to do it in, whilst the dear ones in the centres of
desolation, though they had all the time there
was, crammed it with work—manual or mental.
Three miniatures on ivory in one little case the
sea rover carries on him : a handsome young
woman, a jolly little boy of four, and a chubby
small girl—they are his dear ones. He starts his
journey homeward next month. On Friday he
lectures in Sydney, the proceeds to go to certain
hospitals; on the Sunday follovving he leaves for
Melbourne, where he also speaks. The Dudleys
have harpooned him for dinner, and on another
evening he will tear a bit of food at Admiralty
House.
What a first-grade pirate of olden times
Shackleton would have made. His clean-shaven
face, his low forehead with bulging brows, his
locomotive-fender jaw, and strong deep-chested
figure give him the look of the sea-rover of fiction,
especially if your imagination sees him in top
boots, a jelly-strainer cap on his head, and a
swathe of red silk around his neck. Odds Blood !
When, at his lecture on Thursday night, he
showed a picture of himself as he was after his
three months of flannel (the same flannel) next the
skin, that picture showed him with a sinister
beard, huge and curling. He was the real thing in
Vikings and nothing else doing. His name was
Garth or Balder some such. S’death !
Shackleton stays at the Australian Club, works
in strict privacy with his secretary or runs about
Sydney in Professor Pollock's motor-car. At the
Governor-General’s ball he escaped from the dais
after bending the spine to the Lady, and went
and sat in a corner by himself. He seemed moody,
but perchance he only gloated on the fair scene.
Gad’s head ! May not a man sit by himself and
gloat? His picture films, insured for £20,000, are
now on their way to London, where the Daily
Mail has first call on what the explorer has to
show and say.
As a speaker, our partially-frozen brother
Shackleton is somewhat hesitating, but he has
a voice that could be heard through the
roll of war-drums. He spoke for but five
minutes at his Town Hall receptfon, and
the whole show was over at nine. Other
members of the expedition were on the plat-
form, and the girls liked Lieutenant Mackin-
tosh best, chiefly because he was fair and carried a
superior type of moustache. It looked like a
pedigree moustache—not the mere hair that you
can pick up anywhere. He had lost an eye, and
Baronet Brocklehurst a toe. Mackintosh has a
glass optic, but whether Brocklehurst has a brass
toe, even as Miss Kilmannsegg haa a golden
leg—anyway the matter is of no concern except to
himself. Cheers were given for Mrs. Shackle-
ton and the two little Shackletons, and the
audience dispersed. But as nine o’clock was
much too early to go home, the receptioners
went next door to the Cathedral, and looked at
Archbishop Saumarez Smith lying in state. Two
clergymen in surplice and cassock watched and
prayed. Purple drapery drooped around the walls
of the church, and the gold crook lay on the oaken
coffin.
The Sir Philip Brocklehurst whom Sydney met
for the first time a fortnight ago, and who has
claimed of right a fragment of the bright lime-
light that beats on all returned Polar explorers, is
an unusual type. He is but 22, and is immensely
rich. The ordinary affluent young man of his
time of life prefers to do in his cash in almost any
other locality than the South Pole. Not so the
subject under discussion. The South Pole is
with him a hobby. Nothing, he avers, but force
majeure shall stop him being there when the next
effort is made to get to it. And, if nobody else
appears, he intends to fit out a South Pole expedi-
tion at his own expense, and lead it at his and
its own risk. When not Poling, he is a natural,
fair-complexioned, pieasant-looking person, and
even in the blubber-and-fur over-coating, which
is the dress garment of the Far South regions, he
is said to look as seemly as most.
The Governor-General’s two elder daughters—
the demoiselles Morvyth and Honor Ward-
started their Australian schooling last Tuesday, by
going to Miss Badham’s academy in Forbes-street,
Darlinghurst. The girls have their hair looped up,
a fashion that the Sydney girl of 15 or under will
now rush with all her might. Hitherto she has
let it float loose until she got into her coming-out
frock. The Ward schoolgirls reach their place of
cram in a motor-car.
Mr. Edwin Geach was married on Thursday, at
Neutral Bay, to Miss Willis, a young Maoriland
member of his company. Mr. Jeffrey Hill
propped up the nervous groom, and Mrs.
Bussell, the consort of one of the legal firm of Sly
and Russell stood by the bride while Mr. N.
Montagu gave her over to Edwin’s permanent
safe keeping. Two of the Geach girls were
present. The bride wore a navy-blue cloth
tailor-made and a shrill-green hat, and went off
for a South Coast honeycomb.
Mention of the South Coast reminds me that
the proprietor of one of the summer resorts there
has a grievance that deserves airing in these
columns —if only as a matter of justice to accredited
correspondents. A female person, by masquerad-
ing as a member of the staff of This Bulletin,
was enabled to live for some weeks at
the proprietor’s boarding-house free of ex-
pense. No money equivalent happened along
for the loss of his ham and eggs and
roast lamb, and for the use of his hammocks
and kapok mattress and surf-bathing towels.
Rumors from other coasts and quarters lead me to
suspect that the masquerader is liberalising the
education of other boardinghouse-keepers. There
are no female persons on the staff of The Bul-
letin save your “ Akenehi ” in Sydney, and
“Johanna” in Melbourne, “Rosna”in Adelaide,
and “ Mario ”in Brisbane. Four in all, and we
are all at our posts and writing with more vigor
than legibility. And we don’t act the rdle of
locusts. And if we stayed at a hostel, most likely
we would be the last to own up to such a disre-
spectable thing as work. When we are on a holi-
day we try to look as if we wouldn’t know what
work was if we saw it floating in the soup.
The Edwin Geach wedding cake, a presentation
cookie, stood 4ft. high in its sugar. Amongst the
vast array of presents from theatrical co-workers
was included a safety razor. With such it is, I
believe, impossible for even an infatuated bride-
groom to spill his vital fluid to such an extent
that death will necessarily supervene.
Hon. Miss Jane Gathorne-Hardy, who inhabits
Federal Government House on a visit to the
Dudleys, is a daughter of Lord Medway and
granddaughter of the Earl of Cranbrook. Visi-
tors of this calibre ought to be welcome to Aus-
tralians. They go back and tell their kind that
we are not black and don’t dress in ’possum skins.
Our old Commonwealth nobility has taken up
the bait of the Christian name as the mouse
goes for the toasted cheese. Persons on the
dais or close to the edge of the carpet
at the Garden Party, have heard Lady Dudley
address the chamberlain as “Dick.” Someone
else is “Sandy” to her consort. Happy is the
fashionable youth who is simply named, and may
now be called Billy or Jimmy. Reggies and Guys
are outside designations.
A curious combination menu and programme
was sprung on the guests at the Arthur Allens’
big dinner and theatre party last week. The food
and the fun were named on the same card, and that
was decorated with scenes from ‘ ‘ An Englishman's
Home.” The play is over at 10.15, and Lord
Richard Nevill, who came in with two ladies at
10 past 10, was in time to see the final curtain.
The trio looked as surprised and hurt as the
Brown family do at the foreign invaders.
Of the deceased Primate Smith’s big family of
daughters, Mabel, who is now in England, had
the closest connection with Australia. She
accompanied her father hitherward on his
appointment to Sydney, and went under the
musical tutelage of the late Miss Woolley. On
returning to England, she passed for the
A.R.C.M. diploma, and launched out as a player
and composer. One of her sisters, Ursula, is a
clever ’cello player, and another, Katherine, a
capable pianist. Music was the deceased
Primate’s chief recreation for many years past,
and his daughters supplied it free of cost.

Mr. Ernest Brooks, trekking Vic.-wards on
Friday night last, left £l5OO worth of his wife’s
jewels in a Sydney cab. He only discovered his
loss when he had settled down in a carriage of the
Melbourne express. The train hadn’t started,
but your genuine man of fashion does not lightly
abandon a comfortable seat and start fussing round
after lost property ; and so Mr. Brooks merely
directed a policeman to keep a casual eye open for
the strayed hardware, and then leaned languidly
back, and, so remained till the train departed.
There is something rather magnificent in this com-
plete repose of manner in a situation that would
stir the normal citizen to frenzy. I remember
encountering a man on a ferry-boat who had inad-
vertently left a Jew-fish, or leather-jacket, or
prawn or something in the vehicle which conveyed
him to the Quay, and his grief was terrible to wit-
ness. And yet the marine product was quite
dead, and it wasn’t worth anywhere near £l5OO.

cloth long-tailed tailor-made, heavily be-buttoned
and braided. Another returner is Mrs. Dick
Horsfall. And a Tobin girl (an heiress from Mel-
bourne), who has also been abroad, looks the latest
fashion plate in toast color and a black extinguisher
hat.
I see that Ballarat has decided its love-letter
competition, which has now been running since
some time in last year. Here are some extracts
from the winning screed :
You ask me to forgive you. What can you ever do,
sweetheart, which for one moment could make me forget
what you are to me? Or that love which has made earth
heaven, and my life a joy ? Have Ito forgive the sun for
lurking behind the clouds, when he has shone on my days
and made them golden, or shall I welcome him the less
when he comes forth to warm me again ? Beloved, if I
have aught to forgive, it is that yon I hold so dear have
asked the question. I have no desire to know anything,
except that vou have loved me, and love me still. Faith
is unquestioning, for have I not crowned you king, and
my king can do no wrong! These eyes of mine, which
have closed beneath your kisses, are sightless until vour
lips unseal them ; my ears are deaf except to the magic
call of the voice of my beloved, and my heart has ceased
to beat until it can throb on yours. lam sleeping, and
shall awaken but at the sound of your footsteps. You
say, “ May I come?” Dearest, I am waiting.
The second prize went to the unlooser of the
following
Dearest. —The garden is just a wilderness of roses to-
day, a running riot of color in every corner, and in a few
weeks, or perhaps only a few days, they will have drooped
and dropped. There is usually something so subtly sad
in the passing of flowers that my whole mind seems
steeped in the wistfulness of it all. But this year it is
different; I have counted away each long mont hof your
absence on the calendar of flowers, and as each blossom
breathes away, it means one day less—one whole day
nearer. Do you remember that day, that day of days,
for you, when I showed you my hands full of violets?
You stooped and kissed them ; you mistook them for my
eyes; since then my whole heart seems to have leapt into
the violet. Each morning I search the leaves for just one
tiny bud. When that comes, you come. I have loved
every different flower as it budded, blossomed, and then
crept into the past. They have been all my thought, my
love for you excepted. M.v love is undying.—Yours till
the violet’s good-bye, Eleanor.
They are very beautiful and intense productions,
but I diagnose them as emanating from some dear
old lady who has not loved since the nonage of the
late Queen Vic. —if then. Here is a love letter
which I did not enter for any Ballarat prize, and
yet I think it is superior, artistically speaking, to
either of the ones that scored, for it is genuine.
A girl acquaintance of mine suffered me to take a
fair copy of it before she sent it to her latest young
man :
My dear old thing,—What in the name of goodness are
you snorting about now ? What if you did see me in the
Oentralia Autumn Garden with Billy yesterday ? Did I
say when I told you I was going shopping that I
meant to swear off all sustenance for the day ? The
suggestion is too absurd. I wonder you, who, with any
sort of luck, are now competent to earn a full 10s. 6d.
per week at the Bar, should entertain it for a moment.
No; I certainly don't want,you to swear off drink. Alii
object to, as I have told you many times already, is your
habit of calling on mother after your 27th whisky, or
whatever it is, and sobbing all over the new chintz covers.
It is a banal practice at the best, and it is intensely annoy-
ing to my family and to the maids. I don’t exactly want
you to be a teetotaller, as experience has shown me that
Che no-liquor racket renders you morbid and suspicious,
and otherwise offensive. The golden mean would seem to
be the thing —i.e., when you find tearfulness supervening
resolve to hire someone to drag you away and put you to
bed. You ask do I love you ? Mow what an appallingly silly
question. Would I take all this trouble over your welfare
if I didn’t? About the flowers for Friday night, counter-
mand them ; the wretched things die as soon as one puts
them on and they cost a fearful lot, and, anyhow, Billy is
getting me some. With the money you save you can run
round and get me that diamond spray we were looking at
in de Smythe’s window last week. Chin-chin and be
very, very good.—Your adoring Arabella.
Exhibits relating to professions* trades and
hobbies for girls—examples in full working order—
fill the basement and ground floor of the Town
Hall. The girl has a wide and varied choice. In
the basement .she milks a cow, and the presence of
a motor with a girl seated near it suggests that
the vehicle with the toot in front and the slain
hen behind may be another means of earning a
roll and butter. Elsewhere the daughter of the
land makes sweets, or paints china as exhibited by
Miss Richards. The exposition of the Norland
Institute will be as a magnet to attract the girl to
infant tending and training, or she may make
lace,, or bind books, or do lithography. Two nice
girls are ironing all the afternoon of every day
this week. The girl as a washerwoman js not
represented, but as a mender, darner, ai d
renovator she is all there. On Friday Lady
L>udle3 r , arrayed in pale heliotrope, the color
that is supposed to attract the highest spiritual
influences, opened the Exhibition, and the young
person from three years to 23 years has crammed
it ever since.
The question—“ What shall we do with our
girls?” is answered by scores of professions,
trades and hobbies. But there was no equivalent
at the Ex. for the “smart general.” Had there
been an exhibit of a girl doing the work of, say,
four rooms —parlor, bedroom, dining-room and
kitchen—with what eager, greedy eyes would the
women (who are not faced by any problem re what
to do with themselves) have gazed upon the all-
round house-workee. Of specialists in housework
there is really no acute shortage, but the general
has all but finished the disappearing trick. One
registry office here has 30 orders for generals. And
since Easter Saturday not one general - has ap-
peared to seek even a “ light place.” Woe to the
missuses ! One day there will be.,three years’
compulsory domestic service for girls, just as there
will be compulsory military training for men.
Certain of the race-week balls were sanctified hv
the presence of a divine. He ministrates aboard
one of the warships, and when not actually en-
gaged on the job of soul-salving hears scant out-
ward resemblance to the conventional theological
expert. At evening functions he is a plain
ordinary young man as to his coat and the things
they swathe the piano-limbs with in Boston, but
severely clerical as to his waistcoat, collar and tie.
I don’t know if this is meant to be symbolical in
any way—indicating, for example, that albeit
doggedly spiritual in certain directions he is pre-
pared to be worldly to a limited extent in others
—but there it is. If he cures souls as skilfully as
he dances, the heavenly prospects of Ed. Vll.’s
tars now in port should be bright.

A new use for a miniature of your grandmother
or other and older ancestor: Take its hinged
cover off, get a long pin affixed to the back of the
miniature, and use your ancestor as a hat-skewer.
Such a skewer sat opposite me| in a tram just
now—at least the skewerist did. Her decoration
was a largish beautifully-painted picture of a
lady of the powder and patches time, covered
with a convex glass, and rimmed with gold. It
secured the hat from the left front of the crown,
and a blackbird stared permanently at it from
the other side of the headgear.
Returned wanderers by the same liner that
carried a certain Earl and Countess and their two
grown-up sons tell of the aristocrats’ amazing
economy. The lads had two pairs of white pants
each, and these they would wash in their cabin.
Their lady mother wore no underskirts, only a
skirt and serviceable bloomers. When she
ascended to the bridge the occasion made one of
the side-shows of the day’s programme.
1 hewed this sad outcry from the Adelaide
Register (15/4/’O9)
From “ A Mot,her” “ I think it, is disgusting the way
the governesses are going on. I had five last year, and
all wanted to do nothing but teach, and never soil their
hands, though they had £26 a year and beautiful board.
Whatever do they want? Two had very good offers of
marriage. Of course these things and good homes are
not counted—nothing but grumbling and no thanks.
I hope other ladies will have a say, and put a period to
such nonsense.”
It conjures up a hopeless picture. One sees in
fancy the kindly lady-mother in her home, with
its “ beautiful board ” (from which five govern-
esses retired in disorder during a single
year), and its culture and opportunities
for physical development by means of
useful toil. On the surface, it is pleasing
enough. But look closer. Its bosom warms and
nourishes a viper in the form of the sixth annual
governess. This person—T cannot bring myself
to term her woman—draws the lavish stipend or
honorarium of 10s. per week. Yet is she
dissatisfied. Nay, more; she is rebellious.
Little Bill has had his music-lesson (piano,
harpsichord and oboe); his sister Martha has
been heard her French verbs; the twins
have hearkened misunderstandingly to the basic
principles of surds; the Greek and Latin compo-
sitions of the family have been corrected ; evening
approaches. The day’s work of the governess, on
its intellectual side, is over. What more natural
than that the pampered creature should be desired
to swing an axe on the woodheap for half an
hour, and bluestone the terra-cotta lions on the
front-porch as preliminary steps to cleaning out
the fowl-house. The suggestion is put to her by
the lady-mother. Lo! she side-steps it. She
says that she is weary, or has home letters to
write, or advances any other paltry excuse of the
kind that occurs to her contumacious mind.
And so the billet of seventh governess yawns
for another. The sort of thing—this seeth-
ing discontent, and arrogance and contempt
of constituted authority, that is becoming
so manifest among the lower classes —is
the direct result of Australia not taking Mr.
J. Cook’s advice and going in whole-heartedly for
Anti-Socialism. “A Mother” errs in thinking
that the fact of “other ladies” having “asay”
in the Adelaide Register on the governess ques-
tion is going to “ put a period to such nonsense.”
Nothing but the return of Mr. Cook to power
with a large majority will be of the slightest avail
to produce educated females who will train the
intellects of the young, and when not so engaged,
will cut wood and dig the garden, all for a fraction
Over one shilling per diem.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

Why not have a Dance in
Your Own Home?
Now that the weather is getting cooler, you will naturally
be longing for a homely dance—the most delightful and
healthful of all indoor, cool-weather recreations.
The trouble with home-dances generally is the lack of a
good accompanist. If you do have one, then that member of
the party is debarred from participating in the enjoyment.
The way out of the difficulty is to buy an Edison Phono-
graph, and have it play you whatever music is required. All
the newest dance music is available, as played by the best
bands, and there is an almost infinite variety.
You have the Edison, not for one night only, but for
always, and in between the dances it is ready to provide items
of entertainment that you will all appreciate.
Buy one now, for the winter will soon be here. We will
gladly send you a full list of records and machines, and the
name of the nearest dealer. You can then call on him, and
hear the Edison for yourself.

The average Australian business firm is pro-
gressing hugely on the artistic side. A volumelet which
Arthur Rickard and 00. have got together to advertise
the charms of Greenacre, a new residential suburb of
Sydney, looks more like one of those ornamental works
that are displayed on drawing-room tables than the
trade circular which it rightly is. On the outside a
chaste color scheme in green suggests one of those bind-
ings which the cultured Bostonian female demands for
her Shelley or Keats, and inside the beauty spots of the
site of promise are shown in a fine series of photographs.
Taken for all in all, the book is a boshter ; and there are
indications that the suburb which it advertises is in the
same class.*

The MOST DESIRABLE TYRE
Reliability, durability and efficiency may be considered
as going hand in hand as the first considerations in the
manufacture cf the most desirable Motor Tyres and
in no tyres will this trinity of features be found more
pronounced than in the ever-popular Continental variety.
The Continental Co.’s interpretation of the word
“reliability” is “ability to get there,’ and not only
to get there, but to get there well; to be certain at the
commencement of a journey that the end is in sight.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, April 26, 1909.
Mr Dear Yarrie, —
Practically the first dance of the season among
the Very Best People was given by Solicitor
Stewart, of the Mallinson, England and Stewart
legal institution, for the coming of age of his
adored granddaughter. The event was of such
importance to the old gentleman that he actually
returned from Sydney in the middle of a bowl-
ing tournament for the frivol. Only a hardened
and incurable bowler, such as he is, could
appreciate the monstrous sacrifice. The ball was
as brilliant as a March meteor. It was given at
the Heyington house. The Stewart palace
hangs on the high banks of the Too-
rakian curves of the Yarra, in the parts
where the maligned river is beautiful and
willow-fringed, and tops the hill with the flat-
roofed effect of an old Italian pile. Wide veran-
dahs spread out on terraces to the very edge of
this abrupt precipice, which is tangled with
wattle and willow. A complete and unabridged
supper of old-time splendor was served at numer-
ous round tables, like the one at which King
Arthur drank his olden refreshment, in the
roomy spread of the terrace. Electric lights were
hidden in roses and strung overhead, and a bliss-
ful orchestra played to a “dream” of a floor.
Miss Debutante Stewart, in her ninon and pearls,
had the best “good time” of any spoilt maiden
in the golden cradles of this city, and all the
youthful guests followed suit with enthusiasm.
It is a recognised fact that none but the church
paper is capable of striking the truly lyric note
when up against dull, everyday things like mar-
riage announcements. As a case in point, ex-
amine this, which is from the East St. Kilda kirk
monthly for April:—
The congregation will be sorry to learn that they have
lost their sweet singer in Miss Elsie Dwyer. She has
gone the way of many' singers before her, and sacrificed
her art to her affection. In sunshine and marital hap-
piness she now drinks the dews of the morning and
listens to the singing of the stars by night. Ad multos
annos.
She or he were a churl who hesitated to describe
the above as sweet —aye, bee-yew-tiful. Withal, it
is misleading. lam willing to bet £8 6s. 4d. to a
deceased boot that it emanated from the slim
white paw of a celibate curate with about
as much knowledge of the ways of a modern maid
with her young man as he has of the effects of 17
Manhattan cocktails absorbed in quick succession.
The old-world pastime of early-morning dew-
swilling is stone dead amongst up-to-date brides.
None but the veriest back-numbers go in for it.
And as for listening “to the singing of the stars
by night ’’—well, unless they are imported by an
impresario of note so that they attract a large
mass of the cither cats’ frocks within their orbit,
the normal hubby needs grappling irons to chain
his female encumbrance within sound of them.
The marriage of Mr. Thomas Bent and his
widow ladylove is said to be definitely arranged,
and the wedding tour planned. They are to
honeymoon in Maoriland unless the earthquakes
get too demonstrative. The arrival of Victoria’s
terrible ex-Premier is pretty certain to produce
an earth tremor of more or less violence.
Miss Frederika Meyer, a sentimental young
spinster with strong yearnings stageward, gave a
recital within the respectable shelter of the
Austral Salon walls —she had engaged the Salon
for the occasion—to an absolutely and ultra-
“ correct ” audience in evening dress. Sprinkled
among the audience were samples of the dilettante
young man. That kind of young man is skilled in
social graces, and he is often garnered in by the
Salon because he “tones” with the feminine
paintings ot flowers and the portraits of beauty
mummers that adorn those chaste walls. The
audience was backed up strongly by soulful yearn,
materialised into maidens of a subdued and
“ correct ’’artistic bent. Miss Meyer, in ghostly
white chiffon draperies, waved her arms as
at a seance, and recited to slow music. She
boasts a pleasant voice in its low and whisper-
ing registers, but any Attempt at dramatic
force brought a thin and tinny wail that
heightened the weird seance-y feeling. A gentle
trio, Miss Coral Trennerv, Mr. Felix Petsch and
Mr. Hermann Morris, played a soulful extract
from Mendelssohn. Also Hermann Morris mur-
mured an impromptu of Schubert to the piano,
while the reciter expounded Tennyson’s beautiful
“ Day Dream.” Claude Hall contributed some
of his favorite small love songs, and Miss Wagner
sang pleasantly through the lengthy programme.
At a recent big theatrical first night, the Puck
of the booking office was unfortunate in the way
he sorted people out. A divorced male person,
recently arrived from abroad, had with him the
parted wifeof an Australian matrimonial difficulty.
The combination was bound to be dangerous, but
the concern of the two half-parted people was
serious when the usher placed them side by side
with the lady’s ex-husband, who was accompanied
by the vision who had caused all the trouble
between him. and his ex-wife. This sounds com-
plicated, but it isn’t a patch on the real disturb-
ance that arose. Things soited out eventually
through kindly-disposed acquaintances changing
£eats with the disturbed scarlet crowd.
A pal, writing from London, speaks of the
success of Victoria Beatrice Miranda, the sister of
Lalla of the golden throat. Miss Miranda has
been singing with the Carl Rosa company for the
last few weeks. For her Elizabeth, in “ Tann-
hauser,” she gathered in special press notices.
The same girl snorts at the billing in a London
daily of “Bertha Bird, the phenomenal Aus-
tralian soprano.” Moreover, she extra snorts at
the clingsomeness of a London audience to a
favorite. She saw Maud Allan, the vaunted
danseuse , and saw a played-out show. The fact
that Londoners keep a show going for much time
is not evidence of merit—it is only evidence that
there are an immense number of Londoners.
A “name” is all a mummer or dancer
or singer wants in London. One single
“hit” starts the ball rolling, and by the
time it ceases to revolve, that performer
should have much money in the stocking. It
takes some time for a fairly large fraction of
London, say 1,500.000 people, to go and see for
themselves what this talked-of wonder is like.
They need not repeat the dose. Even at 12,000 a
week it takes many weeks to exhaust 1,500,000.
Millions are horrible things to get thinking about
—they take so long to count. So do billions. A
mathematical master in my early school-youth
staggered me by announcing that, had Adam
begun counting a billion in the early rural days,
he n might have “got there” about now—had he
lived. It seems to me that it might have kept
that muddled progenitor out of mischief.
J. A. Miller, who directs the prosperity of
West’s Pictures in Melbourne, possesses a brace
of daughters who do him proud. There are three,
in fact, but the youngest is not yet “out.” One
has been so useful in the Pictures business that she
—the fair Miss Phyllis—is to depart next month for
London and a long, restful holiday trip, as the
guest of West. The other, Miss Molly, a blue-
eyed girl with a temperament, a tip-tilted nose,
and a few teens to her age, has just returned from
an 18 months’ riot in Manila, staying with the
Joneses.
Jones is an American of importance, who
undertook to sanitate Manila with up-to-date
American sewerage, and a point of hurrah to
Australia is that every inch of piping in the huge
Manila-cum-American contract is being made in
Melbourne, papa Miller looking after the ship-
ment. Whenever the Melbourne girl showed
signs of home-sickness the Joneses would drive her
past vast piles of her country’s newly-imported
sewer pipes, whereat the young Melbourne lady
would breathe the familiar air and be comforted.
Trippers from Manila usually indulge in stories
that are hot; but the “ nice people,” according to
Miss Molly, don’t go the glad and giddy pace in
outward whirling. The “ cream ” entertain Jtq.
gether in their own homes, and do the polite and
legitimate form of fleecing with Bridge parties.
Their everyday programme seems to be to ( get up
with the lark, or its tropical feathery-tailed
equivalent, and do anything that in that lazy
land might be called work., (The natives —lazy
beggars ! —do the chores, and a white has half a
dozen of them to wait on her for each finger of
each of her hands.) Then the white man hoists
the white man’s burden by having tiffin, and
droops qff to a siesta. At four o’clock everybody—
all-the-world—us—drive on the Luneta, a serpen-
tine esplanade gay with side parterres of tropical
shrubs and with a band playing. The natives
are most musical. Their music is on a higher
plane-than the average nigger melody. Like the
negro minstrelsy, it is weird and haunting and sad.
The constabulary band that plays on the Luneta
has since toured the States, and there it was
counted one of the best.
A woman’s chief servant in Manila is her,
lavateur or washerlady. She is much more
workable and more considerate and cheaper than
her Australian prototype. In Manila it takes a
whole complete and extensive room for hanging
the week’s white dresses, just on their snowy
lone.
Miss Miller did not return to Melbourne by a
direct route. She journeyed in a roundabout way
that took her to San Francisco and such places,
and dumped back by a Vancouver boat for
Sydney. One of the Fleet pressmen travelled on
the same boat, hastening to his paper in Los
Angeles (Cal.). Clotworthy was the man—that
well-groomed personage among the visiting Fleet
inkslingers with a bearing like an important
statesman and a face that might have meant 17
years or 70. Clotworthv spoke of the Australian
reception with enthusiasm, vowing that it was
very much the best in the bunch, and that officers
and men enjoyed it most. At Manila there were
restrictions about going ashore. The plague was
raging, and great was the rage thereof. That
plague was a terrible thing. Only the most
excessive cleanliness among the whites made it
possible to avoid the scourge. Dishes, plates and
cups were taken from the native servant with
gloved hands and plunged into an antiseptic bath
on the sideboard in view of the eaters before they
were used. One boarding-house matron was almost
lynched or convicted of bigamy with violence
because of the merest deviation from the Only
Way, her deviation having led to the death of
some of her boarders.
In Japan Clotworthy remarked on the wonder-
ful attention to detail throughout the country. At
his first dinner in Japan he refused wine, and
mentioned that he did not drink it. At every
subsequent dinner (in other Japanese cities) given
to Fleet followers, he discovered his glasses
turned down, and no attempt at wine service was
made. The small matter of taste and character-
istic fads of everyone of that American company
was studied and sent on ahead to other com-
mittees, or their prototype, in Monkeyland.
Accuracy and detail are strong points with the
wily Jap—except when he wishes (as he often
does) to convey an inaccuracy to outsiders.
From “ Rosna ” in Adelaide :
Mrs. William Campbell and her daughter Alice are back
after a year’s wanderings. They hastened across Spain,
climbed over Scotland, slipped through Italy, and saw
quite a quantity of the small isle on which Edward VII.
sits wearing his golden crown and upon his golden throne
in the Tower of London. The charming daughter’s engage-
ment was announced just after her return, the prospective
Benedict being Mr. W. Strachan. Both prospectives are
still very youthful, so the sacrifice will not occur for
some time.
David Mawson, Adelaide’s special Antarctic lion or
Polar bear, came home from getting up the Pole last
week, and the ’Varsity boys played “See the Conquering
Hero ”in his train all over the landscape. They invaded
the station in bulk, and kidnapped Mawson under tile
very eyes of the Faculty, took him up to North Terrace,
put him in a handcart, and trundled him with much
triumphant bump and noise to the University'. They had
a brand new jingle, which they sang at him diligently'.
Professor David, when he was associating with seals and
other meat, wrote reams of nice things about Mr. Maw-
son, who is quite young but a truly giddy marvel in the
matter of intellect. But for him the expedition wouldn’t
have got as far towards the Pole as it did. So there now !
Adelaide scores again.
We had an epidemic of weddings the other Wednesday,
and old Hymen was worked so hard that he was found in
a fainting condition on his front doormat afterwards.
Early in the morning Loudie Pizey was joined to her
cleric, the Rev. E. W. Hines, who has the cure of
Anglican souls at Quorn. The ceremony was quiet, but
at 11 a.ni. the little bride’s friends were bidden to the
' Lyric Club, to wish her luck, and devour the festive
victual. She was one of the club’s first members, and has
helped to spread mirth and melody over its countenance.
But now she goeth forth to the wilds of Quorn, and the
Club pipeth its eye.
On the same day, the Holden-Peacock marriage spread
much traffic over the region of Flinders-street, and the
church had a ton of vegetation about its ears. And on
the same day St. Peter’s College chapel was beauteousl v
arrayed when one of the Seppelt damsels was made the
wife of Mr. Tandy'. This pair has gone wandering among
the Blue Alsatian Mountains, the South Sea Isles, and
other far-off places on a long, long honeymoon.
Nigel Brock ran through Adelaide on his way' to
Westralia last week as forerunner to the three Cherni-
avski youngsters. He only stayed long enough to glance
at his people and say that he would be back presently.
The complicated Russian or Polish youngsters, with
their sundry melody', have arrived, and are giving us a
brief season at the Town Hall. Their new Exes, have
showered their patronage on the infants.
The Rowing- Club closed its season with a thud on the
17th, and closed it so well that we defy anyone to open it
for another six months. The occasion was coincidental
with the christening by Mrs. E. VV. van Senden of the
new “eight,” and there was tea in the boathouse under
idyllic weather circumstances. We couldn’t have had
' better weather if we’d had the first coupon and reserved
it beforehand.
Down at Narracoorte they are discovering caves while
you wait. It’s most exciting. Early next month the
Admiral has promised to go down and formally thrust
open the very latest discovery, which is to be called the
Alexandra cave. The whole neighborhood is going to
sound the loud timbrel to celebrate the occasion. But
the neighborhood is so hollow with caves that if it sounds
too much timbrel it may fall right through and be swal-
lowed up. ....
Rather chill weather blew over Victoria Park for the
Chief Justice Ways’great Masonic garden spree on Friday.
Still it was fine, which was more than any good Mason
had the right to expect, seeing that the country is going
to the dogs or the elephants or some other mammals for
want of rain. There were flags by the furlong in the
atmosphere, and there was a sumptuous afternoon coffee
to be had in the tea room or at little tables outside. And
scrambling and tearing of hair were not. The hostess was
decked in a smart fawn raiment with beauteous applique
bespread on it, and pale blue plumes in her toque.
Mrs. Edwin Thomas wore a charming tailor-made prune
cloth, and a prune toque with a queer-toned blue feather
in it ; and Mrs. Mayfield had a braided black coat and
skirt, with forget-me-nots in a pretty toque. It was too
dull as to weather for very resplendent chiffons. The
Lewis Cohens were there, surrounded by a clan of old
friends ; and there were the Walter Wises, from Tas-
mania, and the David Griffithses, from Victoria, to say
nothing of 2976 more. One Mason came in evening dress
and a bowler hat, but he seemed very happy about it !
The late W. Shakspeare’s birthday party occurred at
the Lyric rooms on Friday eve, when the University
Shakspeare Society (which is so called because it has no
known connection with the University) gave a perform-
ance of said William’s works in fragments. You were in-
vited to same and asked to pay a shilling, and besides the
drama there was music. The show included a scene from
“Cymbeline,” and a scrap from “Romeo and Juliet,”
and an excerpt from “ The Taming of the Shrew,” and a
slice of “The Merchant of Venice.” The dressing of
“ Cymbeline ” was of a truly gorgeous style. Some good
work was achieved, particularly by two damsels, Ella Jiirs
arid Muriel Craigie. The former’s elocuting of “Portia’s
Lovers ” was a particularly bright circumstance.
Something new and thrilling in the prodigy line has
turned up on this horizon. It is a ten-year-old composer
infant named Merle Robertson, whom pianist Willie
Silver discovered at Petersburg or somewhere. He intro-
duced her to his students the other eve, and the kiddy
performed a Wedding March, “ Spanish Dance,” and
sundry other things of her very own composition—per-
formed them moreover with wondrous skill for her brief
years.
Yours affectionately,
J OHANNA.
Thus The Bulletin’s Oldest Inhabitant :
The Star of Saturday, in an article on the early post
offices of Sydney, gave a portrait of Mr. James Raymond,
the third postmaster, as being that of Isaac Nichols, who
was the first gazetted postmaster. Ikey came out in 1791
in the Admiral Barrington, and in course of time became
clerk to the naval officer or collector of Customs, Robert
Campbell “of the Wharf.” As clerk, Nichols had the
duty of collecting all rnailsfrom ship captains and sorting
the letters, etc. Acting-Governor Foveaux appointed
him postmaster, and Macquarie confirmed the appoint-
ment. His house in George-street was the post office.
Nichols died in 1819 and was succeeded by George
Panton. On his death, the office of p.-m. was
left in abeyance; the mails being sorted on the
premises of the Colonial Secretary and distributed
at the back door of the office in Bent-street. In 1829 Mr.
Raymond was appointed postmaster, and he held office
until his death on May 29, 1851. If Mr. Raymond knew
that he was doing duty per portrait for Ikey Nichols, he
would turn in his grave in old St. Peter’s Cemetery, on
the Cook’s River-road. The same article stated that
Nichols’ house site was now occupied by the fire-station
in Lower George-street. The fire-station stands where
stood Mrs. Reiby’s house. Nichols’s was further north.
“8.A.” discourses on the habits of some
Governors :
Lord Dudley’s uncompromising remarks at the Sydney
Agricultural Show to the appalling little men who got up
and squealed in his presence about the alleged follies of his
(Dudley’s) advisers ought to have been made long ago by
former holders of his job. The abuse that the present G.-G.
took exception to has been a regular feature in the past of
every public function, from club dinners to kirk-founda-
tion stone-layings which vice-regality and Fat attended
contemporaneously. A chronic victim of it was Lord
Northcote. He was an amiable person,,well-meaning and
right-minded. But he lacked strength of character. It
caused him anguish during the Watson regime to have to
sit and listen while Fat, under the pathetic delusion
that it was winning his approval, abused his Ministers and
all their deeds. However, he never summoned up the
nerve to protest publicly. His method was to send for
the offending orator afterwards, point out the “ gross bad
taste ” of his conduct, and implore him never to do any-
thing of the sort again—an utterly futile proceeding, of
course, and one that would only have been adopted bv a
total stranger to the country, and one who was imbued wi th
a false conception of the sense of decency possessed by
local Tories. ’ The politician invariably said he wouldn’t
do it again, and hastened to go back on his assurance on
the next opportunity. Governor Rawson was similarly
plagued, but he was a good, easy man, who
didn’t worry much about anything. No Governor
in the history of the Australian States has
ever abrogated such functions as a Governor pos-
sesses under the Constitution in so whole-hearted a
fashion as the bluff mariner who is now on his way to
England. Sir Harry declined to think for himself on any
toi ic. He left every blessed thing to his Ministers. If
they thought fit, which they often did, to rail at the
Opposition policy in his presence—well and good. He
argued that it was their affair. He was in the embarrass-
ing position of apolitical neutral, and gentlemen would
respect that neutrality ; but if it was his bad luck to be sur-
rounded by folk who didn’t know how to play the game,
the only thing was to take it philosophically. That was
the way N.S.W.’s ex-Governor explained privately his
public attitude in this regard. Of course he was never
placed in quite the position tha,t Lord Dudley was the
other day, inasmuch as his Ministers were of the Tory
persuasion during the whole of his tenure of office, and
Labor comports itself socially in a far more seemly
fashion than Toryism. But in any event there is no
reason to think he would have kicked. He wasn’t that
sort. Lord Dudley’s vigorous talk was good business
from a tactical point of view. (The crowd likes a straight
hitter.) Also, it is going to make things more comfort-
able for him personally as a fair-minded and self-respect-
ing man. And it promises to save future G.G.’s of his
way of thinking the unpleasant necessity of speaking
out. All round it was a shrewd and commendable move.

“ Politics are vulgar when they
are not liberalised by history.”
POLITICS MEAN MORE in Australia than
they do in England, where Sir John Seeley’s
memorable saying loses something of its
appeal, for the simple reason that in England so
many people do quite well without politics at all.
To the Englishman, “the man in the street,”
politics are a distant and confused spectacle, from
which the great issues seldom emerge unobscured.
In Australia, on the other hand, there is no “ man in
the street,” and the great problems are very present
•in the less encumbered perspective. They have
been described as
a series of contradictions.
Australia’s commercial existence among the
nations still rests primarily upon wool, and the position
of a world supplier of raw products may be argued
to entail the correlative of free imports ; but the
country is devoted to Protection. Wool, again,
marches well with large holdings and a sparse
population, with a society highly individualistic and
patriarchal; but the country is devoted to another
ideal. The claims of centralization are obvious ; but
state rights and suspicions die hard. Australia of
the “ colonial ” days set up its house in a hemisphere
so vast and unclaimed that thought of the future
might well lose itself in the immense distances. The
nearer distance, indeed, remains vast enough to a
population of four millions in a continent of some
three million square miles. But meanwhile, in the
twinkling of an eye, the further horizons have closed
in. The overflowing East, astir at last, seems all too
near to. a land devoted to whiteness. Japan, the
United States, Germany, factors in the game un-
dreamed of a generation back, have- swept forward.
Australia finds herself within the currents of a
possible new storm centre of world politics, and with
what equipment ? She is part of an empire, com-
posite indeed, but capable of that naval predominance
to which alone, for years to come, Australia can
fairly look for security. How far, then, may she
reasonably indulge an inclination to keep for herself
what must yet count, if it is to count much, as
a contribution to a common fund of strength ?
Where do you get your opinions ?
Here are problems that invite opinions, nay com-
pel them—indeed, the burden of entertaining opinions
is one that none think of trying to escape. But
where do they come from ? How are they formed ?
How should they be formed ? For upon the attitude
of each individual in the community the issue
depends, and not upon the wisdom and foresight of a
few. They whose gift or trade it is to express
political opinions are accustomed to do so with a
scorn that should make such as think differently
ashamed to be wrong where it is so easy to be right.
But in his own mind every man knows that he cannot
be sure of following his feelings, or his party, or even
his favorite organ, to a sound conclusion, and there
are moments when party moves and anything like
special pleading seem as poor as feelings are crude.
One greatly desires a finer basis —and this is where
Sir John Seeley’s sentence has its application :
“ Politics are vulgar when they are not liberalised by
history.”
The school of political judgment.
The experience of the human race—there is but
this one school of political judgment, for it is all that
we know. The commonest of sayings in regard to
history is that it repeats itself, and it is this sameness
under infinitely various guises which, in giving history
its surpassing fascination, renders it also the most
potent agent of enlightenment. Land tenure ; the
relation between the state and the individual, whether
as regards taxation, the fostering of industries, or the
adoption of measures for the inducement of personal
good conduct; the status of religion ; the move-
ments of trade; the attempt to “level-up” between
the poor and the rich ; the relations of states to a
federal government, and of colonies to a mother
country ; sea power and land defence —these things
are precisely the material of history throughout all
ages and in all countries.
The oldest complaint in the world.
To imagine that political problems here and now
are something apart and radically different from those
which have been wisely or unwisely faced in the past,
this in itself is the beginning of political error. If
the history published by The Times did no more
than relieve its readers of this blindness, the benefits
would amply repay the labor of its production. What
has a more “ modern ” sound than the complaint of
decadence in a civilisation which has already reached
its prime ? It is the oldest reflection that has come
down to us. The Historians’ History of the World
quotes from an Egyptian document known as “the
oldest book in the world,” written perhaps 4000 years
8.C., to show that thoughtful Egyptians of that time
regarded civilisation as being past its zenith. They
were tired of the degenerate days in which they lived,
and looked back to the good old days when the
Egyptians, as it seemed to them, were a great people.
Of Egyptian history as a whole—to single out the
most distant from ourselves —the Editor-in-chief of
the History says : —“ When one considers the ancient
resident of the valley of the Nile as a human being,
with desires, emotions, and aspirations almost pre-
cisely like our own ; a man struggling to solve the
same problems of practical Socialism that we are
struggling for to-day—then, and then only, can the
lessons of ancient Egyptian history be brought home
to us in their true meaning and with their true
significance.”
Socialism 800 B.C.
Amongst the earliest traditions in Greek history
are the laws given to Sparta by Lycurgus. Finding a
prodigious inequality in wealth, he had the old
divisions of land cancelled and new ones made, that
all might be equal. To the same end he practically
eliminated the use of money by changing the coinage
from gold and silver to iron, so that a yoke of oxen
was required to remove the equivalent of He
instituted public tables where all ate in common of a
meal determined by statute. Has the most advanced
Socialism ventured further ? The record of the
Greek states is one long commentary upon the
subject of federation ; and its central episode, the
thirty years’ war between Athens (who centralised her
power by accepting her colonial contributions in
money instead of in ships) and Sparta, is the struggle
between the imperial ambitions of a democratic naval
power and the disruptive forces of conservative
particularism. The accumulation of land in the
hands of a few is the central theme of Roman history
from the Carthaginian war to the time of Ciesar—and
similarly through all the ages, and in every country,
the reader sees the same humanity failing or succeed-
ing in the face of problems fundamentally alike, so
that the last event explained in the Historians’
History of the World, the passing of the Surplus
Revenue Act by the Federal Parliament last year,
sounds no very different note from what else the
reader may have seen chronicled in any of the six
thousand years during which the doings of mankind
are traceable.
Ignorance of history is a wilful waste of
experience.
It is not that one can “ look up ” a burning
question in even the fullest and best-arranged library
of history, and settle it as one may the gender of a
Greek noun by reference to the lexicon. The
political value of historical reading is by no means
limited to the discovery of actual parallels, instructive
though they be. But from the reading of history
comes the right attitude, the habit of historical
thinking, which is better than historical information.
A mind thus exercised is not a prey to shibboleths,
is not shaken by every gust that blows. An imagina-
tion fed upon history is filled with ideals that are not
illusions. The eye that has seen into the past can
look beyond the immediate present. To be con-
versant with what has happened in the lives of
nations gives a balance, a mellowness, not to be
derived from the most enlarged personal experience,
and a faith that can see without dismay many suns
go down upon hopes unfulfilled. To this end every
page tells, nor is one passage in the great epic to be
preferred to another. The glory of Venice is not the
less good to know because the readers’ lines are cast
in Coolgardie, nor the quarrel of the investitures the
less true because he puts his trust in neither popes
nor princes.
Germany and “world-histories.”'
The Times takes credit to itself for this, that in
the volumes of the Historians’ History of the World
it has, for the first time in 130 years, rendered pos-
sible for readers of English the entry into this common
heritage of mankind’s experience. This is a self-
conscious age. The future is uneasily canvassed at
every step. The business for each one now is to be
conscious with effect, constructively. It is conscious-
ness rendered effective by culture that has carried
Germany so far in the last half-century, and no one
who knows the country will under-rate the part that
has been played by the diffusion of historical know-
ledge—knowledge not of their own record only, but
of the experience of other nations also. Ever since
Schlosser, in the middle of last century, issued his
“World History for the German People,” the presses
of Germany have sent out a never-ending stream
of universal histories : Ranke, Weber, Rottock,
Bekker, Leo, Weiss, Oncken, one can easily count
eight of conspicuous popularity, many of which have
gone through numerous editions—-Schlosser’s is now
in its 20th (18 volumes).
•
Indeed, culture pays. The British mind the
world over admits the truth with somewhat of an ill
grace ; but history possesses this great recommenda-
tion that it affords such reading as surpasses all other
in interest. The most exclusive lovers of fiction,
once introduced to history of the right kind, must
find their allegiance waver.
A means of forming your own judgment.
A prospectus is necessarily concerned with a
recital of contents and authors, with descriptions of
plan and arrangement. The Times has endeavored
to make the prospectus of the Historians’ History
explicit in all such matters. Nevertheless it believes
that the specimen pages and illustrations included in
the prospectus, and the resume given of each volume,
will enable the reader amply to satisfy himself also as
to the enthralling interest of a history constructed
above all to be readable. This 84-page prospectus is
sent post free for the asking.
A material consideration.
In opening to the English reading public a realm
hitherto closed to all but the student, The Times
does not take credit to itself for offering with one
hand what it withholds with the other. Anyone who
wishes to do so can see the prospectus, and anyone
who has examined the prospectus will be able, should
he wish, to buy the History itself. For the printed
form of subscription sent with the prospectus shows
that the Historians’ History of the World is purchas-
able by a series of monthly payments of only 7s. 6d.,
the first of which secures immediate delivery of the
complete 25 volumes (with an oak bookcase to hold
them, if desire J). And the total price thus paid is.
less than a quarter of the price at which biographies,
travels, and other new books of general interest are
published. The Times intended the price to
register a record, and believes that the History is
indeed the cheapest new book ever published. If
you would be interested, however, to see the pros-
pectus, there is a good reason why you should write
for it at once. The low price and easy terms of pur-
chase apply only to the subscription edition, the sale
of which has already closed in London, and will now
shortly close in Australia also. You should therefore
use the attached form of inquiry at once.
This Inquiry Form, duly filled in, will go for Id. in an open
envelope, and will receive immediate attention at any of the three
offices of The Times [London] in Australia: Sydney, 7 O’Connell
Street; Melbourne, 75 Elizabeth Street; Perth, Forrest Chambers, St .
