ROSY CHEEKED
AUSTRALIAN GIRLS
Are the Girls who keep their
Blood Rich and Red with
DR. WILLIAMS’ PINK PIKLS
FOR PALE PEOPLE.
Every Australian girl should be bright, active,
cheerful, happy. There is no reason why any
girl shouldn’t be who has enough good red blood
in her veins. She should sleep well, and take the
most out of life. There is not the slightest
shadow of a doubt that girls who don’t enjoy this
good health have the blood to blame. Anasmia is
the enemy of girls and women. A lack of suffici-
ent good red blood causes all those headaches and
sideaches, weakness, paleness and languor. To
become bright, well and happy, the blood supply
must be increasing, and to make new blood there’s
only one thing—Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. They
represent the highest achievement in medical
science as a blood restorer and nerve tonic.
Take the case of Miss Mary Blakley, 91 Thomas
Street, Ultimo, Sydney; it’s so like hundreds of
others that scores must saj r , “ That’s just the way
I am.” She stated :—■
“When I was 17 I became anaemic. The mere
smell of food turned me sick. For months Hived
on bread and butter and water, no tea or meat.
I had black circles round my eyes. My face
hadn't a scrap of color in it. You could see
through my ears and hands. I had fainting turns
and my ankles swelled up a lot. I was afraid to
go out for fear a weak turn would take me in the
street. I grew so weak that I could hardly get
about. I had no strength whatever ; even to do my
hair was a trouble. If I tried to go upstairs I
had to rest half way, my heart thumped so that I
thought I was going to die. My people were
terribly worried about me. I had nasty dragging
pains across my back, and was nearly driven mad
with neuralgia. My hands and feet were always
as cold as stones. The doctor said I was on the
point of going into consumption. I took a lot of
medicines but I derived no benefit. Then I read
about Ur. Williams’ Pink Pills and made up my
mind to try them. After the second box I found
my appetite was better, and I began to take a
little interest in things around me. The third
box made my lips quite rosy, and a good color
came into my cheeks. My folks were delighted.
Evert' day saw me steadily improving. By the
time I had finished the fifth box I was quite
changed for the better. I was .able to work about
the house. The sixth box completely cured me.
To look at me now you wouldn’t think that I had
ever been ill.”
While so promptly effective these pills contain
no harmful stimulants or opiates. They are per-
fectly safe and create no drug habit.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are sold by chemists
and storekeepers, or sent by mail, post paid on
receipt of price, 2s. 9d. per box, six boxes 15s. 3d.,
by the Dr. Williams Medicine Co. of Australasia
Ltd., Sydney.

POLITICAL
POINTS
S.A. is getting tired of being regarded
merely aa a wheat-growing State. The great
show of home-made goods lately put up in all the
shops by concerted agreement has set the Chamber
of Manufactures (which is in its 40th year) count-
ing things. It finds 1086 factories, employing
18,408 males and 4227 females. They use in a
year nearly £6,000,000 worth of raw material, pay
£1,750,000 in wages, and have an output of over
£9,000,000. Kather unexpectedly, metal and
machinery works (including those of the Govern-
ment) come out as the class of industry employing
most workmen—nearly 7000.
Sydney City Council, which sees no pros-
pect of converting the Queen Victoria Markets
into anything like a paying asset, has, as a last
hope, urged the Australian Government to
take over the whole thing and make a
general post office of it. Sydney’s G.P.O.
suffers from the same old curse which afflicts the
railway station and other structures—the builders
didn’t look far enough ahead. The only people
who seem to look far enough ahead are those who
build churches. But the idea of a complete new
G.P.O. is a very big order.
Under the N.S.W. Land regulations
administered by the Land Board of 1829 (the
Board consisted of William Stewart, Lieutenant-
Governor ; William Lithgow, auditor; and
Captain Dumaresque, engineer), a special grant
of land was made to every Australian-born woman
on her marriage. The concession ran : “ That to
every Australian-born lady, on her marriage, there
shall be given two square miles of land for farm-
ing purposes.” Few women applied for the land,
and most of those who did apply promptly sold out.
So, after a few years, that plan of settlement was
buried.
Ex-Premier Bent’s Victorian Gold Buyers’
Act, passed at the behest of the Judkinses and
certain mine-owners of the State, actually makes
it impossible for a private individual to buy speci-
mens, for keepsakes or for gifts, without break-
ing the law. Lately, Con Burrow, trustee of the
Balhausen Rifle shield, which the Ballarat team
recently won in competition with the Coldstream
Guards, wished to send to his old regiment, the
Grenadier Guards, a few specimens, but found
that to do so he would either have to buy a gold
mine or go fossicking and find the articles. And
he isn’t in the humor to do either.
Robert Lowe makes Lord Stanley impart
to his secretary in the Colonial Office this piece of
blank-verse description of N.S.W.’s early Parlia-
ment :
You have doubtless heard
That New South Wales has got a Constitution ;
Such .an Assembly as, I should think, never was
Seen since the time of Romulus—all thieves ;
Several who have not yet received their pardons ;
And Stephen says they voted it a breach
Of privilege to pick a member’s pocket
While in debate engaged.
The late Chief Justice Alfred Stephen did, it
appears, indulge his vein of humor to the effect
cited.
“Red Tape Junior ”
The N.S.W. Public Service had its annual conference
while its country cousins were in town. The chief
matter discussed was the right of appeal from the deci-
sions of the Public Service Board other than to the
blessed Board itself. Many of the leading lights of the
conference believe that Section 10 of the Act wasn’t
meant by Parliament to mean that the right of appeal
conferred by the section from the decisions of the Board
was to be only an appeal to the B ard which gave the
decision. It is the general view in the service that no
one in or out of it does really know what Section 10
means, and the P.S. Association is to ask :
(1) Will Wade, as Attorney-General, advise himself, as
Minister in charge of the Board, what Section 10 intends
to say ?
(2) Will he, as Minister in charge, instruct the Board to
be guided by his own advising as Attorney-General ?
(3) Will he, as Minister, take proceedings against him-
self, as Attorney-General, in the law courts to decide the
signification of good old Section 10?
(4) As Premier, will he pass a brand new No. 10?
So we ought to get something settled some way or other.
THE PROPHET.—(MODERN STYLE.)
Mr. James A. Fatten, the American grain speculator,
whp has been forcing up the price of wheat by his opera-
tions on the,Chicago and New York markets, prophesies
that “ before many months the people of Europe will have
to struggle for bread in famine-stricken queues.”—Cable-
gram.
Mr. Patten, who made a profit of nearly £1,000,000 by
his recent operations, is in fear of personal violence, and
has engaged several detectives to protect him.— T’other
Cablegram.
The London Spectator, not talking about
Australia’s Federal capital, but hitting"’ it very
hard by implication:—
Washington was chosen to avoid the jealousy of the
States ; Madrid, for a similar reason, and one might
think that in putting it on an arid plateau its founders
had thrust a pen on to the most central spot on a map of
the country, a,nd said without any further consideration,
“ There shall be the capital.” ... No city can have real
greatness unless it stands on a considerable river.
We learnt at school that Madrid was “o.i the Maii-
zanares,” and the conventional phrase lent, a certain
dignity, to the picture in one’s mind. But the Man-
zahares, when you come to see it in reality, is a wisp of
water trickling among wastes of sand, and husbanded by
washerwomen in the hot months, lest the raw material
of their industry should fail.
In fact, the Manzanares is the Cotter over again.
AUSTRALIA.
NOTE.— The largo Blank Patch is SYDNEY. The white spot, like a dead caterpillar, in the
middle of the Lame Black Patch. is the Federal Cental. Those noble streams, the Cotter and
Motrmglo Hirers, arc shown plainly.
“ Oby ” writes :
New South Wales wastes a lot of good money over its
Circuit Courts. The ordinary District Courts and one
touring Supreme Court Judge could take up all the cases,
criminal and civil, that now disorganise the Supreme
Court Bench and absorb buckets of public money. Cir-
cuit Court Judges often go to places where no
work awaits them. They take with them Associates,
Crown Prosecutors, and other costly parapher-
nalia. Panels of jurors are also brought together
at mileage rates, and the Judge collects the offering of
white gloves and tells the jurymen that they are not
wanted. Crown Prosecutors who know their way about
and make inquiries have been, known to pocket their 50
guineas fee in Sydney without taking a single step to-
wards the place where they are supposed to prosecute the
prisoner that doesn’t exist. If they added a bill for the
travelling expenses they didn’t incur while going to the
place they didn’t go to, it is believed they would still be
within their rights. A lawyer has so many rights that it
is difficult to know where they begin and where they end.
In Victoria cockying is reckoned to be
less horribly uncertain than in most other parts of
Australia; yet if you cut
the 1908-9 wheat and oat
harvest averages of Vic-
toria in halves, you get
almost exactly tiie Vic-
torian averages for 1907-8.

though the cooky never

to get 26 bushels, or 13, or

trouble to harvest his Mal-
lee lands five years ago),
the Foreign-trade crowd
hasn’t even now quite
given up the yarn that
Australia should stick to
the “great natural in-
dustry” of eoekving, and
never mind about the
“ artificial manufacturing
industries.” Neither wheat
nor oats are “natural” to
Australia, in the sense of
being natives of the
country ; but iron is. Still
a small leak like that
doesn’t trouble a Foreign-

the argumentative tear.
“ There’s a business-
like Government for you ! ’
said the delighted Fats of
the Commonwealth when
Maoriland’s Joe Ward
weighed out with a Dread-
nought ; “ it docs things.”
it does. For instance, it
did tins the other day
(Government Gazette) :
On and after the first day of February, 1909, the
maximum weight of any sack containing wheat, barley
or oats to be carried by the Railways shall be 2001 b. Any
sack of wheat, barley or oats containing a greater weight
than 2001 b. shall be charged for at four times the ordinary
freight.
It was the sort ot thing that the Australian State
Insects talked about for months and then didn’t
do, because Bat wasn’t willing, and thus they forced
the Commonwealth to try what it could do on its
own account.
There was a pleasant family party at the
Yic. Employers’ Federation the other night,
when Secretary Walpole read a paper on the need
for immigration. Walpole’s views were neither
new nor interesting, hut when he had waded
through his screed Senator Sledger observed that
the. stagnation was due to that party that
wanted to prevent borrowing. Then that relic of
the Victorian Four, F. E. Smith, discovered that
“ Australia was a fine country, but if her people
would not use it, some other people would seize
it ” —which was a nasty jar for the class to which
Smith belongs, the Fat Landlords. After him,
Fairbairn got off the bright remark that “nobody
is allowed to do anything”; and McKet'chup,
the loneliest Tory in the Cabbage Garden, assured
his hearers that what Australia wanted was “the
expenditure of two or three hundred millions in
development.” The report is sawn off here—
probably even the Tories were staggered by the
gorgeousness of McKetchup’s “remedy.”
G-. H. Reid told a Sydney suburban liter-
ary society, the other night, that in his youth he
was ridiculed by older men. But one day, he
said, the worm turned. “And,” he drawled, “it’s
—been—turning—ever—since.” This accounts
for George’s frequent changes of policy, also for
his giddiness.

The Government should show that the telephones cost
more than the revenue before asking the people to pay
more.—President Fairbair.v, M.P., at a recent meeting
Victorian Employers' ( A nti-Socialistic) Association.
The advantage t > the people was held to counter-
oalance any loss, and the loss was spread over the
population.— Another Anti-Socialistic speaker at the same
meeting.

Who’s as great an anarchist as any Russian, sir 1

Which—er—brings me to the subject of discussion, sir.
Regarding telephones:
The Labor party (Groans)
Seems to think it can oppress us with impunity.

That the benefits we gain
Should be paid for by—that is—the whole community !

Will endeavor to restrain
His impatience, I’ll explain.

Mister Chairman, to resume.
When I look around this room
On the members of this—er—this great society,
And consider we’ve to pay .
For these benefits, I say
It’s —er—bordering on—um —on impropriety !
The —er —people, sir, should bear—
(What’s that? ... I protest, sir ! Chair !
Tnis—er— person who seems bent upon confusing me
With his most unseemly din—
I don’t know how he got in
Cannot shake my argument, sir, by abusing me).
And I answer him, sir, Bosh !
Bosh ! How dare he call me Sosh 1
Our position is invinc—er—ah—invincible !
His remarks, sir, I resent,
And I hold my argument
Is a basic principle of—um —of principle !
As my worthy friend, Bruce Smith
(Bosh, sir !. .. Petriana myth !.. .

Um —er—break up fam’ly ties !...
Lies, sir ! Socialistic lies !
Alarming capital and immigration, sir !).
Now (Eh !. . . Order ! Sir, I claim
Your protection ! What’s his name ?
Is this man a member ? Sir, show your authority !
An impostor, I’ve no doubt.

CONSTIPATION AND LIVER
COMPLAINT.
A Well-Known Grocer Praises Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills for His Restoration
to Health.
Mr. W. H. Jones, of 19 Rosser Street, Rozelle,
one of Balmain’s oldest residents, tells in liis letter
to the proprietors of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills how this wonderful remedy, by regulating
the bowels and correcting the liver, restored him
to health and strength. With his kind per-
mission we publish his photo., together with his
letter, which is as follows :
“Some few years ago I suffered acutely from
constipation, liver complaint and greatly impaired
Mr. W. H. Jones.
(From a photo.)
health, and my whole sys-
tem became deranged. I
tried many medicines, but
nothing was as good a cor-
rective as Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills. They
put my bowels into perfect
working order, overcame
my liver troubles, and my
health became as well as I
could wish it. As a pur-
gative medicine I always
strongly recommend them,
and a dose occasionally
keeps me in the best of
health. I may add that the
most part of my life I have
spent in Balmain, and for 27
years I have kept this shop, and, therefore, I claim
to be one of the oldest residents of Rozelle, and
am, consequently, well known.”
Only about one manor woman in a hundred is per-
fectly healthy. The other 99 have some digestive
trouble, and perhaps more than 50 per cent, of
these could trace their trouble to that most pre-
valent evil—constipation. It’s a simple thing of
itself, but, like many simple things, it may grow
and become complicated. Constipation is the
root of nine-tenths of the sickness of man, and a
large proportion of the sickness of women. Nature
often requires a little assistance, and if this assist-
ance is given at the first indication much distress
and suffering may be averted.

DOWLING ST., REDFERN, SYDNEY,
SPORTING
NOTIONS
Sydney Metropolitan Rugby Union started its
competition on Saturday, and some thousands rolled up
to see the first bloodshed. They went away disappointed.
Not a man was killed or even biffed. Play was humorous,
for most of the teams showed a hideous lack of condition,
and a Rugby team - out of form is a clownish thing. At
Hampden Oval Sydney met North Sydney, and fell upon it.
The hillmen from over the water were soft as butter, and
the Sydney crowd were at least as hard as cheese ; and
the spectacle was a sorrowful one for the Northern bar-
rackers. The men onlookers from the North swore and
some of the girls wept bitterly. Other girls, dazed at the
turn of events, proposed “police interference” while
gazing with dry, anguished eyes. When it was o>er
Sydney had scored 26 points to three.
The surprise of the day was the sudden collapse
of the Eastern Suburbs men, who were contending with
Glebe. At the start, the Easterns had a stiff wind right
in their teeth, but playing up in an amazing' fashion,
they more than held their own. With a pack that wolfed
the leather and a back division that seemed as sharp as
needles, they hemmed the Glebe crowd round the goal
and then stormed the line. Right through the half
Glebe was penned up in its own territory, breaking out
spasmodically only to be hurled back. Iri one of these
sudden charges Glebe scored, and the wind carrying the
ball between the posts, made the records five all. Eastern
Suburbs having held its own so easily with the wind
against it, felt confident when the gale was behind it.
But the stress of that first half had been too fierce.
Quite suddenly the condition of the team gave out, like
that of the one-horse shay ; and over the remnants Glebe
stalked to victory; winning by 24 points to five. An
amazing collapse.
For the rest, Newtown and Manly played a
draw with three points each ; and the fact that Manly
can face a team like Newtown on level term indicates
that this season will see fulfilled the prophecy of The
Bulletin that it was merely a question of time till the
surf-bathing town took its place among the front-rankers
of the Union. The way the Villagers fought their uphill
fight during the season when they were down under
showed the team to be composed of the right stuff.
South Sydney and University did some very level peg-
ging, which resulted in favor of South by 11 to eight. But
the defeat of St. George by Western Suburbs was a pitiful
business. West let loose charge after charge that swept
through St. George like fire through tussock, and the
hand on the scoring board showed 20 to three.
• * • *
The League had eight teams in the field, and,
take it all round, its matches showed closer finishes than
those of the Union. The only walk-over was that of
Eastern Suburbs when it stepped on the corpse of Glebe.
It was a cake-walk in which the East scored 38 points
to Glebe’s five. This was fairly decisive, even for
Northern Union rules. Western Suburbs also downed
Newtown by 10 points to three ; while South Sydney,
escaping defeat by the skin of its teeth on a dozen
occasions, managed to win out by four points to nil.
Never did a team so often get to the point of all but
scoring as Balmain. Newcastle had a joyous trip, and
broke up North Sydney by 13 points to seven. All the
real excitement was at the League matches, where the
casualties were one broken leg and a solitary fractured
nose.
* * * *
In the Sydney Soccer competition for the Raw-
son Gup, in which a level dozen teams are competing,
Rozelle and Pvrmont, each with two wins, head the list
with four competition points each. Sydney and Glebe
make the second line with three each ; Garden Island,
Ultimo, and Balmain, have a pair ; while Newtown, the
Powerful, and Granville have one. Northern and Western
Suburbs have so far failed to score even a draw, and are
still commencing life with absolutely nothing.
The fact that Garden Island and the Powerful
have each a team in the field puzzles some people.
But the explanation is simple. Garden Island does not in-
clude sailors or marines. It is a club strictly reserved to
artificers, engineers, and such like aristocrats who would
not be seen dead in company with a blue jacket. This,
in the community’s present state of mind, strikes one as
humorous. But, of course, there are good reasons for
the position from a football point of view. The ships are
often away, and to rely upon the crack sailors would be a
blunder. So the permanent hands at the Isle have their
own club.
* a * *
Appearances point not only to huge attend-
ances at Melbourne football matches this season, which
begins next Saturday, but also to an extra-fierce bogging-
in of the various League and Association players, and to
a reckless but sub rosa splashing round of capital. Of
the 10 League clubs, eight have scoured the Common-
wealth for new talent, men from Maoriland, N.S.W.,
Queensland, Westralia. and S.A. having been tried in the
practice matches. Carlton has sampled no less than 13
possible recruits, Essendon 18, St. Kilda 22, Collingwood
10, South Melbourne 12, Fitzroy 20, Richmond 28, and
Melbourne 15. Geelong has tested but six from
outside its own district, and ’Varsity, though it
has the whole Commonwealth to choose from, is
in effect a strictly “district” club, in that its
players are chosen from past and present students
only. The committee that manages the Carlton
Ground at Prince’s Park, and the Collingwood Council
which bosses Victoria Park, have put up gorgeous new
stands—which the takings are presently expected to pay
(or—and already the jingle of coin can be heard as the
heavy turnstiles swing. Collingwood’s city fathers, with
a nice concern for possibilities, have struck the brilliant
idea of constructing a subway with separate entrances for
each team, direct from the playing area to the ’bailers’
dressing-rooms, so that the ill-regulated bloke with the
handy paling and the hysterical Lizzie with the vicious
hatpin may be baulked of their prey. Also, the
umpire will escape assault and battery—unless the
Lizzies aforesaid swarm over the fence, and “ get
to ” him on the field of battle itself. The
said umpire also seems eager to qualify for the
stoush and the two guineas that mostly represent his
day’s pay—no less than 213 of him having applied to the
League for appointment, and 200 to the Association. The
majority want to be field umpires. The boundary and
goal umpires come in for more damage and less pay. The
Association clubs have acquired a number of League
rejects and many promising juniors. The latter circum-
stance has caused strained relations among the junior
bodies, which naturally, but unsuccessfully, object to their
best players being enticed away. Besides these numerous
organisations the resuscitated Vic. Rugby Union will hav e
six clubs mauling each other for a trophy, and the Socce r
enthusiasts are preparing a small fray among themselves-
Certainly, Melbourne won’t go short of toeballthis season,
though the lack of enclosed grounds will compel all but
the 20 League and Association clubs to be strict amateurs.
Which is good for the morals if bad for the pocket.
* « * *■
The remnants of the N.S.W. Rugby League
team scrambled back on to Australian soil last week with a
feeling of thankfulness. But for the charity of the
Northern Union the blessed team would have been
stranded in the Cold Land, owing to the paucity of gate
money. Several members of the team stayed on with
English county clubs at fair salaries, and they are the
only ones who did any good out of the tour. The
Rugby Union treasurer is also wearing sackcloth
and ashes,. for the tramp abroad of the Wallabies
has resulted in a loss of £1392 13s. 4d. Paucity
of gate money is again the trouble; but still,
there might have been enough to go round if the team
hadn’t indulged in such Babylonian luxury. First-class
travelling on all the railways, the best hotels, and the
finest and oldest of liquors are said to have been the order
of the day, and the prettiest of masseuses were employed
to remove the battle stains. The All Blacks brought back
a profit of £9500 a couple of years ago ; but the Wallabies
only raked in £7787 16s. 7d. altogether. The simultaneous
appearance of two Australian teams is blamed for the
disaster, bub that is probably bunkum. England is pass-
ing through an awful slump, and thousands of people who,
two years ago, had money to splash on football, to-day
haven’t got it to spend on food. Anyhow, there will be no
more English fifteens for some little time to come.
* * * *
N.S.W. Rugby Union showed signs of being
leniently inclined when it came to dealing with the cases
of T. Griffin and A. B. Burge, who were ordered off and
suspended in England. In the case of Griffin, it was
decided that the Glebe player had been sufficiently pun-
ished, and the whitewash was administered right away.
Burge was not so lucky, but the council decided to ten-
tatively remove the suspension, so that Burge can play
for South Sydney while the English Union is forwarding
further particulars. So presumably Burge is as good as
out of the wood.
* * * •
Messrs. Beeby and Moffat, the honorary solici-
tors to the N.S.W. Rugby League, having given an
opinion that Messrs. Giltinan, Trumper, and Hoyle were
acting as agents and trustees of the Rugby League in
their transactions with various ground proprietors and
others, and that their contention that they were acting
personally is leaky, those gentlemen are to be called upon
to furnish accounts. The League formally adopted the
advice, and issued orders that Messrs. Giltinan, Trumper
and Hoyle be promptly called upon to furnish such
accounts. This is generally regarded as being the signal
for the band to play.
• * * »
As champion, with the right of choosing the
water, Dick Arnst has decided that the Wanganui River
(M.L.) shall be the scene of the race with Webb, and he
will begin his preparation right away. In spite of the
crushing defeat he experienced, Webb has a numerous
following, which, strange to say, is prepared to splash its
money readily. Father Tuck is again the Wanganui
blacksmith’s chief backer, and the fact that the party is
willing to risk another £5OO suggests that it is satisfied
that in the former race Webb was not at his best.
Brother Jack will train the champion, and it is said that
Peter Neilson, who trained Arnst for the former race, has
been offered the job of getting Webb into shape. As he
has not heard from Arnst, it is more than likely the offer
will be accepted.
* * * *
Still they come : Hagney, the mighty cyclist,
spends his days struggling to discover the art of balancing
an outrigger down Middle Harbor way (Sydney); and
now Tom Larcombe, the champion road-racing cyclist,
has also taken to the water. Peter Neilson, who coached
Dick Arnst, will be his mentor.
* * * *
Big Jack Arnqt, the M.L. cyclist, got a large
share of limelight on April 14. At 5 a.ni. he left Christ-
church (M.L.) in hurried pursuit of the Christchurch-
Dunedin bike record. It then stood to the credit of
A. Humm, another prominent M.L. bikist, who some
weeks ago covered the 245 miles between the towns in
the remarkable time of 14hrs. lmin. This was considered
by some experts to be a superhuman performance, but
Jack Arnst didn’t think so. Paced by two motor bicycles
and attended by a car and attendants, Arnst covered the
old M.L. Dunlop Road Race course to Timaru in shrs.
llmin., which was 44min. better than Humm’s time.
Filled with hope and a large quantity of food supplied
from the car, he fled south, breaking all the between-
towns records as he went. At Palmerston (205 miles) he
was over an hour and a half inside the previous best
figures. Then he struck the hills that surround
Dunedin. One motor bike refused to go on with the job,
and the other could not travel fast enough for Jack, who
was hungry for work and bursting to get to the end of it.
The motor car, however, was equal to the strain, and
stuck to him. Three punctures came along, and delayed
the anxious cyclist for twenty minutes. But he mended
his tyres and rushed onward, reaching the Scotch
metropolis at 5.21 p.m. He had covered the distance in
the marvellous time of 12hrs. 21min., and clipped
lhr. 40min. from the previous best. Until a fortnight
ago, the snail train took 9hrs. Bmin. to do the same
distance ! Arnst broke twelve records during his ride-
in fact he broke everything except his neck.
* * * *
The outcome of the Dunlop Military Despatch
ride from Adelaide to Sydney is that a certain number of
cyclists will be trained in each company of the Defence
forces for scouting and despatch work. The Minister for
War and the Military Board were much impressed with
the enthusiasm shown, and regard the mass of energy
and fervor represented by the aggregate cyclist through-
out the continent as too valuable an asset to be allowed
to run to waste. Meanwhile the Board is considering
how best to turn it to account.
«t * * Hr
Sydney Cricket Ground Trust is standing on
the verge of the biggest fall-in in its history. Both the
Rugby Union and the Cricket Association are kicking
against its scale of charges, and the 0. G. Trust shows a
disposition to prove the possession of a backbone. It ap-
parently has doubts about the earnestness of the Rugby
Union kick ; and is darkly suspected of being able to
exert a pull in the councils of that organisation. But the
Cricket Association is in very deadly earnest, and unless
the Ground Trust makes some concession, the chances
are that there will be no more cricket there for five years at
the least, and possibly for 10 years. The Association has
called for tenders from the controlling bodies of the
various public grounds in and around Sydney ; and it
is probable that the best offer will be accepted for five
years, with the right of renewal for another five. The
Ground asks for 10 per cent, of the gross takings, and
the Cricket Association retorts that, with about 3000
male ticket-holders and God knows how many female
ones doing the deadhead racket, it would lose money even
if it got the ground for nothing, as the Melbourne Asso-
ciation does.
The Bulletin would like to draw the Associa-
tion’s attention to Wentworth Park, which has been
improved enormously of late years. It is an ideal ground
from many points of view, and, with a little expenditure,
could be made first-class in the way of accommodation.
It is well served by trams, and the extra lines necessary
for the increased traffic could be laid down quickly. It
is in the centre of a big population, which under no cir-
cumstances would go to the Cricket Ground, for that
place, when you come to consider it, is right on the border
of nowhere. Quite recently the Cricket Ground Trust
donated £5OO to the Cricket Association. In the event of
the Ground refusing to come to reasonable terms,
the Association should loan the Wentworth Park people
that same £5OO with which to construct a home for
first-class cricket, at present being driven out of doors
by its grasping stepmother, the Sydney Cricket Ground
Trust. There is no reason why the Association should
have any compunction about using the £5OO donated by
the Trust to provide another ground. The money was
donated to foster cricket, and by releasing cricket from
the tentacles of the Ground octopus, the money will be
really spent to foster cricket. Again, considering the
wav the Ground has bled the Association in the past, the
“ donation ” was not really a donation, but merely a i
partial restitution. *
N.S.W. Cricket Association is firmly on its feet,
and finishes the season with a credit balance of £1336.
* * *• *
Club cricket in Adelaide, coming to a tardy
close, shows the batting list headed by Claxton, Caust,
Dolling, Hack, Pellew, Leak and Mayne (Gehrs was 24th,
and Clem Hill hardlv played at all). The top seven all
averaged over 50 (Claxton was 68). Biggest aggregate
was Dolling’s, the highest score Pellew’s 215 (but “ Solly ”
Hill, Clem's little brother, made 211 not out). In bowling
the leaders were Pretty, VVhitty, Reedman, Webb,
Coombe, Wright and Travers. O’Connor, who only rises
to big occasions, and has by’ the terms of his engagement
to play for the weakest club on the list, comes 34th.
Travers took most wickets, as he did continuously in the
seasons from 1900 to 1905. In fact, the one striking
feature of the season (except that S.A. won two Shield
matches, instead of its usually invariable single win) is the
success in Saturday games of Travers, Reedman, Hack
and Leak —all veterans who had been dropped from the
State XI.
* * * *
The appearance of automobile corps at the
Easter manoeuvres in N.S.W. and Victoria recalls the fact
that the German Government is just now building some
hundreds of armored automobiles for field work. One
Sydney newspaper expresses the opinion that autocars
will not be of much use in actual fighting, but this writer
begs to differ. The cars that the Germans are building
are bullet-proof as far as rifle fire is concerned, and are
made to accommodate each 40 men besides carrying quick-
firing artillery. For raiding purposes these cays will be
invaluable, and in attacking' positions will no doubt play
a prominent part. In armored motors it will be possible
to rush a small army of men across a fire-swept zone in
a very short space of time ; and the moral effect on
a force which is charged by a squadron of these
vehicles loaded up with rifles and machine artillery
worked by men behind armor will be fairly disastrous.
Properly handled the idea would enable a small attacking
force on anything like smooth ground, where there had
been no time to erect defences, to get to close quarters
and deal out fire at its own discretion till artillery
arrived. And, anyhow, at close quarters the rifle always
dominates the cannon ; so probably armored motor cars
would prove useful even for attacking artillery. The
trouble is that at present the cars that are being operated
in conjunction with our defence training are not military
cars, but costly and luxurious vehicles built only for
touring.
• * * *
Amateur athleticism in Maoriland has
brightened. The newly-patched-together agreement
among five centres contained a provision that the
old M.L. Amateur Athletic Association must burst up
and cease to be; and the heads of the old body have
therefore thrown in their boots for the feet of the new
body to wear. There is more harmony in the new Associ-
ation than in the old—so far, at any rate.
* * * *
The hypocrisy of the N.S.W. Girls’Swimming
Association, which recently ukased that one of its cham-
pions must not have her photo, published in a paper, was
again displayed in respect to the public schools’ girls’
carnival. This event was attended by at least a dozen
male persons, in defiance of the Association’s rule. If
the Association had the courage of its protests, it would
hasten to disqualify each of the 700 girls who took part
in that carnival; but obviously it is a flabby concern,
and is afraid to tackleanything with the power of clawing
back. The girls who attend private schools are also
under the ban, for their carnival was carried out under
the direction of male officials. Consequently, if the
Association dared to be honest, it would disqualify every
girl of any aquatic ability at present attending school in
Sydney. This, however, would leave very few to carry
out the future programmes of the alleged Association, so
it thinks it best, like the crook policeman, not to see too
much. But the solitary girl in bathing costume, whose
photo, seemed likely to’ go into, the papers, was only one
girl, and the Association’s valiant attitude towards one
girl is very valiant indeed.
* * * *
T. R. Allt, who died a while ago, left behind
ten shares in the North Coast S.N. Co. for the Balmain
(Sydney) bowlers to gamble over. One share will be the
yearly prize till the whole ten are disposed of, and the
struggle promises to be mighty.
• # * *
As might have been expected, Tahmmy Burns is
breaking out in a fresh place shorty, if he has
not already done so. He is going to teach the
Australian the theory and practice of baseball
as it is balled in Amurka. Tahmmy was a mighty
’bailer before the Lord in the days when he had
not attained to the rank of a first-grade pug, but all the
same there are nasty Australian ’bailers who prophesy
that he will yet start out to instruct his grandmother in
the scientific method of extracting albumen from the ova
of the phasianus.
* * * *
Among the contestants at the recent Wonder-
land (Melbourne) tournament was a half-caste Chinese
youth, who got as far as the semi-final and was very close
up to the lad who outpointed him. Though in one of
the lighter divisions, the Chow pug was a splendidly-
built fellow, and knew more of the game than
most of the competitors. So far, Chows haven’t shown
up much in the ring. A few years back Mick Nathan, of
Melbourne, put up, in the weird amphitheatre attached
to his British Lion Hotel in Lilly Buck-stleet, a scrap for
the alleged championship of the Chinese quarter; but
the Mongols who took part made it a sort of cat-scramble
before the end of the first round. A year or two later
San Francisco talked of the great things that were
expected from one Ah Wing ; but the first genuine white
pug who came into contact with him made him feel like a
Korean under Japanese protection. The Jap. pug hasn’t
appeared as yet, and it’s a matter for congratulation that
he never will be in the heavyweight division.
The latest inquirer after Johnson is Marvin
Hart,- the lengthy Yankee who walloped him on points at
their last meeting, and whom he side-tracked at Phila-
delphia some 18 months ago. Hart seems really anxious,
and is blowing off steam in the most approved Yankee
style bv declaring that he will just die of disappointment
if he can’t “get” Johnson for a bout of anything up to
100 rounds.
* * * *
That the Yankee is no sport is evidenced by the
way he goes about arranging his fights. A scrap in the
Sta'tes is 99 per cent, chin-wagging and coarse personal
abuse, and one per cent, scuffling in a ring. The usual
procedure is for some scrapper to announce that he is
just dying to fight someone else for love, honor, glory,
and anything up to a million dollars. The other
fellow, after listening disdainfully for a while, opens
his head to remark that when the “ ham ” that has
just spoken has proved his right to a fight he can have
it, for anything up to five million dollars. Also he can
have it for anything from one round to 1000 ; likewise he
can have it in a 10ft. ring after throwing the key away.
In addition to which, he will receive such a trouncing
that in the third round he will jump through the ropes.
Back whizzes the reply that the skiter who has talked so
big has failed to put up his splosh ; and that his mother
was a lady who indulged in habits peculiar to Thibetan
women of fortune ; and when the speaker gets to him he
will curse the day he was foaled. In the next stage the
interchanges of compliments becomes unprintable. Then
the fight. The last scene of all is the flourish of the
sporting papers, which have discovered indisputable evi-
dence that the fight was a fake and a pre-arranged affair
from start to finish.
* * * *
Choice sample of Yankee fight talk :
“ Me and me wife can lick a smokehouse full of hams
like Jim Jeffries ! "Stonewall Jackson Butt, colored,
fighter.
“Jim can do just what he thinks about fighting John-
son. I will not try to influence him either way. It’s up
to Jim. If he wants to sail into Johnson and beat the
life out of him, I want him to.”—Mrs. Janies Jeffries, in
a recent interview.
We have seen it in the Jingo dailies, time and
again, that the Yankee is our natural ally, and that in
the Empire’s day of distress the White Fleet will come
to the rescue of the reeling Titan in the North Sea. We
have been exhorted to look up to the Yank as a brother
and a last refuge, because blood is thicker than water ;
and slush will tell. But the Yank is no sport. After re-
ceiving our moral assistance in the war with Spain, and
being helped by the British with coaling facilities to take
the Philippines, and after having assured Bull that nothing
would be done to damage Bull’s trade in the islands, Bull
awoke the other day to find that the door of the Islands
had been slammed in his face. That was bad enough.
But this week a worse outrage comes to light.
All former iniquities are mere pin pricks compared to
this latest indignity put up by the Yankee. Our one
world-championship scrap is being filched from us. We
put up £6OOO for the Yankee Burns and £l5OO for the
other Yankee Johnson. We did them both proud, and
allowed Johnson to pose as a scientist, and to make re-
marks about Reid, Deakin and Bruce Smith. And now
the swindling humbugs are moving to ignore our cham-
pionship scrap by declaring that James Jeffries is still
boss fighter of the world, and that he has been challenged
by Al. Kauffman who will, if he wins, claim the globe’s
championship and draw the color line against Johnson.
He has deposited $lOOO with the challenge, so he means
business. Now, honestly, what use have we for these
Yankees after that? These ruffians who seek to deprive
us of our one little ewe lamb world-championship scrap.
No, the Yank is no sport.
* * * *
New Yorkers are resorting to elaborate dodges
to get round the law prohibiting prize fights ; and the
latest idea is the athletic club device. To become a
member of the club one has to pay an entrance fee and
be balloted for after the most careful inquiries have been
made by the officers of the club. When a scrap is on the
fighters are engaged as “ entertainers,” and paid as such.
There is no referee and no decisions are given, and
spectators decide to their own satisfaction who is winner.
Some of the salaries paid to entertainers are enormous.
To view the fight the member has first to produce his card
of membership, on the back of which is a solemn and
legal agreement not to transfer the pasteboard. Having
gained admission to the building, another fee has to be paid
to retain a seat in the club’s theatre where the “enter-
tainment” is in full blast. So far this dodge has mostly
prevented the police gaining admission, as all their
attempts to effect membership have been blackballed ;
but even where they' did gain admission by some ruse they
have heen powerless to interfere, as they have been un-
able to prove that the scrap was for a prize. Boxing
displays by hired entertainers are legal. But how willing
some of those boxing displays are ! It doesn’t strike this
writer as being a very new or original device.
* • * *
The lack of sportsmanship in the Yankee
character is appalling. For years the America Cup has
been kept in the States by the regulations and not by the
yachts. When a foreigner wrestles with a Yankee on
Yankee soil, the Yankee is allowed to indulge in eye-
gouging, nostril-bursting, and toe-breaking. No foreigner
dreams of fighting a Yankee at home for a championship
—it’s mere foolishness. And for years the horse-jumping
record of the world was kept in the U.S.A. by the simple
expedient of jumping the animals off springboards that
had been turfed over, and omitting to mention the fact
in the records of the jumps. At the Olympian games the
Yankees swindled and bustled hideously, and made them-
selves detested throughout Europe by their unfair tactics.
* * * *
The boxing world is perturbed in its soul on
the question of weights. It is widely felt that there
should be more grades to ensure more equal battles and
avoid the unpleasant spectacle of a little fellow like
Burns combating a towering mass of meat like Johnson.
As far as can be ascertained, the present weights are :
Bantam, up to 1181 b.
Feather-weight ~ 1261 b.
Light-weight 1281 b. to 1381 b.
Welter-weight 1381 b. ~ 1441 b.
Middle-weight 1441 b. ~ 1581 b.
Heavy-weight, anything over 1581 b.
The whole idea of dividing fighters into separate classes
on weights is to ensure equal contests, and it is argued
that there are so many men making their living by this
form of “industry” now that some more subdivisions are
required to ensure fair mills. So the following list is pro-
posed in Britain and Amurka, the real homes of the
game:—
Bantam-weights, up to 1161 b.
Light Feathers ~ 1221 b.
Feather-weights ~ 1261 b.
Light-weights ~ 1331 b.
Welter-weights ~ 1451 b.
Middle-weights ~ 1581 b.
Light Heavy-weight ~ 1751 b.
Heavy-weight, all over 1751 b.
If this classification comes off, Tahmmy will probably re-
enter the ring, and once more become a world-champion
while the days of his youth run swiftly away.
Sydney Grammar School continues to pickup
championships. Recently it gathered in the top honors
at cricket; and last week, by its defeat of three other
colleges, the Great Public Schools rowing championship
and the Major Rennie Trophy also went its way.

| A letter from “ Kay Ell ”:
How is it that the nastiest and most unfair things
said about England and the English are said by the
most bigoted Imperialists ? Only a few months have
passed since Manager Teeee, of the A.M.P., made remarks
about the English people which, had they been made by a
supporter of Australian national sentiments, would have
been execrated in every right-thinking assemblage as
rank disloyalty and “ Dingoism.” Now Colonel Burns
comes along with the bizarre assertion that one out of
every four Englishmen is a degenerate. If Burns’story
were correct, the sooner the Germans or somebody else
came along and annihilated the whole English nation the
better for the world. A country, even if it be an
island, containing ten millions of degenerates is nothing
less than a menace to all humanity, and, from a race
standpoint, it would be worth while slaughtering the
thirty millions of normal beings to stamp out such a
plague-spot. But, fortunately for the world generally,
anybody who has visited England knows that the Burns’
tale is so much flapdoodle; unless he accepts the rigid
definition of a degenerate which includes every man who
wears spectacles or who is bald at 40. Probably 99 per
cent, of the human race is degenerate in the physical
sense, if compared with its ancestors of the cave era—
assuming that our bone-seekers haven’t mistaken the
mammoth and the extra special gorilla for ancestors—
but the ordinary acceptation of the term does not class
the two Burnses—Tommy and the Colonel—as de-
generates. It regards as normal all men but those with a
mental or moral or physical kink, and that a kink of
larsrer dimensions than is indicated bv merely playing
golf or wearing weird whiskers or haviiig slightly handy
legs or impeaching a whole nation in an off-hand way on
insufficient grounds.
“ Bayonet ” writes regretfully :
I wish this universal training idea had been in force
when I started work, some 13 years ago. The first billet
I ever had was in a drapery establishment, where annual
holidays were not fashionable. I worked there for eight
weary years, and during all that time I neyer had more
than four days’ spell at one stretch. Occasionally a wild
longing to get out and away would come over me. I
didn’t want, either to rest or loaf. I would have been
more than content to sleep under canvas and to drill all
day. It would have been a holiday to me—a whole fort-
night or three weeks of sunlight and open air, away from
the idiotic worry and rush of a badly-run establishment,
where rats were always dying under the stairs, and the
boss’s temper depended on how much of the trading
capital he had lost at the races. (He was a rotten judge
of “form” in horseflesh; consequently his temper was
almost invariably the same.) If Australia had had the
boon of compulsory military training in those days, I
would have been forced— forced, mind you—to take every
year a splendid holiday away from the‘absurdities of that
excited establishment. I am sorry for Australia’s sake
that we did not have compulsory training 13 years ago,
for by now we would have had one of the best citizen
armies on earth ; but I am sorrier still for those lost lti
or 24 weeks of healthy camp-life that will never now lie
mine this side of Jordan.

* * *
“P. Richard”: I believe it would pay anyone to
hawk round copper in the shape of penny-pieces
in certain “working-class” suburbs of Melbourne,
where the penny-in-the-slot system for the supply of gas
is a household institution. Owners of “slots” would
readily pay 3s. 3d. in silver for dozen actual
coppers, such is the scarcity. Realise that a collector
averages from 15s. to 20s. at each house, and multiply
this by many dozens of houses,-and some idea can
be gained of the lock-up of coppers. In some
suburbs it is hardly safe for a man to go about if he is
known to have pennies on him. I was lately' in a sub-
urban bank on a Saturday morning, and I must have
struck it at the busiest hour, for the counter
was packed. I thought I had drifted into a penny'
savings bank when I heard the pressing and
repeated demands for “copper without half-pennies.”
I really expected to see the teller hea?e an ink-
stand at one or other of the applicants. The latter I
judged to be shopkeepers, who wanted the coins for their
own smaller fr.v of customers. “ I haven’t got any—l’m
cleared out,” .the teller exclaimed at last, in sheer and
final desperation. So I’m going to give the dodge a
trial, at any rate—that is, the hawking round of, say, £.5
of coppers (pennies, of course) as a first experiment,'ask-
ing one penn.v commission on every dozen. If the game
succeeds, I will order two tons of'coppers from adjacent
States, and take them round in a van.
* * * *
Those German manufacturers of razors worth
about Is. each, but put up in eases marked “ Price
10s. 6d.,” are evidently trying a new trick ; for the Cus-
toms have got hold of a consignment stamped, “Price
lj dollars.” .They were invoiced at the same old price as
tne 10s. fid. frauds, viz., 7s. per dozen (that is, sevenpence
each). Apparently the Commerce Act isn’t designed to
block this caper; though in a country' where commer-
cialism and honesty,were synonymous, the Police Offences
Act would meet the case.
* * * *
“Dandalup”: Westralia has a bundle of in-
dustries waiting to be developed on big lines. First-class
sponges—some of them much superior to the average
imported article—can be got almost anywhere along the
coast. A fine collection was recently presented to
Prime Minister Andy Fisher upon his visit to the West.
Cotton-growing is another industry that would flourish
with a little assistance. I have seen cotton plants, grow-
ing almost wild and blooming profusely, as far south as
Champion Ba.v. Over ,30. years ago a relative of mine
secured a diploma and bronze medal at the Melbourne
Exhibition for cotton grown in this district. A consign-
ment was sent to London, and realised top prices.
Tobacco also flourishes in the Western State’s sub-
tropical regions. Whaling, which some y'ears ago
threatened to be a sizeable business, has died an unnatural
death, although the coast waters, especially north of
Geraldton, teem with large mobs of whales at certain
periods of the,year. Only recently I counted over 40 near
Cape Inscription, during one day’s sail. Further north,
banana, date, cocoanut, rubber, pineapple, castor oil,
rice, tobacco, arrowroot, and half a dozen other sorts of
plantations will thrive under the white man’s care and
management; while some parts will grow ihe finest pota-
toes that ever wore a jacket. North and N. W. Australia
should be able to supply' all Asia with “Murphies” of
the finest quality if it had the population to grow them.
Yet we have in Australia politicians who tell us: (1)
That the white man can’t live in these parts ; (2) that, if
he can live there, he can’twork there ; and (3) that, if he
can live and work there, he can’t find anything to do.
* * * *
“Bon” : I wandered into a second-hand book
store lately', to discover that it takes all brands of books
to build a library, and all manner of men to sell them.
Questioned the proprietor re sundry more or less out-of-
date works, and wound up with a query' as to bis posses-
sion of an unexpurgated copy of Rabelais. The book-fiend
thought hard, then reluctantly confessed his inability
to oblige. Thought harder, and then queried : “ What
was he, anyhow—historian?” “Yes, some —likewise
humorist.” The bookman brightened up immensely,
and, diving into the depths of a huge box, emerged with
triumph writ large all over him. “ Why didn’t you say
so? Now, if you want something comic, this ain’t too
bad : ‘ Eliza’s Husband,’ by Barry Pain.” I advised him
to pet trampled on by a taxi-cab, whereon he called me a
“ bloke.”
THAT DREADNOUGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Slump !
Sudden slump !
Hear the Jingoes rave and jump—
All in vain the gush and blather of the Daily Pharisee :
Vain the howling of the Press—all is dust and emptiness,
And the grief of old Mayor Smiffkins is a sight that
gladdens Me ;
Mister Smiffkins, feeling ill, weepeth sadly in the till—
He whoyester-week was bawling for the Teuton’s distant
blood ; •
Mister Smiffkins isn’t well, so he furls his crimson yell—
Yea, the craze has fizzled, and its name is
simply Mud.
Slump !
Abject slump !
Wade and Co. have got the hump—
They are cursing Andrew Fisher; hear them howl and
wail and roar.
They had pointed out the way with a loud and joyous
bray—
Yea, the path that leadeth Pawnward to St. Cohen’s dis-
t int door !
But alas for Mister Wade and the blither-struck brigade—
Andrew Fisher would not hearken to the shriek for sudden
loans ;
He was obdurate and grim, hence the clods they heave
at him—
Hence the cursing and the raving, hence the bitter sobs
and groans!
Slump !
P'earful slump !
Stick their heads beneath the pump —
Wash away the gas and frenzy and the tendency to yell ;
For the story of the cow wraps the Dreadnought madness
now,
And the maffiik hath dsparte 1 from the leading article !
To the level of the drunk hath the fool proposal sunk—
Yea, no more the words of S.niffiains are proclaimed for
men to see ;
Gone the glory and the gush, ceased the streams of
“ loyal ” slush—
Yea, the sacred name of Smiffkins sinks in sad obscurity !
Slump !
Total slump !
Who the Devil cares a dump?—
Let us build a fighting navy for our own Australian land !
Uncle Bull is far away—lest the Ape this land should
sway,
Let us launch the ships of battle with a strong, deter-
mined hand 1
While the Smiffkins people moan, while the Wades and
Murrays groan,
Let Australia’s dockyards thunder with a large, incessant
roar ;
Ere the Monkey starts to jump—lest Australia’s star
should slump —
Let our forges flame to Heaven building armaments of
war !

IN BANANA LAND.
Arthur Morgan, who was the first Queens-
land Premier who had the courage to attack in
real earnest the disastrous work of the Continuous
Government, is now Lieut.-Governor. He has
been Speaker, Premier, President of the Legisla-
tive Council, Lieutenant-Governor, and now
Deputy Figurehead, all inside six years. Morgan’s
father fought the squattocracy of Queensland with
a pen dipped in gall. And it was a battle in those
bad old days, when the great estates were being
amassed, when the small settler was hunted as if
he was an interloper or a rabbit, and when the
Big Landlord declared that the Darling Downs
couldn’t grow a cabbage. Arthur Morgan inherited
the fighting instinct, though he had seen his
father ruined financially as a newspaper pro-
prietor by the hatred and the malice of the
squatters. With Kidston as Treasurer, he un-
furled the no-borrowing flag (lately taken down),
preached and practised the gospel that Queensland
must pay its way, and passed many-measures which
the Philp crew declared would ruin the State. It
was an unfortunate day for Queensland when
Arthur Morgan was tempted to throw up the
Premiership for a dull, foolish job in the Museum
of Fossils.

ening of their stomach, a stimulating of the liver,
a cleaning out of the toxins that infest the intes-
tines, is all that stands between them and per-

Died in Brisbane on April 15, aged 66,
W. C. Brown, who discovered gold at Croydon,
now nearly defunct, and named the townshipafter
his birthplace -just outside London. When he
made the discovery he was engaged in squatting,
but he ceased to squat on the strength of his find.
Now a whole lot of Croydon people would be glad
to squat if they had anything better than their
debts to squat upon.
The judicial mantle of recently-retired
Judge Noel has fallen upon an unassuming person
in Townsville, by name A. W. Macnaughton. As
he has practised in Townsville since the inception
of the Northern Supreme Court, 14 years ago, it
is evident he can stand the climate. And as he
was a prominent member of the Philp election
committees, it is obvious that he is a deserving
character.
“ M.”: There is little doubt now that the
days of the Kilp-Phidson Government are
numbered. The nominal Premier went North
a fortnight ago and met heavy weather
and a chill blast that would have frost-bitten the
Shackleton party. Now Home Secretary Bell is
playing up. Denham, Redwood and Jenkinson
are rocking, and Maxwell is so out of friends with
everybody that there is little chance of his again
facing the music. The Government is talking
about getting a dissolution if defeated, and for
this purpose has further deferred the meeting of
the House until the Chelmsford family has de-
parted, and Morgan is in the Deputy-GovernoFs
chair. Chelmsford would hardly repeat his
former dissolution blunder, for so long as
Bowman hasn’t had a turn at the Premier-
ship, the possibilities of forming a Ministry out of
the present Assembly aren’t exhausted. That was
the point which Chelmsford obligingly overlooked
last year, but there is a limit to the overlooking
of things. The chances are that Morgan may
notice the point too, and that the postponement
of the meeting of Parliament may pay no dividend
after all.
AN ATTITUDE OF DISDAIN.
FIRST SUPERIOR ONE: “I say, dear boy, where do you get your hat ironed,?”
SECOND SUPERIOR ONE (with, contempt): “I don’t—I buy a new one.”
Bananaland has abolished the plan of
hiring private agents to sell its public lands. A
couple of them got a good deal of fluff into their
nests at the game. For a time the groups which
were yarded up from the southern States helped
to boom settlement, but anomalies (to put it
mildly) crept in and led to a flood of complaints ;
so for the future the Department will conduct the
business by its own officers.
Portmaster John Mackay, one of Bananaland’s
celebrities, gets an extension of official life, though
he has passed both the allotted span and the Civil
Service retiring age. The explanation is that it
isn’t worth while appointing a new State official,
as the Australian Government will presently take
hold of coastal administration ; but, as a matter of
fact, Bananaland doesn’tknow where it could get
a young man who looks any more durable than the
ancient warrior. He comes from the Mackays of
Strathnaven, who dragged their kilts through
centuries of-chronic Scotch bloodshed in the days
when Scotland was a blanky land, with red hair
bn its head, and a sword the size of a precipice in
its hand, and a bull’s-hide shield on its left arm,
and a howl in its mouth, and porridge and stolen
beef and illicit whisky in its stomach. It was to
a Mackay that an English soldier cried out
“Quarter 1” in a certain battle, and got a memor-
able reply. “I’ve nae time to quarter ye,” said
Mackay, “but I’ll just cut ye in twa.” And
having done that he fled into the thickest of
the battle again, and proceeded to shake things
up a good deal. The sea-adventures of old John
Mackay, of Queensland, would fill a volume, and
his proceedings on dry land during the early
stormy times of Bananaland were highly interest-
ing. He travelled overland at the head of a party
in 1860, and discovered the Pioneer River where
Mackay now stands. Twenty years later he re-
turned there and found a white population of
20,000 people, and much objectionable, smellful,
rum-drunken Tommy Tanna. Now he seems
likely to live to see the last of the Tommy Tanna
desecration.
The Day the Mailman Comes.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHEN Mabel puts her hair in trim.
And Sis her brooches wears ;
When Emma, in the firelight dim,
The floury scone prepares ;
And at the sliprails brother Jim
Across the gloaming stares —
These signs and portents knowledge bring
To all the Bush—and you,
That Expectation, bright of wing,
Young Fancy flutters through—
For—as the kettle seems to sing—
“ To-night the mailman’s due.”
In slop-made suit of dusty brown,
And greasy wide-brimmed hat,
He comes, a welcome guest from town,
Each week to Reedy Elat,
And brings the latest cables down—
A fortnight old at that.
A cheerful, slow, bucolic wight,
Bowlegged and saddle-bred,
With lank, oiled hair an auburn bright,
And nose a blistered red,
He smokes and gossips thro’ the night
Till long past “time for bed.
The cables and the market news
The Old Man in his chair
Absorbs, and ventilates his views
On irresponsive air—
They have no precious time to lose
On Balkan troubles there ;
When Sandy Scott, his local store
Of current scandal, chat,
In-gathered eighty bush miles o’er,
Unloads to glad the Flat,,
And fills his briar pipe once more,
And spits across the cat.
For Milly Jones will married be
In either March or May ;
Old Doctor Dunn’s been on the spree-,
Six months—so people say —
And Wilson’s dance was goin’ free
’Till after break o’ day !
His tale of marriage, death and birth,
The district happ’nings small;
Those things of tragedy or mirth
That tears or laughter call,
The human things from o’er the Earth,
Long Sandy stocks them all.
The pen-scrawled words of love and trade ;
The missives honey-sweet,
In seal across his saddle laid,
He bears with air discreet;
Why should the pathway not be made
More pleasant to his feet ?
Why should he not the best beds get;
His plate the tit-bits hold ?
And by his knife a serviette
Be placed in careful fold.
With something special “for the wet,”
Or else to “ cure his cold ” ?
A golden link he makes, between
The world and Reedy Flat.
In dusty suit, and necktie green.
And greasy, soft, felt hat—
But Emma, turning seventeen.
Could tell you more of that.
No wonder Mabel curls her hair,
And Sis a ditty hums,
And Bill, with neither ear nor air,
The old piano strums —
Romance is in the clear bush air
The day the mailman comes.
E. J. B.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, April 24, 1909.
Mr Dear Thargomindah,—
I told you so !
Our pale Governor has been having: the time of
his life in the Papuan wilds. I rather expected
that he would get up to some mischief, but his
frivolity has passed my wildest expectations. He
has been hobnobbing with pearl-shellers and
making friends with monkey men. who gave
wrestling displays, and I gravely suspect that he
joined in the corroboree that followed. Anyway,
he danced at a fancy ball (possibly in the State
costume of a Papuan diplomatist) given by the
juvenile whites of the island. Lastly, he got loose
on a pearling lugger, and had to be forcibly
restrained when Captain Verney donned a diving
suit and went down six fathoms deep with his
leaded boots foremost. Now our slender Ex. is
on his way home with a wild pig in each pocket,
which animals, if you don’t keep a close eye upon
him, he’ll be letting loose in the Domain one of
these nights.
The wily chauffeur! What havoc he wreaks
upon the heart of the susceptible 20th century
damsel ! Take it from me, ye mothers who aim
at good matches for your chicks, he is a snake in
the grass and a serpent amid the vegetables.
Long ago Jeames, on the box, used occasionally
to upset the family marriage cart, yet a coachman
was a coachman, and his status was recognised.
He was just plain dog, and to love him entailed a
loss of caste. But a nasty indefiniteness sur-
rounds the chauffeur. There’s no real satisfac-
tion about him, and no known description fits
him. He’s neither dog nor sausage, so to speak,
and his smart suit, dust coat, cap, and even his
goggles, lend him an air of “ prince in disguise ”
that is unsettling to the young person.
All this by way of prologue to a pretty game of
hearts that “ tuff tuffs ” past my window daily in
a big family car. And won’t the fat be in the fire
one of these days when ma discovers the dog-
cum-sausage person’s appropriation of her little
bit of muslin.
Ivy Craig, a Junoesque damsel of this city, the
possessor of a voice, and gifted with the tastes of a
mermaid, is to wed a nephew of your late State
Governor, one Appleyard. Where she discovered
her new property I don’t know, but a sight of her
swimming, or a sound of her singing, would bowl
out any average man for «. duck.
The youngest Newton chick, who was suspected
of intent to marry a military man, now announces
her purpose of allying herself with the other, and
wetter, branch of the service, and will pack her
trunks by the 22nd May, and depart to Ports-
mouth (England). There she is to contract for
life-long obedience with Lieutenant Cocky, once
a warm favorite on the Australian naval station.
Sister Violet, who wedded Paymaster Woodman,
will act as referee at the event.
It didn’t rain last Wednesday—it was rather
that the bottom fell out of the heavenly tank and
all the contents dropped at once. It threatened
ruin to the heavy ciop of weddings that came due
on that date. The big pastoral De Burgh Persse-
Co’lins alliance, which was to have taken place
in Beaudesert, had to be done in the house,
because the bride couldn’t swim well enough.
The athletic young bridegroom and his merry men
arrived clothed in jack-hoots and oilskins and
slouch hats. At the homestead of Tamrookum a
sudden change took place, and in 10 minutes an
i mmaculately-frocked groom and best and next-best
men trooped into the pretty drawing-room, where,
amid a bower of chrysanthemums and under crim-
son shaded lamps (the day was as black as the
heart of a defeated political candidate), the deed
was done. Later on, De Burgh packed his bride
into a covered phaeton, and they departed amid a
cloud of mud for a neighboring homestead, cn
route for the South.
Another wedding of note was that of Miss Nina
Vidgen and Mr. C. Crouch, two popular young
people of this city, who departed for the seaside
in a flood of water, rose leaves and good wishes.
As I write comes the sad tidings of the serious
illness of Mrs. (Did) Harris in Sydney, which
caused husband and daughter Leslie to depart to
your harbor side on Thursday. Mrs. E..T.H. was
a Levien, and met her handsome husband in
Melbourne.
On Saturday last Kangaroo Point was electrified
by a real military wedding, when fat Captain
Carter, of South African note, married Violet
Smith with due clash of military steel and
martial music. A certain local daily bursts into
French and declares that a chcveaux de prise was
formed by the officers swords for the bride and
groom to pass under.
Yours affectionately,
MARIO.
The latest ex-cohentingenter to distinguish
himself is (or was) Hamilton Dickson, of Dulaeca
(Queensland). There was a casual public-house
quarrel between Dickson and Pring, but the
matter apparently blew over, and they shook
hands. Yet, though Pring blew over, it seems
that Dickson didn’t. Pring disappeared off the
face of the earth. After many days people began
to remark his failure to turn up to meals, and an
earnest search revealed his horse (shot dead), and
the remains of a fire with some human relics and
Pring’s gun-metal watch among the ashes. About
this stage of the proceedings ex-contingenter
Hamilton Dickson cut things short by putting a
bullet through his military head, and there will be
no sensational trial after all. The result of all this
is that quite a gloom has fallen over patriotic
Dulacca, and the local right-thinking person is
deploring things so hard that his crop is being
worried by crows, and the moth is breaking into
the local Anti-Sosh League and eating its
prineiples-
The recently-completed Geraldton-Harvey
Creek telegraph line seems to be about the
toughest bit of wire-erecting done in Australia for
some time. Although the line is only 26 miles
long it took 11 months to put up. One of the
causes of this was the density and heaviness of
the scrub thereabouts. In one place a fig-tree
measuring 30ft. across had to be cut through.
Wet weather also gave considerable trouble. At
times the men on the job were working up to
their knees in water. Once their camp was
flooded out, and another time they were isolated
by flood waters on the bank of the Russell River,
and lived for a week on sweet potatoes. Yet they
stuck to the job for 11 months in a place where it
is alleged the white man can’t work.
One of the best razors that reaches Australia
from the home of the finest razor-making manufactories
in the world is the “Plum” at 10/6, for which Messrs.
Craig and Aitken, of 654 George Street, Sydney are sole
agents. The “ Plum ” blade is made of unequalled steel,
specially hollow ground, and finally trued to a particu-
larly keen edge, and is justly famed for satisfactory
results. Its growing popularity speaks for itself, and the
price, though not the price of the common stock razor, is
low considering its superfine quality. For 10/6 Messrs.
Craig and Aitken send the “ Plum ” to any address, post
free, and guarantee to keep the razor in shaving order for
two years free of charge.*
The walloping of offenders with a cane is
one of the duties of the Binghi king at the Yar-
rabah (N.Q.) Aboriginal Mission. The other day,
he had to flog four of his subjects for pilfering,
and did it cheerfully. As he can’t whack himself,
he has a special punishment when he goes on the
crooked path—the white superintendent of the
mission deposes him for a while. Recently, he
was relieved of his crown and orb and sceptre and
brass plate and cane for a whole week.

THE SPOILS OF THE WHEATFIELDS.
“No one can estimate the capacity of an acre of
land. What can be yot out of it depends upon
what is put into it. ” — Mechi.
“He gave it as his opinion, that whoever could
make two cars of corn to grow where only one
yrev; before, would deserve better of mankind,
and do more essential service to his country
than the whole race of politicians put toge-
ther.”—Dean Sivift in “ Gulliver.”
“ Age after age has seen the solver go forth to sow,
and the reaper come home with his sheaves.”
There are millions of acres in Western Queensland
awaiting the plough-great rolling plains,
practically treeless, where, beneath the settler’s
feet, run inexhaustible springs in the bowels of
the earth, the waters of which are being brought
to the surface day by day by the Government
diamond drills.
The golden crops of Queensland may yet prove as
great a source of wealth as either the golden reef
or the golden fleece.
The annual wheat statistics of the United States
(1906) were : —

Area under crop ... 47,305,829 acres.
Value £122,583,190.
Comparing size of territory Queensland’s product
should be: —
184,000,000 bushels annually on 19,000,000 acres,
value £30,643,000.
