No. XVII.
THE GOLDEN CROPS OF WHEAT.
By J. D. FITZ GERALD.
The farmer of Queensland, aided by that expert
assistance which the Government utilities afford,
is coming to a knowledge of the best use to which
his land can be put; so that, by the expenditure
of the minimum amount of labor and capital, he
may get the very highest results. Thus it does not
always follow that the land will be used for the
purpose it was first put to. It has been shown
that maize growing, in certain districts, was
superseded by dairying, as the distant markets of
the world became accessible by the growth of
shipping facilities. Cotton growing in other
districts gave place to more profitable crops
where the local conditions conduced to larger
profits as a result of a change of the product.
There are immense tracts in Queensland which
are eminently suitable for wheat farming. The
HARVESTING OPERATIONS.—TYPICAL QUEENSLAND FARMING LANDSCAPE, GALTON.
Darling Downs and the Western areas have great
spaces suitable for cereal crops. The march of
development so far has gone on other lines than
the growing of crops for market. The natural
pasture grasses are so luxuriant that it may be
said that, for pastoral purposes-—for cattle,.horses
and sheep—Nature has provided the crops with a
prodigal hand. The Canadian, British and Con-
tinental farmer will think it incredible that-Nature
furnishes all the fodder necessary for fattening
and sustenance purposes on the great territories
of Queensland, when he has to grow his crops to
provide winter forage for his beasts. We can
realise the nature of these immense Queensland
natural pastures from a short description by Mr.
Randolph Bedford (the well-known Australian
novelist)
HARVESTING WHEAT WITH REAPER AND BINDER, NEAR WARWICK.
“There are,” he says (writing to the World’s
Work on the wonderful results of Governmental
aid in saving £2,000,000 worth of wool and meat
by bringing the sheep from droughty areas on to
better country during one bad season), “ there are
200 miles of Flinders grass growing between the
tussocks of the Mitchell—dry—a self-made hay—
and the finest of stock-fatteners. The plains have
adapted their grasses at the cost of many centuries
of experiment; the comparatively rainless condi-
tion of the country is perfection for the Mitchell
grass; rain at the time of its self-conversion to
hay would blacken and rot it. Reaped and stacked
and roofed, it will keep as hay for five years; yet
few men trouble to make the plenty of the good
years the stand-by of the lean. That this is the
right course to take is proved by the fact that two
men at the 30-mile coach change cut one hundred
tons and sold it for £5 a ton in the last drought;
few, however, have followed that excellent ex-
ample in this season of spendthrift plenty.”
This is by the way. The amount of territory
ON THE ROAD TO MARKET, GATTON, SOUTH QUEENSLAND.
under all crops in Queensland in 1907 was 642,979
acres, or about the seven-hundredth part of the
territory of the State. In 1906-7 the main crops
were wheat, maize, hay, green forage (lucerne,
etc.), and sugar-cane. Qf these wheat covered
114,575 acres, and produced 1,108,902 bushels,
valued at about £180,196. The time for our great
“ Epic of the Wheat ”is not yet. Wheat growing
may be regarded as in its mere infancy.
Its prospects are illimitable —for this reason.
Queensland is the nearest of the Australian States
which are in any way developed, to the teeming
millions of Eastern Asia. It has been a matter
much noted by those who have travelled in China
of recent years that the Chinaman is beginning to
regard flour with more favor than rice. All
classes above the mere coolie are beginning to look
upon flour as a necessity, rather than a luxury.
The Americans developed the market from their
distributing centres in Hongkong and Shanghai;
but when their goods were boycotted by the
Chinese in 1905, Australian flour came into use,
and it should have an immense future before it m
these regions, where hosts of men exist pn a coun-
try given over to rice and cotton culture,
So far Queensland must look to the future for
the time when she will have her share m trie ex -
ploitation of the markets of Eastern Asia. lor
the present, she has not produced enough flour to
supply her own markets. Thus, in the year IJO7
she imported 253,550 tons of flour from other parts
of the Commonwealth. Queensland is in the posi-
tion now which N.S.W. occupied in the ’SO’s and
’9o’s, in which period the Mother State devoted
herself to the crudest of the primary industries,
Thus, in 1890-1 the wheat crop of N.S.W. was
under four million bushels ; while in 1900-1 it was
over 16 million bushels, and this was accompanied
by a wonderful development of dairying and
mixed farming. Queensland mis magnificent
areas in the Western Downs district which could,
in time, furnish the wheatfields, not only for the
home requirements of the State, but for export to
the ends of the earth. Queensland may yet be a
factor in stopping the representatives of preda-
tory capital from exploiting the people sfood, and
may intervene at the right moment and make it
impossible for men like Patten, exploiter of the
Chicago wheat pit, to run up prices of the staff of
life in the homes of the working bees of other
nations thousands of miles from the scene of
his dramatic attempts to control the world’s
wheat supply. It must be remembered that
Australian wheat brings the highest price in
the world’s markets. In 1906 the prices realised
for Australian wheat topped those of the best
wheat-growing countries in the world. A few
comparisons of prices are interesting :
Average Prices of Wheat in 1906 per Imperial Quarter
at Mark Lane, London.
Argentina .. .. .. .. .. 29/10
Germany 27/7
Russia .. .. .. ■ ■ ■ • 79/10
U.S.A 30/11
Canada .. .. .. • • • • 30/8
British India 29/4
Australia 31/2
In the year treated (1906) Maoriland wheat
brought the top price, 325. 2d. per imperial
quarter; but the year before Australian wheat
brought 325. 4d., while M.L. brought only 30s. Id.
This is worth noting. The Commonwealth
Statistician accounts for our pre-eminence in this
way“ Generally speaking,” he says, “Aus-
tralian wheat shows a grain of bright, clear tex-
ture, rich in gluten, and of fine milling quality.
Its excellence is attested by the high price which
it realises in the English markets.”
The average yield of wheat in Queensland is
relatively high. In 1907 the average of that State
exceeded that of either Victoria or New South
Wales. Taking it over a period of years,
Queensland’s average is 13'8 bushels to the acre,
which is the highest yield of the Commonwealth
States but one, that of Tasmania. Being
at the crude beginnings of the industry, it cannot
be said that, so far, Queensland farmers have
completely adapted themselves to their environ-
ment. They are experimenting continually, and
seeking perfection. And, meanwhile, all the
great improvements in knowledge, in inventions,
in chemical analysis, in appliances, which the
modern world has at its service, are equally at the
service of the Queenslander. Thus the disastrous
consequences of rust in wheat have been overcome
by the aid of cross-fertilisation of suitable wheats,
and this has practically stamped out rust. And
there is the home market open to the farmer,
which is more immediately advantageous to him
than the outer markets of the world.
The active Department of Agriculture does
everything in its power to develop the efficiency
of the wheat industry and the productivity of the
soils and the varieties of wheat. Last year it
THE CHEMISTS AT WORK ON AN EXPERIMENTAL WHEAT FARM.
approached 100 wheat farmers, in different locali-
ties of Queensland, and supplied them with a
bushel of wheat each, in order that tests might be
made as to the suitability of particular brands of
wheat to the soils of the various localities. These
“stud” wheats have been thoroughly tested by
the different recipients, who have reported to the
Department, and lacer on the knowledge acquired
will be turned to account. Rust-resistant wheats
are being evolved. Last year experiments in the
milling qualities of different sorts of wheat were
carried out by the State Agricultural Chemist.
That wheat growing is susceptible of improve-
ment by scientific treatment is proved in a hun-
dred cases. The experiments in wheat growing
at Rothamsted, near London, which were initiated
by Sir John Bennet Lawes, Bart, (the dis-
coverer of superphosphate manures), are now
known to scientists throughout the world. And
all that science can do in the known world to in-
crease the productiveness of the wheats, either by
discoveries in manuring, analysis of soil, milling
tests, or selection of special wheats, will be at the
service of the Queensland farmer, who will be kept
informed by the State agencies.
The English experiments by Mr. Gorton in the
cross-fertilisation of wheat are well known. But,
after all, the common-sense of growing from the
largest and best ears has been known to every
Australian farmer for years past. Mr. Gorton’s
cross-fertilising has strengthened the selected
stocks, until it maybe said that he has created
new plants. In fact, it is claimed for him that he
has “ brought new plants into being.” He has
cross-fertilised his plants by using fertilising
agents obtained from weeds and vegetable pests,
and has harnessed the “enormous vigor, mar-
vellous reproductive power, and adaptability to
their environment” which the weeds possess to
the service of the new wheats.
In the rapid roll of Queensland development,
the forest land of to-day may become the wheat-
field of to-morrow. But, as the late Mr. Traill
pointed out, and he had travelled extensively,
there is little or no difference in appearance
between the Western Queensland sparsely tim-
bered or treeless country and the “ prairie” land
of the United States and Canada, about which we
hear so.much. It is on these great plains that you
find the “bonanza” farms of which we read,
ploughed by great navies of ploughs and stripped
by moving landscapes of stripping machines. In
Queensland the huge bonanza farm is unknown,
and the distribution of the land for farming pur-
poses is more equitable than in the United States.
Queensland is the Paradise of the small farmer.
Those almost feudal conditions of settlement
and tenure which we discover in a perusal of such
books as the late Frank Norris’s “ The Octopus ”
and “The Pit,” as existing in the United States,
are absent from the conditions of Queensland
farming. A few words from the State Statistician
will show how small the areas of the farms were ;
and these will act as an encouragement to the
emigrant who is looking towards the Common-
wealth. Says Mr. Thornhill Weedon, Queensland
State Statistician: —
“The greater proportion of farmers cultivated
land in blocks between five and 50 acres in extent.
There were last year 6090 persons who farmed
68,143 acres, in areas of five acres and less than 20
acres, giving an average of 11‘2 acres to each. And
there were 5257 persons cultivating 164,422 acres, on
farms between 20 and 50 acres, or an average of
31 3 acres each, making altogether 11,347 out of
17,271 farms returned. Of small holdings of less
than five acres each there were 2454, aggregating
6327 acres. Farms with a cultivation area exceed-
ing 50 acres numbered 3470, with a combined area
of 404,087 acres, or 63 per cent, of the total culti-
vated area. This gave an average of 116'5 acres
to each holding.”
The reader was told in a previous article how it
is possible for a man to make a splendid living on
a few acres by growing pineapples. In Queens-
land it is impossible for the railway combines and
money kings to “commandeer” the wheat pro-
duct, as is done in the United States, if one is to
believe the picture drawn in Norris’s fine works of
fiction. In our Queensland “epic of the wheat,’
which will be written in the days to come, there
will be no story of predatory bankers, crafty rail-
way men, soulless mortgagees, or Stock Excnange
gamblers. The State of Queensland owns the
system of land transportation, and supervises the
traffic as far as the ends of the world.
Nor has the prospective farmer need to fear the
terrible consequences of such pests as those de-
scribed in Newman’s vivid picture of third century
life, “ Callista.” Chapter xv., of the “Loss and
Gain ” novel written by the greatest modern master
of English style, shows one Of the plagues which
render Africa, with its vast territories, an unde-
sirable place for the emigrant, even at the present
day. So horrid was the locust, which, in its
swarms, ate everything before it in its North
African invasions, that one imaginative writer,
quoted by Newman, described it as having
“ the head of a horse, the eyes of an elephant, the
neck of a bull, the horns of a stag-, the breast of
a lion, the belly of a scorpion, the wings of an
eagle, the legs of a camel, the feet of an ostrich,
and the tail of a serpent.” We have nothing in
the way of pests to parallel this scourge. lhe
“ Granary of Rome,” as Africa was called, was
eaten out in a week by these locust pests, and
reduced to famine.
Wheat cultivation is only in its infancy m
Queensland, but when we mark its progress in
the neighboring State of New South Wales with-
in the last decade, and remember how much
richer the soil, and in how many respects more
favorable the climate of Queensland is to all
others, we can safely predict that this is one of
the natural industries of the country which is sure
to grow by leaps and bounds. The golden crops
may yet prove as great a source of wealth, per-
haps, as either the golden reef or the golden
fleece.

“DAD’S AND DAN.”
The New Waybabk Book you are bound to read, Is.
The Inky Way.
A family of inkslingers are the Pratts, of Australia and
elsewhere. The record of the tribe was recalled by the
recent marriage in Melbourne of Arthur 0. Pratt, one of
the younger members. He began as a freelance in
Westralia, joined the Perth Morning Herald, passed to
the 8. M. Herald, and changed to Hobart Mercury. He
has two brothers, Fred and Byd, on the Herald, while his
father, for many years a prominent Sydney pressman, is
now on Melbourne Age. But this does not end the tally,
as two of the elder Pratt family push the fountain-pen in
London, and their father had the same failing in the
English midlands. It is not stated whether the infants
of the clan have started to cover themselves with blots in
their early attempts at literature, but the chances are
that they' have.
» * * *
“D.”: The People, the Tory London Sunday paper
which has lately been cast in ±lOOO damages for alleging
that Lloyd George, the British Chancellor of the Ex-
chequer, had paid away good legal tender-to avoid being-
sued in a divorce suit, is the only journal I know which
was once edited by a person of royal descent. The late
Colonel “ Gussie ” Fitzgeorge, a son of the old Duke of
Cambridge, ran the sheet for a while about 14 years ago.
It was misconducted somewhat on the lines of the
Saturday editions of our evening papers—seven-
eighths being stolen from the current London
dailies, and the other fraction containing the paper’s
rabidly Conservative policy, expressed in an indifferent
brand of grammar. None who saw the Colonel (with his
brother “ Dollie ”) padding in intensely deliberate fashion
along Bond-street years ago would have taken the mili-
tary potentate for a journalist. He was a tall, vacuous-
looking person, with a beautiful figure, and unimpeach-
able clothes. His prevailing characteristic was utter
languor and indifference to the sordid details of life (which
are the things your day-by-day writer must be absorbed
in, or file his schedule). But perhaps the Colonel wasn’t a
journalist. Brooding on the awful sheet for which he
was responsible, I am fain to think he was not.
♦ fc *
“J.W.” rambles about a printery : Edgerton and
Moore’s, the oldest business in Melbourne’s “inky way,”
went under the hammer lately'. Fifty-seven years
ago a pair by' the name of Trembath and Goodhugh
started a printing office in Flinders-lane. In those days,
a timber yard stood on the present site of Sargood’s ; just
round the corner, the Age had its modest publishing
office, whilst not far away, in a lane near the G.P.0.,
the Evening Herald crept furtively forth as soon as
it was dark. Trembath caught the gold fever, left his
presses, and made for the fields. His place was taken by
a party named Hough. Hi 1854 a strike occurred at the
Argus office. Goodhugh and Hough came briskly for-
ward and set up the embarrassed daily' in their establish-
ment. Later their premises were burnt down. Of course
the strikers Were blamed for the fire, but their
guilt was never proved. When the premises were
rebuilt, William Moore, an old Herald man, took over
the business. From then onwards, many' of Melbourne’s
best-known printers were connected with the firm.
James Fergusson (Fergusson and Mitchell), William Det-
mold, J. M. Tait (about the most ancient printer in
Melbourne), Stewart, Bird, McCarron, Brown, Prior, and
Watt all drew money from it. Other prominent citizens
who worked in this office were George Fisher, of
Maoriland, who was at one time an apprentice,
and the late Janies Munro, knight, who was a
pressman. In its palmiest publishing days the
office produced many important literary works,
and it was a common thing when doing business there to
bump into Henry Parkes, James Bonwick, F. L. Smvthe,
James Blair, Francis Ormond, “ Garryowen,” and other
litterateurs of the time. Almost every weekly' and
monthly in Melbourne has at one time or another been
issued from this ancient printery, from the Baptist to the
K. O. Advocate and Tribune ; from the sober S. S.
Teacher to the giddy Table Talk. Not only the Argus,
but the short-lived People’s Daily was at one time
helped out.
•* * * *
A Melbourne daily pressman tells this story of a
political pilgrimage. The party had come to the con-
stituency of an eloquent and pertinacious member, and
there was a sort of social shivooon. The member was chair-
man, and he called upon this person and that to enter-
tain the company'. It was noted that nobody refused,
although in many cases it would have been hospitable to
have done so. At length the eye of the member lit upon
the pressman. There was a gleam of exultation in that
eye. He called upon the scribe to sing. The scribe was
eagerly declining, when a sort of Bill-Jim resident edged
in behind and nudged him. “ For the love of Heaven,
sing!’’said Bill-Jim. “But I am a rotten singer,” ex-
claimed the pressman. “I dare say,” replied Bill-Jim,
“ but if you don’t sing he will, and no man on gorsearth
can sing as bad as him. Whenever any body refuses he
sez he must reluctantly fill the vacant place in the pro-
gramme himself.” Then the scribe got up and sang his
very' worst.
AUSTRALIAN SONG; A WHIT-HEN-AUST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Shall we Whit Whitily ?
Pieaseth me rather to Hen.
Jingle of rime hath its place,
Measure of rhythm its charm,
But the measureless rhythm caught from the warm deep
bosom of things,
This is more—far more.
Haste of the steed fast driven with its clatter of hoofs,
Wash of the long waves curling and blanching in foam,
Golden effulgence of fading even,
Croak of the night-frog,
Hum of the droning bee,
Twitter of birds in the dew-light,
And the old, grey wonder of things
Shot with the old, old glamor of wizard gold—
Who knoweth these things may Hen.
He who would Whit
Must have his nose attuned,
Hollowed behind for all Yaukeeness,
His must be choice of all words
Harshest of utterance,
Skill to destroy
For the sake of a thought or of no thought
The music of things—
This is to Whit with a Whitman,
Pieaseth me rather to Hen with a Henley.
Yet, of a truth,
Were it not better to Aust with Australia,
Taking whatever of best all the gods may nave given
Before to all bards that have chanted,
Even from David, who sang to Lite wife of Uriah
Old Hebrew thought-rimes love-laden.
To Kipling, with phrases of jingo gold
Set stiffly in wooden-legged verses ;
From Omar to wonderful Austin,
The Alfred who singeth to Ned.
All that had strength to endure
Down through the years,
All that would glow transplanted
In a new clean air and soil,
All that glowing would mix with the new clean things,
This is Australia,
Yea, in spite of.the evil that cryeth,
And the liars that lie all day long,
This is the real Australia.
So let us take of all —
Let us Dave it with David,
Home it with Homer,
Shake it with Shakespeare,
Shell it with Shelley,
Goet it with Goethe,
Let us steal from them all with a skill that shall not
betray us,
Let us take of them all and enweave it with gum leaves
and sunshine—
This is to Aust.
Bosh ter the breath of the morning
Breathed through the bu-h,
Drink in its blanky glory,
Open your eyes and your heart,
Collar a bullock-yoke handy,
String it with greenhide, make music, and sing like the
Devil—
Aust with Australia 1
Aust!

MAGNESIA
Some remarks by a London weekly on
Britain’s new “Territorial” Army are worth the
consideration of young Australia :
The electors are taught to think, and they seriously do
believe, that the time which an occupied citizen can
spare from his business is sufficient to enable him to learn
the soldier’s craft. If the craft to be learned were watch-
making', engine-driving or navigation, everybody would
see the absurdity of such a notion. Continuous and de-
termined application for many months is necessary to
impart even the rudiments of soldiering to the most de-
voted and patriotic citizen. Seaside camps for a week or
a fortnight are not soldiering. Even the Druids under-
stood the seriousness of war better than our rulers. The
Druidical mobilisation system provided that the last man
rolling up to muster after being called to arms was
burnt It is not enough to glow with patriotism, or
to commend patriotism. We must do the things essential
to salvation. And now a word to the girls and matrons.
My dear ladies, you are the people who make or mar the
men. If skulking is fashionable, and “ rotting” national
service not disgraceful, you are responsible. Matron and
maid ! Keep your favors for those who accept the burden
of manhood. One girl may shame a dozen wasters into
national service.

you. You are trying one medicine after the other with the same disgusting disappoint-

Why don’t you stop this medicine habit ? Why don’t you avail yourself of
the modern remedy ? Proceed on Nature’s own lines. Use the life-giving, healing,
strengthening forces of Electricity ! This is the electric age. You can see on every
hand the wonders of Electricity. You don’t need doctors; you don’t need medicine,
for medicine gives no nourishment. You DO need Electric Life Force. Use it as we

Nearly every ailment which afflicts humanity, outside of contagious diseases, is due to the breaking-

“Ak.”: A friend who was at the Show
lunch—Nyhesre His Ex. castigated our Anti-Sosh
politicians for abusing his Ministers to his face and
behind their backs—assured me that the most
diverting part of the business was a' study of the
countenances of the majority of the lunchers
before, during and after the painful affair. They
were all obviously panting to please the Governor
at any cost. Their loyalty was something stu-
pendous. At each mention of the Flag they
cheered wildly ; when the worn old Dreadnought
subject was banged on the table they yelled with
joy; and when words of rebuke and contempt were
spilled on the man Fisher they went fairly fanti.
And always they watched Himself, with dog-lik'e
attention, and strove to pierce the imperturbable
mask which is upon his face, and find what was
at the back of that sleek, well-coiffed head. They
weren’t left in doubt for long when he got on his
legs. And then it was a glorious sight to observe
swift change of attitude. Stout worthies,
who had applauded Mr. Wade’s angry references
to the way the Australian Government proposed
™ ) ™ rl S the States, frowned darkly when Lord
Dudley hinted it was a wrong thing on Mr.
Wade’s part to orate on such controversial sub-
jects in his presence. “Wade’s a fool,” they
muttered cholerically. “ A tactless blunderer.
Ought to be dashed well ashamed of himself drag-
ging politics into a function of this sort.” And then
Mr. Cook was condemned as a vulgar stirrer up of
strife, and hopes were expressed that the Lord
Mayor vvouldn’t get his knighthood after all for
his criminal stupidity in projecting himself and
his Dreadnought business into an atmosphere that
should surround nothing but cows and sheep and
mangel wurzels. The elderly gentleman who
cheered frantically on hearing Mr. Fisher assailed
as a confirmed State-betrayer, and then cheered
still more frantically on hearing the assailer of
Mr. Fisher put severely in his place, was a de-
plorable but quite common feature of the
gathering.

WONDERFUL WONDAI LAND
WHEN" you buy a farm, what do you look for 1 We take it that you
want well-watered land, rich and fertile, producing abundant crops
of maize, potatoes, lucerne and fattening grasses ; that
you wish to be near a railway, that you require long terms and land at low
prices without survey or residential conditions, with a good rainfall and well
watered. You will, therefore, be exactly suited by acquiring a farm on
“Mondure” Estate, Wondai —Ringaroy District, Queensland.
We are now subdividing this fine property of 21,000 acres into areas to suit
purchasers. The average rainfall is 31 inches. A permanent creek runs
right through the property, and water may be had at shallow depths in any part.
This Estate has for many years enjoyed a great reputation as
first-class fattening, agricultural and dairying country. Lucerne,
maize, paspalum, potatoes, and rich fattening grasses are produced there in
abundance.
“MONDURE” is only 4 miles from Murgon Railway Station,
6 miles from Wondai Railway, and 19 miles from Ivingaroy., Terms are
one-fifth cash, balance up to 10 years at 5 per cent. Write us for
particulars promptly.

By this paper’s Stipendiary Chow
News reminiscent of the Japanese occupation of North-
erh Australia, as described in the Lome Hand story, “The
Commonwealth Crisis,” has just drifted this way. Some
14!)mjles north-east of Hongkong- lies the PrataS Reef—
an island belonging to China. Awhile back, some Chinese
fishermen engaged about the place called-loudly to,the
Viceroy of Canton for help, alleging that a party of
Japanese had driven them off, burnt their junks, de-
stroyed a temple, and hoisted the Ape flag over the ruins.
Official representations to Japan brought the reply that
the report was only a fish-yarn—there wasn’t a Jap with-
in miles of Nevertheless the Chinese Govern-
ment sent along a couple of gunboats under the command
of Captain Woo (the same officer whose capture of the
cun-running Japanese steamer, Tatsu Maru, led up to the
big boycott). Woo found the Jap flag on the island all
right; in addition he found depots, houses, piers, trollies
running on rails, tortoise-breeding farms, water-tanks,
shops, etc. ; also a couple of hundred Japanese removing
guano as fast as they could dig'it out. The Japs talked
big at Woo for awhile, but Woo pointed gracefully to
his two gunboats, and suggested the lowering of the
Japanese flag. The calico was lowered, and more palaver
happened. Jap remarked courteously that he owned the
isiand, and in proof pointed to a post inscribed “Dis-
covered on the eighth moon of the 40th year of Meiji,”
i e., about 18 months ago. Woo, with equal courtesy,
asked where was the Chinese temple that stood on the
island much more than 18 months ago. Jap didn’t know
'he whereabouts of the temple, but observed curtly,
“ The island was thrown up by a volcano just before we
found it.” “With 10ft. of guano-ready-made on top of
it,” replied Woo, politely. “Anyhow, we’re here now,”
quoth Jap. “You won’t be here much longer,” con-
cluded Woo. Then Woo took photographs of the
depots, etc., as evidence of the Jap occupation, and
hied him home. Thereupon followed more repre-
sentations to the Jap Government, which at first
flatly; denied that any Japs were on the island.
When Woo knocked the bottom out of that yarn
with his testimony, backed by his photographs,
the Jap authorities admitted that they knew their
countrymen were there, but declared that they had re-
garded Pratas as a no-man’s land, and sought to end the
discussion by announcing that they wouldn’t insist upon
the occupation. But the Cantonese wanted to know
about the estimated 5,000.000 yen worth of guano, pearl,
and tortoise-shell collared by the Japs ; and at latest re-
ports they were hinting loudly that a, revival of the boy-
cott wouldn’t be a bad thing if the Jap Government
didn’t say something satisfactory about the missing
Wealth.

Dr. Sheldon’s
New Discovery
for Coughs and Colds.
POST-MORTEM examinations of healthy men who have met their death by accident
nearly always disclose tubercular scars in the lung tissue, showing that the subject had
at some time or other been attacked by the germ of Consumption, but had been strong
enough to resist its inroads.
These healed-up places in the lungs afford indubitable evidence of the curability of Lung
Troubles in their earlier stages. At any time the Consumption Germ may lodge in the lungs
of the most healthy of us and commence to multiply ; but if our constitutions are sound and
unweakened by a cold or other chest trouble these germs are speedily killed by the blood
corpuscles, whose task that is, leaving behind nothing but a scar to show where their deadly
work was nipped in the bud.
Hence the necessity of dealing with a cold, sore throat, or cough, directly the first
symptom is felt.
Consumption only too frequently arises from a neglected cold, which depresses the system
and presents a favorable opportunity for the Consumption Germ to fasten on our lungs and
multiply.

A WONDERFUL CURE .
Suffered Three or Four Years, Coughing- Day and Night.
Breathing So Bad, Could Hardly Speak.
A Remarkably Quick Recovery.
THE SHELDON DRUG CO., LTD., SYDNEY.
Dear Sirs,—
“ For three or four years I was very ill. During- this time, not for
a single hour did I feel myself. My throat was always itching and
tickling. I was coughing all day and all night. It was a dry, hack-
ing cough, and I could cough up nothing. My breathing was strained
and heavy, and people said I was going into Consumption fast. I
looked hollow-cheeked and haggard, and lost considerable weight.
All day long while at work I felt weary and depressed, and when I
came home of an evening I would have to go straight to bed. My
breathing was often so bad I could hardly speak. Sometimes I was
laid up for days at a time. Chemists’ and doctors’ medicines did me
no good, and I took them by the gallon. As the days went on I grew
worse and worse. The coughing shook me to pieces, and gave me bad
headaches. One day when I was lying in bed, feeling- very bad, I read
about Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery. I sent for a bottle, and obtained
an almost immediate benefit. It loosened the oough so that I could
get rid of a lot of phlegm. The itching in the throat got less and less.
I took five bottles in all during a period of about three weeks, and by
that time was absolutely cured of every sign or symptom of my cough,
and began to put on weight rapidly. I have never been ill since.
Considering I was ill for four years, to get cured in three weeks seems
to me to be remarkably quick work.”
Y'ours faithfully, A. W. COLLING.
108 Regent Street, Camperdown, Sydney.

Grand Dairying Land.
Grand Dairying Country, indeed, is MOUNT
LARCOMBE ESTATE, which we are now
subdividing. To the dairyman, the most import-
ant question is one of water, and this is answered
most satisfactorily at Mount Larcomhe. The
average rainfall there is 43 inches, and every
block is permanently watered by running streams.
The flats are very fertile, splendidly adapted for
maize, potatoes, and lucerne. The hills are
warm and sheltered, and the whole Estate pro-
duces those rich luscious grasses that mean a
big butter yield.
MOUNT LARCOMBE
is divided into areas to suit purchasers, whose
fares we pay from any part of the States.

Ishmael.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SIX off, and still unbroken—
No fretting bridle bar.
No spur has left its token,
No girth has left its scar.
Free as the wind that follows
Its own unfettered will;
Free as the wheeling swallows,
He rests or revels still.
The Bush is stall and manger,
His grooms are wind and sun;
Dusk chieftain, pallid stranger,
He payeth toll to none !
The Bush is st- ’l and manger,
He snorts and stands aloof,
And flouts the face of danger
With rout of ringing hoof.
Heads low his comrades waddle
In weary ways of graft;
Galled withers ’neath the saddle,
Galled ribs against the shaft.
Where jocund kookaburras
Make merry on the bough,
Above the long brown furrows,
His playmates pull the plough.
On dusty road and dreary,
Or broken bridle track,
They wander far and weary,
And weary wander back.
No one demands his leisure.
From burden or from chain
He waits no master’s pleasure
To take the track again.
And only he runs idle
In pasture lands of Fate,
Unvexed of bit or bridle,
Or reekless rider’s weight.
Runs idle—who shall blame him ?
He flings the gauntlet down,
The man that dares to tame him
Shall take him back to town !
The sprites of earth and ocean,
The war of wind and flood,
Old Freedom’s rebel potion
Run riot in his blood !
Bold outlaw, reckless liver,
But by that dainty tread
And that red nostril’s quiver
Ye know him thoroughbred !
Six off, and still unbroken,
No saddle-mark shall stain
That satin coat for token
Of mastery and domain.
Dream no.t, your bondage leaden
That flashing eye shall blur,
Nor dream those flanks shall redden
And reek beneath the spur !
A monarch of the ranges,
A sultan of the plains,
A spirit that estranges
Fell Fear from his domains !
Wild as the wind of Heaven,
Fierce as the leaping flame,
He scorns your coward leaven —
And who shall gire him blame ?
VICTORIA.
MARIE E. J. PITT.
The Tool and His Money.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHEN Dan Brophy’s uncle died,
leaving a fortune of £5O to his
“beloved nephew,” Dan’s true friends
were appalled at the change it made in him. His
wife, who had expected at least the price of a
“daeint silk dhress ” from the lord of her pulse,
expected in vain, and Cassidy and Mabony, who
worked beside him in the drains of the “ Car-r-
-poration,” counting confidently on an era of
beer, were shocked to find that not one shout
came from the man they had trusted. From the
careless, genial, navvy soul, who, drawing each
week £2 Bs. from the “ Cir-rporation,” spent the
odd eight shillings on Saturday evenings at
Geoghegan’s Sewerman’s Arms, he became
cautious and sober. That was the worst sign—
SOBER!
“’Tis ahlwis th’ way—monney do be shpilin’
lots of dacent fellies,” said Geoghegan upon the
third Saturday evening, and Cassidy and Mahony
gloomily agreed. And on Monday they refused
to speak to Dan at his work, which made Dan
angry. So when Cassidy started telling Mahony
all about the incredible habits of misers, he
was rolled over in the good old way by Dan,
and discovered that wealth had not impaired
Dans physical powers, whatever' evil it had
wreaked upon his soul.
Dan had no knowledge of high finance. He did
not know the difference between a P.N. and a
current account. He had a vague idea that
“ Dr.” on the top of a ledger meant “ Docthor ”
and that “ Cr.” stood for “ Councillor.” He was,
you will please observe, a very ignorant fellow,
lacking enlightenment upon the subject of the
gods of Israel. Absolutely the only documents
which he understood were pawn-tickets. So,
when the cheque came, endorsed by Messrs. Pur-
chase and Costs, he turned it over and over, and
scratched his head, and, having lostall his friends,
took it to his wife. She showed herself full of
scorn at his simplicity.
“Ban, me mahn,” she said, “ye’ve bin done.
.They want ye to take thot little bit iv pa-aper
instid iv y’ure fifty pound. Oi’ve heard iv thim
thrieks befure.”
“But,” replied Dan, “here ut says: ‘Pay
Daniel Brophy the sum iv fifty pound ’ !”
.. , r °ight,” scoffed the wife of his bosom.
Take it to th’ ba-aker thin.”
bought was a good idea, and took it.
lhe baker was suspicious, and Dan, who had
heard a good deal about lawyers, and very little of
that to the credit of the profession, came back
nome, and, arraying himself in purple and fine
linen—a magenta tie represented the purple—
went into Melbourne to make inquiries. He
flamed into the office of Messrs. Purchase and
Costs like the archangel Gabriel, and threw the
cheque upon the table in front of a thin young
man in quiet, grey tweeds. The thin young man
looked up.
“ That’s a nice tie you’ve got on,” he said
gravely.
Dan paid no attention to the compliment. “Oi
want me money,” he declared.
“Ah ! I perceive you are an Irishman. Cead
viilhe failthe, colleen og,” replied the young man
suavely. “ Won’t you sit down ? ”
Dan sat down, cowed for a moment by the sheer
novelty of this strange young man. Then, a little
more civilly, “ Oi want me money. Oi s’pose
y’u’re th’ boss ? ”
“ Unfortunately, no, Mr. Purchase is at pre-
sent drawing and engrossing a brief for counsel in
a writ of Ji fa. Mr. Costs is up at the Divorce
Court directing a case for separation a mensa et
thoro. But I really must ask you where you got
that tie. It’s one of the brightest things I’ve
seen for years. We get so little of brightness, of
gaiety, in the grim precincts of the Law, Mr.”
—he looked at the cheque—“er—Brophy.”
“ Oi got ut fr’m a crimson Afghan,” said Dan,
nervously.
“It is very beautiful,” replied the other,
wistfully.
“Ut ought t’ be,” was Dan’s comment. “Oi
paid fourpince f’r ut.”
The clerk gazed dreamily at the tie. There was
quite a long silence. Dan shuffled his feet upon
the floor. Then his conqueror picked up the
cheque. “Pardon me, I quite forgot,” he said.
“ You wish to cash this ? Take it to the United
Federal Bank, just round the corner, in Collins-
street. I assure you it is quite in order.” Dan
rose. “Mind the step —good-day, Mr. Brophy.”
As the door closed the suave young man turned
wearily back to his “notice to produce.”
So Dan went to the United Federal Bank and
disorganised the business of that institution for
just over half an hour. By accident, or instinct,
be found his way to the paying teller. The pav-
ing teller was a long, slim youth with a languid
air of complete detachment from the institution
by which he was paid. His chief aim in attend-
ing the bank seemed to be the decoration of the
premises with his beautiful clothes and nicely
brushed hair. He wore a prodigious collar and a
very small pale lavender tie, which he was care-
fully adjusting before a little mirror when the
furious Dan blew in upon his peace. Dan leaned
over the counter, holding out his cheque. The
teller went on fixing his tie. Dan continued to
hold out the cheque. The teller continued to fix
his tie. Dan began to get annoyed. Just as he
was about to explode, the teller took the cheque,
and, giving his tie a final pat, tapped the docu-
ment thoughtfully with his manicured forefinger.
He handed it back to Dan. “Can’t pay on
that,” he said shortly, turning to the mirror
again.
Dan started to climb the counter. “ Oi t’ought
there was somet’ing cronk about ut,” he bellowed.
Alarm seized the Adonis as Dan’s big, red hand
reached out for him. “Thqre’s nothing cronk,”
he replied quickly. “Only—you want to take it
to the ledger-keeper over there.”
Dan looked over to the row of grinning ledger-
keepers. “Which wan?” he growled, sus-
piciously.
The teller told him the one at the end.
“Ah!” said Dan. “ Th’ wan wid the boss-
eye?” And the smile died from the face of one
of the row quite suddenly. He took the cheque
across—it was sulkily snatched from his hand and
endorsed. Then he took it back to the teller.
“How’ll you have it?” inquired the latter.
“In me hand,” said Dan, firmly.
“ I mean, gold or notes ? ”
Dan, with fortune so close to him, became a
little excited. He retained sufficient presence of
mind, however, to say that he would have gold.
He got it, and dropped two of the sovereigns on
the floor. The bank porter stooped to pick them
up for him, but Dan’s foot descended on his hand
just as it came down on oneof the coins. “Hands
off!” said Dan, stooping and collecting his own,
while the porter examined his fingers and swore.
Then Dan counted all the money carefully at one of
the tables, while all the bank officials neglected the
other customers to watch him.
The ceremony of counting complete, Dan took
the money back to the lavender tie. “Yous
moind monney f’r blokes ? ” he inquired. “ Loike
a sa-avin’s bank ? ”
The teller admitted that this was some part of
the purpose of the bank.
“Thin moind this f’r me,” commanded Dan,
spreading it out on the counter. “Y’see”—he
leaned confidentially across the counter, and the
teller shuddered at the smell of onions, “Av Oi
lave ut at home me ould woman’ll be bound t’
foind ut.”
“Take it to the next counter,” said the teller
faintly.
So Dan took it to the next counter, and was
sent on to the “New Accounts” department,
and, with his tongue hanging dangerously out,
scrawled his name in a large book, and became a
depositor with a current account. After which
he strode out of the bank with the air of a Pier-
Eont Morgan, and went home to show his pass-
ook to his wife. She was not impressed.
“ Take care iv ut ! ” she sniffed. “ Wance Oi’d
had it in me hand loike thot, ’tis Oi wud be
ta-akin’ care iv ut mesilf.”
“F’r Hiven’s sake, hould yer jaw ! ” answered
Dan. “ The yoong fellies at the bank tould me
Oi c’d have ut anny toime Oi wanted.”
“Av Oi was you, thin, Danny, me mahn, ’tis
mesilf ud be havin’ ut to-morry.”
Dan laughed, but the seeds of mistrust had been
sown. And so, that night, he lay awake and
thought of his fifty pounds aione and unprotected
in the hands of strangers. What a fool he had
been not to keep it when he had it. He
determined that he would look in at the bank
next day and see that they had not dipped into
his store. He looked in. The paying teller was
still adjusting his tie—a pearl-grey one to-day.
He explained to Dan that he would have to buy
a cheque-book. Dan, after demur, bought one.
Then, after having the method of drawing a
cheque carefully unfolded to him, he sat down,
and ten minutes later brought this to the counter :
The teller took it and began to laugh. Then he
showed it to the receiving teller, who also
laughed. The rest of the officials came and looked
at the document —more laughter. And when the
cheque had been taken in to the General Manager
(who had it framed as a curiosity), Dan had

Suffered Agony for Five Long Years—
Life was a Perfect Misery.
If you have indigestion, you may recognise it by these symptoms-feverish headache
and coated tongue on rising in the morning; heavy, dull, and listless feeling all day ;
sleepless nights ; feeling of fulness after eating ;
severe pain in chest and. hack after meals; pain
and distension of abdomen. There are innumer-
able other symptoms of indigestion, for digestion
begins the moment the food enters the mouth,
and continues until it is turned into tissue-build-
ing blood by the stomach and bowels. For all
forms of indigestion Bile Beans are a sovereign
remedy.
Mr. li. J. Tucker, a hairdresser, residing at
Franklin Street, Adelaide, says:—“For the past
five years I have been troubled with Indigestion
and Dyspepsia. I suffered awfully at times, but
could get nothing to give me relief. The Indiges-
tion made my life a perfect misery. I could not
attend to my work. I lost all appetite, and suffered
from vomiting and chronic headaches, and all day
long hot, sour water kept rising into my mouth.
Doctors’ medicines did me no good. One day I received
one of your pamphlets bearing on Bile Beans, and
reading of cases similar to my own that had been cured
by Bile Beans, I decided to give them a trial. It now
gives me great pleasure to state that I am completely
cured of Indigestion, the credit of which is solely
due to Bile Beans.”

another filled up for him. He signed it, and
received his money. As the teller handed it over,
he asked Dan whether he wished to olose his
account. Dan counted the cash twice and handed
it hack. It was all there. His confidence in the
bank was re-established.
During the first week Dan came three times for
a careful counting of his fortune. During the
second he came twice; during the third, once;
and, for a long while, once a week was his tally.
His absolute trust in the bank at length made him
careless, and lie missed five weeks. In the
interval five shillings disappeared.,
He was not slow to notice the odd silver amongst
the gold, and. when he had verified theloss, his
remarks shook the plaster down from the gilded
capitals of the banking chamber. They had begun
to rob him—his wife had told him all along that
they would. Where, lie asked, with vulgar em-
phasis, was the missing “dollar?” He didn’t
want any of their jaw ; all he well desired
was his money. Would the bank give
it to him? In vain the teller sought to inform
him of the usual bank charge upon current
accounts. This open confession of the,robbery
maddened Dan. He put the remains of his for-
tune in his trousers pocket, and, calling upon
Heaven to abolish the scarlet bank, started his old
trick of climbing the counter. But the forces of
the bank had gathered. The porter, three clerks,
and a casual customer threw themselves upon
him, and, after a short but animated struggle,
fired him into the street with a threat of instant
arrest if he crossed the threshold again. But he
was satisfied—he had got his five-shillings’ worth
out of the porter’s uniform, and one of the bank
clerks sported a black eye for ten days. The feel-
ing of satisfaction was almost as keen as though
he had toyed with a policeman, and there was
not the aftermath of a belting in the cells, nor a
fine of two pounds and damages. Taking it all
round, it had been well worth,the money.
Wherefore Dan walked happily down the street
with his hand in his pocket amongst the gold.
For awhile he did not notice the Polite Stranger.
The Polite Stranger had been a witness of the
scene in the bank, and shepherded Dan down the
street while he formed his plan of attack. He
passed a friend, an irreproachable person in a
frock coat and a silk hat. “ Take off that
hat,” he said, rudely. “ This cove’ll shy at style.
Honest lago’s the dart.. I’m going to steer him
into Antonio’s. Fiji’s good enough for him.
Get!” The silk hat got.
Then the Polite Stranger stepped alongside,
holding out his hand, and beaming. Dan took
the hand because he couldn’t think of anything
better to do. “ Good-day, Mr. Brophy,” said the
Polite Stranger. And forthwith Dan had it all—
how the bank had. after all, decided to be honest,
and had sent the Polite Stranger to make restitu-
tion. Here Dan received five shillings. After
that it was all over.. Who could doubt a man
who gave one five shillings ? So the Fijian Uncle
worked like a charm, and it was not until Dan
had waited four hours in Antonio’s that the awful
truth dawned upon him. When it did, he threw
a bottle through a mirror, and ran yelling out
into the streets. Here he was collared by “Mad
Mick,” who, with the aid of a cruising plain-
clothes man, “shot him in.”
And lo ! next Saturday Dan’s two friends,
Cassidy and Malionv, fell upon his neck as he
came into the dusky haven of Geoghegan’s bar.
Dan’s loss in money was their gain in fellowship.
But the greatest gain was to Geoghegan. One
cannot have too many fools working for him.
HASSAN.
The Breaking of the Drought.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
rnms is one of the things to remember
all one’s life—one of the small things, like
surveyors’ pegs, scattered here and there
in the years. This is the breaking of a drought,
not many years ago. For months the land was a
desolation of heat, dust, twanging wire fences,
and awful silences, embroidered with the gruesome
notes of the crow. For months the promise of
rain had come, and passed on. Over and over
again the sky had become pregnant, but had borne
no fruit ; and our hearts were weary as the earth.
I remember a Wednesday morn that burst
in intense heat from the hills, when nothing
moved on the face of the earth but dust gliding
to and fro—the wanton dancing girl of the drought.
That awful noon little ruffled bodies fell from
the pepper-trees to the path, slain by the heat,
and an old cattle-dog crept in on to the dining-
room oilcloth and died. It was too piteous for
tears, with the dying all around. As noon crept
past a terrible feeling oppressed us that we had
come to the end of endurance, while, outside, the
plains still rocked in the sun, in a terrible mono-
tony, save for a peculiar dimness on the horizon,
as of a blurring of the sight.
At four o’clock I crept from under the bed
(always the coolest place), and noticed that a
shadow seemed to have fallen over all. The one
hopeful being in a house of unbelievers, I made
for the verandah. Truly a shadow, or more as if
the shadow of a shadow, lay on the earth, and a
terrible stillness. And in the east, far away,
stretched an enormous cloud, as if millions and
millions of black feathers had been blown into the
sky. It gave the semblance of gigantic wings,
and as it grew, covering the heavens, it seemed as
if also a huge foot was speeding across the hills.
I caught sight of the Celestial cook sitting stolidly
beside the tank, but he only shrugged indifferent
shoulders in face of that fearsome sky. It may
have been seconds, it may have been hours, before
those terrible wings had gathered us up beneath
them. There was an un-nerving tension, as if
Nature were expectant of some coming horror.
Nothing stirred ! Looking to the hills, I saw the
gums leaning breathlessly against the sky.
' Suddenlv it came. The tension broke as a banjo
string breaks, and a great wind belched from the
heavens. As a knife slices a carrot, the stricken
gums went down before it. The dead creepers
were torn from the rocks, and stretched out like
tattered flags on the rushing air. It leapt upon
the plain, licking up the dust like a huge wave
before it, and even as I fled it was upon us. It
was quite dark and the house pitched and groaned
like a laboring ship. Noise ! If you have never
heard the maniacal scream of wind, you have no
idea of what noise is; and this wind rolled all
others before it. The roar of the blast shrieking
down the valleys, the crashing of trees, and the
thin squeal of sand on the roof made us think
those great wings were beating us to death in
some gigantic struggle.
It could only have been moments counted on
one hand, when suddenly, as the blast com-
menced, it ceased, and the silence that followed
hurt physically. Then into the silence fell heavy
drops as of blood. One by one, heavier, quicker,
till with a rush the rain was upon us ' -t renzieci. y
a year-old puppy sought refuge in my arm, wm e
out in the paddocks we faintly heard the choke
cry of the raptured cattle. I cried that nignt or
the poor old dog that died before the rain.
CARO.
The Cat.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
YES,” said the woman with the
sombre eyes, “ I hate cats ! You
know (or perhaps you don't know) I
was going to marry a man once (What! Oh,
don’t be funny !) —and I didn’t.”
“Your husband?” I questioned. Where
does lie come in ? ”
She tore another strip off the palm-leaf fan
before she answered. (The click of the balls came
from the billiard-room, and the sound of a coarse
laugh floated up from the stahles.)
“Kenneth? I married Kenneth, there s a
difference between the man you marry and the
man you don't. The other man was what I had
always longed for,” she went on dreamily, and
for a time I—l—thought Heaven must have been
over-rated.
“Some people asked me once what being in
love was like. I told them I loved the night, be-
cause it brought him. And I loved the morning,
because it brought another night. I suppose they
thought I was mad. It was a sweet madness.
(She went.on tearing the fan in silence.)
“But about cats?” I reminded her. You
said you hated cats ! But why?”
She straightened her hack in a way I remem-
bortid •
“This is why,” she said, throwing away the
palm leaf as she spoke, with a sudden passionate
gesture. .
“There was a woman lived opposite to us who
seemed to have everything in the world but a
human interest. But —she had a cat. She
seemed to run after that cat. All day and half
the night—and the tragedy of it was that the brute
did not want her. I used to pity her, I who
thought 1 had so much !
“ Then he went away. ... I was sitting out
on the verandah one night, and Kenneth came.
I was thinking, and it bothered me to have him
there, so I sent him out on to the lawn to smoke.
“ After a time a special messenger came with a
letter. I tore upstairs to my room. ... You
know”—her voice trailed off drearily—“l said
I had thought I was so happy that Heaven . . .
Well, after I had read that letter, I thought the
other place could not he worse than reading a
letter like that. I looked in my glass when I
could —it seemed ages; but I suppose it was only
half an hour or so, and I knew that a good many
things were over for me—for ever. I wanted to
see what a woman looked like when she had lost
— everything!
“I was so cold, so awfully cold (it was a hot
night, too). I went down to the garden ; it was
very dark under the trees. In the house opposite
the lonely old woman was wandering round with
a candle and a saucer, calling the ciit. The light
from the candle fell on her long thin throat and
anxious face. It suddenly came to me : ‘Perhaps
the time will come when I, too, will have only—
only a cat to care for ! ’
“And then I ran— to Kenneth—under the
trees. ‘Where are you?’ I called. ‘Are you
still waiting ? ’ It was so dark, I fell into his
arms. ' . ,
“‘Yes,’ he said, holding me tightly— waiting
for you ! - God, am I to wait for ever ? ’
“‘No,’ I almost shrieked. ‘No!’...and
then—he kissed me .. .
* * * *
“ And over the road the woman was calling,
calling the cat.”
BOH.
The Jewels.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
JEWELS a-glitter in radiance rare ;
Costly and fit for a queen to wear ;
Jewels set in a fair maid’s hair.
Bedded in blindness the earth below,
Cruelly torn from her peaceful breast,
For this is the covetous orb of woe
Where the glutted queen it among the rest.
And we are as lovely as sun-kissed snow.
Ours are the tints of the sedge-fringed meres
Aglow in the glory of evening skies,
Or of sunshine mirrored within the tears
That well to the fount of a fair maid’s eyes ;
That sweet with the bitter that scalds and sears.
We are as pure as a crystal spring ;
The palm which held us was foul and red
With the life of that blenched and tumbled thing
So sorely smitten ; so strangely dead ;
With a finger severed because of the ring.
We have led pride to the feet of shame ;
We have found virtue, and left it vice ;
We have been by when Frailty came
And bartered her body to Avarice.
Lo ! We are pure and are not to blame.
Yes, we are pure without flaw or speck,
Pure above purity, e’en because
The pure have exchanged at our royal beck
The woman that is for the woman that was ;
So we might gleam on her bare white neck.
For us the galleon tall aflame
With her bloodstained decks and her rifled hold,
The men’s black torture, the women’s shame,
And tales that the waves to the islands told
Of deeds encrimsoned, without a name.
The wild-beast orgies, the reek, the din,
The women’s sobs that the drink-songs drown,
The plank, and the flash of a cruel fin,
And the smothered shriek as a man goes down.
And we were the price that bought the sin.
Jewels a-glitter in radiance rare ;
Costly and mete for a queen to wear;
Set in the coils of a fair maid’s hair.
MAORILAND.
FRANK HUDSON.
